The Method of Reasoning to Exit Taqlid: Contemplating the Self
Sanusi presents the practical reasoning from self-contemplation to a necessary Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first step of the rational method to exit taqlid by contemplating your own existence and recognizing the necessity of a Creator.

Core Idea
Your own existence is the closest proof for a Creator.

After establishing the importance of leaving blind imitation, Sanusi now gives a practical method. He directs the student to the most immediate evidence: oneself. This lesson explains how reflecting on your own coming-into-existence leads to the rational necessity of a Creator, through the impossibility of self-creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the Quranic verse and the logical steps.
The text is incomplete; focus on the presented reasoning.
2
Identify the key steps
The argument has four clear moves: (1) look at yourself, (2) know necessarily that you were not then you were, (3) infer a creator, (4) argue that self-creation is impossible.
Each step builds on the previous.
3
Understand 'easier for you'
Sanusi says if you could create yourself, you could also create another being easier than yourself. This highlights the absurdity: self-creation involves a contradiction.
The contradiction is preceding and being after yourself.
4
Note the incomplete argument
The text cuts off but the core idea is clear. The full proof continues later; for now, grasp the foundational reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كيفية النظرِ المُخرج مِنَ التقليد وإذا عرفت هذا - أيُّها المقلدُ الناظر لنفسه الرحمةِ - فأقربُ شيءٍ يُخرِجُكَ عَنِ التقليد بعونِ اللهِ : أَنْ تنظر إلى أقرب الأشياء إليكَ ؛ وذلك نفسُكَ ، قالَ الله تعالى : ﴿ وَفِي أَنفُسِكُمْ أَفَلَا تُبْصِرُونَ ﴾ [الذاريات : ۲۱] ، فتعلم على الضرورةِ أنَّكَ لم تكن ثم كنتَ ، فتعلم أنَّ لكَ مُوجِداً أو جدَكَ ؛ لاستحالةِ أنْ تُوجِدَ نفسَكَ ، وإلا لأمكنَ أَنْ تُوجِدَ هو أهون عليكَ مِنْ نفسِكَ ، وهو ذاتُ غيرِكَ ؛ لمساواتِهِ لكَ في الإمكان ، وإنَّما قلنا : ( أهون عليكَ ) لِمَا في إيجادِكَ نفسَكَ مِنْ زيادةِ التهافتِ والجمع بين متنافيين ؛ تقدُّمُكَ على نفسِكَ وتأخُرُكَ عنها ؛ لوجوبِ سَبْقِ
[The Mode of Reasoning that Removes from Taqlid] And if you have understood this, O you who practice taqlid and look for yourself out of mercy - then the closest thing that removes you from taqlid, with the help of Allah, is that you look at the closest of things to you; and that is your own self. Allah Most High said: {And in yourselves, do you not then see?} [Quran 51:21]. So you know necessarily that you were not then you were; so you know that you have a creator who created you; because it is impossible that you created yourself; otherwise it would be possible that you create what is easier for you than yourself, namely the essence of another; because of its equality with you in possibility. And we only said 'easier for you' because in your creating yourself there is increased inconsistency and the combination of two opposites; your preceding yourself and your being after yourself; because of the necessity of precedence [cut off]
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents the practical method (kayfiyya al-nazar) that removes one from taqlid. Sanusi addresses the muqallid (one who practices blind imitation) and says: after understanding the previous warnings, the closest way to exit taqlid is to contemplate the closest thing to you — your own self. He cites the Quranic verse 'And in yourselves, do you not then see?' (51:21). The reasoning proceeds: you know with necessity (darura) that you once were not and then you came to be; therefore, you must have a creator (mujid) who brought you into existence. It is impossible (istihala) that you created yourself, because if that were possible, you would also be able to create something easier than yourself — another being like yourself — since that being would be equal to you in possibility (imkan). The argument then begins to explain why self-creation is impossible: it would involve combining two opposites (tahafut and jama' bayn mutanafiyayn) — your preceding yourself and your being after yourself. The text breaks off at 'because of the necessity of precedence...'. The proof is thus incomplete, but the core idea is the contingency argument from the fact of one's own existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first step in the reasoning that removes one from taqlid?
A) Reading the Quran
B) Looking at one's own self and realizing one was not then was
C) Following a scholar
D) Performing acts of worship

Key Takeaway
The closest rational proof for the existence of a Creator is the contemplation of your own existence and the impossibility of your self-creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that your own existence proves a Creator. Have you ever truly looked at yourself—your heartbeat, your thoughts, your very being—and felt the weight of that proof? Or do you take your existence for granted? Spend one minute in silence right now, contemplating that you were not then you were. Let that realization settle into your soul. What do you owe the One who brought you from non-existence?
?
The argument says self-creation leads to a contradiction. But consider: do you live as though you created yourself? In what areas of your life do you act as if you are the ultimate decider, as if your existence is self-sufficient? Repent from that arrogance and acknowledge your utter dependence on your Creator every moment.
?
If the closest thing to you is your own self, and that points to the Creator, then what is your excuse for not knowing Him? Have you been avoiding this obvious proof? From today, commit to making contemplation of your own existence a daily habit—even for one minute—so that you never forget your Creator.

Reflecting on the Self to Know the Creator
Sanusi presents the method of reflection starting with oneself, proving a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reflecting on your own existence leads to the necessary conclusion that you have a Creator distinct from yourself.

Core Idea
Self-reflection is the nearest path from imitation to certainty.

Sanusi invites the seeker to begin the journey of reflection with the closest and most accessible object: oneself. This passage outlines a simple yet profound argument: since you were not and then were, you must have a bringer-into-existence. The argument then eliminates the possibility of self-creation, establishing that the Creator is other than yourself. This foundational reasoning delivers one from blind imitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the direct address to the 'imitator' (المقلد) and the Quranic verse.
The tone is personal and encouraging – 'for mercy'.
2
Identify the two key premises
The argument has two main parts: (1) you were not then were, so you have a creator; (2) you could not have created yourself. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the contradiction argument
Sanusi explains why self-creation is impossible: it would require you to precede yourself and follow yourself. Work through this logical contradiction carefully.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument shows that a creator (موجد) distinct from the self must exist. Consider why this step is sufficient to escape imitation.
The proof does not need physics – it starts with your own existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كيفية النظر المخرج من التقليد وإذا عرفت هذا - أيها المقلد الناظر لنفسه الرحمة - فأقرب شيء يخرجك عن التقليد بعون الله : أن تنظر إلى أقرب الأشياء إليك ؛ وذلك نفسك ، قال الله تعالى : ﴿ وفي أنفسكم أفلا تبصرون ﴾ [الذاريات : ٢١] ، فتعلم على الضرورة أنك لم تكن ثم كنت ، فتعلم أن لك موجداً أوجدك ؛ لاستحالة أن توجد نفسك ، وإلا لأمكن أن توجد هو أهون عليك من نفسك ، وهو ذات غيرك ؛ لمساواته لك في الإمكان ، وإنما قلنا : ( أهون عليك ) لما في إيجادك نفسك من زيادة التهافت والجمع بين متنافيين وهو (٢) ; تقدمك على نفسك وتأخرك عنها ؛ لوجوب سبق الفاعل على فعله ، فإذا كانت ذاته نفس فعله (١) .. لزم المحذور المذكور .
The method of reflection that delivers from imitation. And if you know this – O imitator, looking for yourself for mercy – then the nearest thing that takes you out of imitation, with the help of God, is that you look at the nearest of things to you; that is your own self. God Almighty said: {And in yourselves, do you not see?} [Quran 51:21]. So you know necessarily that you were not, then you were. So you know that you have a bringer-into-existence (mūjid) who brought you into existence; because it is impossible that you brought yourself into existence – otherwise it would be possible that you bring into existence what is easier for you than yourself, which is the essence of another, because it is equal to you in contingency. And we only said 'easier for you' because of the increased inconsistency and combination of two contradictory things in your bringing yourself into existence. That is (2): your precedence over yourself and your being after yourself; because the agent must precede its action. So if the essence itself were its own action (1), then the mentioned problem would follow.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage begins with the heading 'The method of reflection that delivers from imitation' and then instructs the seeker to look at the nearest thing: himself. By reflecting on the fact that he was not then existed, he necessarily knows he has a creator (موجد). The argument then demonstrates the impossibility of self-creation: if one could create himself, he could also create what is easier, namely another being like himself. Moreover, self-creation involves a contradiction—the agent must precede his action, but if the agent is identical with the action, this leads to the absurdity of being prior to oneself and posterior to oneself simultaneously. Thus, a creator distinct from the self is proven.

Key Terms

موجد
إمكان
تهافت
تقدم
تأخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that proving a creator requires complex philosophical or scientific arguments.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the proof begins with simple self-reflection – the fact of your own existence is sufficient.

Checkpoint
What is the first step Sanusi recommends to escape from imitation (التقليد)?
A) Reading books of theology.
B) Looking at one's own self.
C) Following a scholar strictly.
D) Praying for guidance.

Key Takeaway
Reflecting on your own existence – from non-existence to existence – leads necessarily to the recognition of a Creator distinct from yourself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever truly paused to reflect on your own existence from non-existence, or do you still rely on what you were taught without personal certainty? What would change if you claimed that certainty for yourself today?
?
If this simple argument leads necessarily to a Creator distinct from you, why do you still live as if your life is your own? Name one decision today that you will consciously make in recognition that your existence is from Him.
?
Sanusi calls you 'O imitator looking for mercy'. Are you satisfied with second-hand faith? What will you sacrifice this week to move from imitation to direct, personal conviction about your Creator?

The Objection of Sperm and the Answer of Body Increase
Sanusi answers an objection using body increase as proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi responds to the objection that personal existence began as sperm by using the increase in body to prove creation from non-existence.

Core Idea
Your body's increase proves something came from nothing, needing a Creator.

In the previous lessons, Sanusi established that everything that begins to exist needs a cause. But an objector might say: I never experienced non-existence — I existed as sperm in my father's loins, and so on. Sanusi's answer is both simple and profound: the increase in your body from that original sperm was non-existent and then came to be, which suffices to prove a Creator without needing to prove your entire non-existence. Let's read the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims he only knows transformation from form to form, not creation from non-existence. He argues that he existed as sperm, so he never truly came from nothing.
Notice the objector's focus on his own personal history.
2
Identify the key observation
Sanusi points out that your current body is larger than the original sperm. This increase (ما زاد) is something new.
The increase is the 'zā'id' – the added matter.
3
Trace the logical inference
Since the increase was nonexistent and then existed, it must have a bringer-into-existence (موجد). This proves a Creator without needing to prove the non-existence of the original sperm.
This is the 'burhān qāṭi‘' – conclusive proof.
4
Reflect on the proof's strength
Sanusi says this suffices as proof for the Maker without other arguments. Reflect on why this is a powerful and direct argument.
It avoids the objection entirely by focusing on observable change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ بعض الحوادث لم يكن ثم كان ] فإن قلت : كيف أعلم ضرورة سبق عدمي وقد كنت ماء في صلب أبي ، وكذا أبي في صلب أبيه وهلم جراً ؟! غاية الأمر : أني أعلم ضرورة تحولي من صورة إلى صورة ، لا من عدم إلى وجود كما ذكرت . فالجواب : أن ذاتك الآن أكبر من النطفة التي عنها قطعاً ، فتعلم على الضرورة أن ما زاد كان معدوماً ثم كان ، وإذا كان معدوماً ثم وجد فلا بد له من موجد ، فقد تم لك البرهان القاطع بهذا الزائد من ذاتك على وجود الصانع دون حاجة إلى غيره .
[Some events were not then became.] For if you say: How do I know necessarily my prior non-existence, while I was water in the loins of my father, and likewise my father in the loins of his father, and so on? The extent of the matter is that I necessarily know my transformation from form to form, not from non-existence to existence as you mentioned. The answer: That your essence now is certainly larger than the sperm-drop from which it came. So you know necessarily that what increased was nonexistent then came to be. And if it was nonexistent then came into existence, it must have a bringer-into-existence. So the conclusive proof has been established for you by this increase from your essence for the existence of the Maker, without need for other than it.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objector argues that he does not directly know his own non-existence because he existed as sperm in his father's loins, and so on back; he only knows transformation, not creation from nothing. Sanusi answers by noting that the current body is larger than the original sperm. That increase was nonexistent and then came to exist, which proves a creator (موجِد) for that increase. This suffices as a conclusive proof (برهان قاطع) for the existence of the Maker, without needing to prove the absolute non-existence of the original atom.

Key Terms
نطفة
صورة
برهان قاطع
صانع
موجد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If I existed as sperm, I never came from non-existence, so I have no proof of a Creator.
Correction: Even if you existed as sperm, the increase in your body from that sperm was non-existent and then came to be, proving a Creator for that increase.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi answer the objection that one existed as sperm and thus never came from non-existence?
A) He argues that the sperm itself came from non-existence.
B) He points out that the increase in the body proves something came from non-existence.
C) He claims that transformation itself implies a creator.
D) He dismisses the objection as invalid because the sperm did not truly exist.

Key Takeaway
The increase of the human body from its original state as sperm proves that something came from non-existence, establishing the existence of a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do I look at my own body's growth and change, and see not just biological processes, but the direct act of a Creator bringing something from nothing? What would change in my gratitude if I started each day acknowledging that my very existence is a constant creation?
?
The objector said: 'I only see transformation, not creation from nothing.' Do I live as if my life is just a sequence of changes, or do I affirm that every moment is a new creation by Allah? What would it mean to live with that awareness?
?
If the increase in my body from a sperm to an adult convincingly proves the existence of a Maker, why do I still sometimes act as if I am self-sufficient? How can I make this proof a living reality that humbles me and increases my reliance on Him?

The Proof of Bodily Increase
Sanusi answers the objection that one only changes form, proving the need for a Creator from bodily increase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the bodily increase from sperm to adult as a decisive proof for the existence of a Creator, answering the objection that only form changes.

Core Idea
Your growth from a sperm proves creation ex nihilo, not just change of form.

Sanusi presents a powerful objection to the argument for a Creator based on personal existence. The objector claims he only knows transformation from one form to another, not creation from non-existence. In response, Sanusi points to the physical increase in the body as undeniable evidence that new matter came into existence, requiring a Creator. This lesson walks through the objection and its elegant refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the objection-and-answer structure.
The passage begins with the objector's claim and ends with Sanusi's decisive proof.
2
Identify the core of the objection
The objector argues that he only knows his own transformation from one form to another (from sperm to adult), not creation from nothing. He denies the need for a Creator based on personal existence.
This objection challenges the argument that one's own existence proves a Creator.
3
Understand the key point of the answer
Sanusi points out that your current body is larger than the original sperm. The increase was non-existent before, so it came into existence. This requires a Creator.
The increase is not just form change; it is new matter.
4
Reflect on the proof's conclusiveness
Sanusi calls this a 'decisive proof' (burhān qāṭiʿ) because it is straightforward and self-contained. No external evidence is needed.
Think about why this proof is stronger than just relying on one's own existence from nothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعضُ الحوادث لم يكن ثمَّ كَانَ
فإن قلت : كيف أعلمُ ضرورة سبق عدمي وقد كنتُ ماءً في صُلب أبي ، وكذا أبي في صلب أبيه وهلم جراً ؟! غاية الأمر : أنّي أعلمُ ضرورةً تحوُّلي مِنْ صورة إلى صورة ، لا من عدم إلى وجود كما ذكرت . فالجواب : أنَّ ذاتَكَ الآنَ أكبرُ مِنَ النطفة التي عنها قطعاً ، فتعلم على الضرورةِ أنَّ ما زادَ كان معدوماً ثم كانَ ، وإذا كان معدوماً ثم وُجِدَ فلا بدَّ لَهُ مِنْ مُوجِدٍ ، فقد تمَّ لكَ البرهانُ القاطعُ بهذا الزائدِ مِنْ ذاتِكَ على وجودِ الصانع دون حاجة إلى غيرِه .
Some events were not then came into existence. If you say: How do I know necessarily the priority of non-existence [for me] when I was once water in the loins of my father, and likewise my father in the loins of his father, and so on?! The utmost is that I know necessarily my transformation from one form to another, not from non-existence to existence as you mentioned. The answer: That your essence now is greater than the sperm-drop from which you were [formed] definitely, so you know necessarily that the increase was non-existent then came into existence. And if it was non-existent then came into existence, then it must have an originator (mūjid). So the decisive proof (burhān qāṭiʿ) is complete for you by this increase in your essence for the existence of the Maker (ṣāniʿ), without need for anything else.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer format. The objector argues that he only knows himself as undergoing transformation from one form to another (from sperm to adult) and therefore does not acknowledge a creation from non-existence. The answer points out that the adult body is larger than the original sperm; the added parts were not present, so they were nonexistent before. Since they came into existence, they must have a creator. This constitutes a decisive proof for the existence of God, and the proof is self-contained (no need for external evidence). The objection misunderstands the nature of change: the increase is not merely form, but new matter that was not there before.

Key Terms
ضرورة
سبق عدم
نطفة
برهان قاطع
صانع
موجد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection that one only changes form could be mistaken for a denial of creation from nothing.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the increase in substance proves creation ex nihilo, not just change of form.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what shows that the increase in a person's body was non-existent before?
A) The person remembers not having it.
B) The sperm did not contain that increase.
C) The body grows naturally over time.
D) The increase is the same substance as the sperm.

Key Takeaway
The increase in your body from sperm to adult shows that parts were created from nothing, proving the existence of a Creator without needing any external evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about your own body. Every cell that is now in your arms, legs, and organs—you know it was not there when you were a tiny drop. Do you truly believe that all of it came from nothing by the will of a Creator? If you are honest, does that realization make you feel owned and accountable? How would you live today if you genuinely felt every breath as a gift that was once nothing?
?
You have just read that your bodily increase is a ‘decisive proof’ for the existence of the Maker. Yet you have known this fact about growth since childhood. Why has it not transformed you? What is stopping you from letting this simple truth settle into your heart and change the way you wake up tomorrow morning?
?
The proof does not rely on distant galaxies or complex arguments—it is right there in your own body. If you ignore this evidence that your own existence points to a Creator, what are you really protecting yourself from? Is it fear of submission? Or perhaps pride in thinking you are self-made? Will you today acknowledge that every added inch of your frame was called into being by the One who needs no help?

The Proof from Your Own Body to the Createdness of the World
Sanusi refutes nature and cause, then proves the world's createdness through personal specification and the equality of similars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world must have a Creator who chooses and specifies, and why nature and cause cannot be substitutes for divine action.

Core Idea
If everything could be caused by nature, all would be alike—but you are not a sphere.

Sanusi turns your own body into a decisive argument. Looking at yourself—your specific shape, size, and qualities—you realize that these could have been different. Thus they required a choice. Neither nature, nor cause, nor chance can explain why you are this way. In this lesson, we follow Sanusi as he dismantles false explanations and builds the proof that the entire world is created.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three false positions
The passage begins by declaring the invalidity of three things: preponderance without a preponderator, nature (ṭabʿ), and cause (ʿilla). Read the opening line and ask why each of these is impossible in explaining the world's specifics.
Preponderance without preponderator means that one possibility wins out without anything tipping the scale—this is rationally impossible.
2
Examine the argument from your own body
Sanusi asks you to look at your own bodily increase—your size, shape, and qualities. Notice that it could have been otherwise; therefore it must have been chosen. Trace how he concludes that the sperm-drop cannot be the creator because it lacks choice, and that nature would produce uniformity.
If the sperm-drop acted by nature, all offspring would be identical—but they are not. And if it were a cause, it would act necessarily, not with specificity.
3
Generalize from yourself to the world
Sanusi says: 'that sperm-drop and the rest of the world are like you.' So the same reasoning applies. Everything is a contingent body that could have been different. Therefore, everything needs a specifier. This proves the world is created (not eternal).
4
Understand the principle of equality of similars
He uses a logical principle: two similars cannot differ in necessary attributes. If part of the world were eternal, it would be distinct from the rest by a necessary attribute (eternity), violating the principle. Hence the whole world must be originated.
This is a powerful argument against holding that some parts of the world are eternal.
5
Synthesize the proof
The passage ends by saying the decisive proof emerges. Review the chain: personal body → choice → specifier → similarity → necessity of creation. Understand that this is a comprehensive proof for the createdness of everything other than Allah.
This is a classic kalām argument known as the proof from specification (takhṣīṣ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطلانُ الرُّجحان بغير مرجّح ، وبطلان القول بالطبع والعلَّةِ ] ثم إذا نظرت إلى هذا الزائدِ مِنْ ذاتِكَ وجدتَهُ جِرماً يعمرُ فراغاً ، يجوزُ أنْ يكونَ على ما هو عليهِ مِنَ المقدار المخصوص والصفة المخصوصة ، وأن يكون على خلافهما ، فتعلم قطعاً أنَّ لصانعك اختياراً في تخصيص ذاتِكَ ببعض ما جازَ عليها ، فيخرجُ لكَ مِنْ هذا البرهان القاطع على أنَّ النطفة التي نشأت عنها قطعاً يستحيلُ أنْ تكونَ هي الموجدة لذاتِكَ ؛ لعدم إمكان الاختيار لها حتى تُخصِّص ذاتَكَ ببعض ما جازَ عليها ، وأيضاً لا طبعَ لها وجودِ ذاتِكَ ، وإلا لكنتَ على شكل الكرة ؛ لاستواء أجزاء النطفة ، ولا في نموّها ، وإلا لكنتَ تنمو أبداً . ومِنْ هنا أيضاً تعلمُ : أنَّ تلك النطفة وسائر العالم لم يكن ثم كانَ ؛ إذْ كلُّهُ مثلُكَ ؛ جِرْمٌ يعمرُ فراغاً ، يمكنُ وجوده وعدمُهُ ، واتصافه بما هو عليهِ مِنَ المقادير والصفات المخصوصة وبغيرها ، فيحتاج كما احتجت إلى مُخصِّصِ يُخصِّصُهُ بما هو عليه ؛ لوجوب استواء المثلين في كل ما يجب ويجوزُ ويستحيل ، وقد وجبَ لذاتِكَ سبقُ العدم، فكذلك يجب لسائر العالم المماثل لكَ ؛ إذ لو جاز أنْ يكونَ بعضُ العالم قديماً ، والقِدَمُ لا يكون إلا واجباً للقديمِ ؛ لِمَا يأتي . . لَلَزِمَ أنْ يختص أحدُ المِثْلين عن مِثْلِهِ .. بصفة واجبةٍ ، وهو محالٌ ؛ لِمَا يلزمُ مِن اجتماعِ متنافيينِ وهو أن يكونَ مِثْلاً غيرَ مِثْلِ ! افتقار الحادثِ إلى القديم ] فخرج لكَ بالنظرِ في ذاتِكَ ، وانعقاد التماثل بينك وبينَ سائر الممكنات .. البرهان القاطع على حدوثِ العالم كله ؛
The invalidity of preponderance without a preponderator, and the invalidity of the statement of nature (ṭabʿ) and cause (ʿilla). Then if you consider this increase from your essence, you find it a body occupying a void, it is possible that it be as it is in the particular measure and particular quality, and that it be contrary to them. So you know certainly that your Maker (ṣāniʿ) has choice (ikhtiyār) in specifying (takhṣīṣ) your essence with some of what is possible for it. So there emerges for you from this decisive proof that the sperm-drop from which you arose is necessarily impossible to be the one that brings you into existence (mūjida), because of the impossibility of choice for it so that it could specify your essence with some of what is possible for it. Also there is no nature (ṭabʿ) for it [to cause] the existence of your essence, otherwise you would be spherical in shape, due to the equality of the parts of the sperm-drop; nor in its growth, otherwise you would grow forever. And from here you also know: that that sperm-drop and the rest of the world (al-ʿālam) were not then came into existence; since all of it is like you: a body occupying a void, its existence and non-existence are possible, and its being described with the particular measures and qualities and with other than them, so it needs, just as you needed, a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) who specifies it as it is, due to the necessity of equality of similars (mithlayn) in everything that is necessary, possible, and impossible. And priority of non-existence (sabq al-ʿadam) has been established for your essence, so likewise it must be established for the entire world similar to you. For if it were possible that some of the world be eternal (qadīm), and eternity is only necessary for the Eternal, for what will come, then it would necessitate that one of the two similars be distinguished from its like by a necessary attribute, and that is impossible, because of what follows from the conjunction of two contradictories, namely that it be a like and not a like! The dependence of the contingent (ḥādith) on the Eternal (qadīm). So there emerges for you, by considering your essence and the establishment of similarity between you and all possible things (mumkināt), the decisive proof for the originatedness (ḥudūth) of the entire world.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a combined refutation and proof. It begins by stating the invalidity of preponderance without a preponderator (rujḥān bi-lā murajjiḥ) and of the opinions of nature (ṭabʿ) and cause (ʿilla). Then it returns to the example of the bodily increase: this increase is a contingent body (jirm) occupying space; it could have been different in its measure and qualities. Therefore, you know that your Maker has choice (ikhtiyār) in specifying it. This leads to the decisive proof that the sperm-drop cannot be the agent that brings you into existence, because it lacks choice and also because if nature were at work, the result would be uniform (e.g., spherical shape) or infinite growth. The argument then generalizes: the sperm-drop and the entire world are similar to you in being contingent bodies. Since your essence necessarily has non-existence prior to it, the same must hold for all things similar to you. If part of the world were eternal, that would require distinguishing one like from another like by a necessary attribute, which is a contradiction. Therefore, the entire world is originated (ḥādith), and the decisive proof for the createdness of the world is established through considering yourself. The argument uses the principles of 'equality of similars' and the necessity of a specifier for contingents.

مِثلين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the world or parts of it could be eternal (uncreated) while other parts are created, because they appear different.
Correction: Sanusi argues that all contingent beings are similar in being possible; if one is created, all must be, because two similars cannot differ in necessary attributes like createdness or eternality.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why can't the sperm-drop be the creator of the body?
A) It is made of water.
B) It lacks the ability to choose.
C) It is too small.
D) It is part of the body.

Key Takeaway
The world's specifics demand a Creator who chooses; nature and cause fail, and the equality of similars forces the entire world to be created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look at your own body—your height, your face, your hands. Do you truly believe that blind, impersonal nature could have chosen these exact details? How does this personal accountability to a Chooser change the way you see your own existence right now? What will you do today to acknowledge that you are not a random outcome of nature?
?
The proof shows that you are not the product of a sperm-drop alone—it lacks choice. Think about the moment you first became aware of your own existence. Has it ever truly sunk in that your existence is a direct gift from a Specifier who specifically wanted you to be as you are? Write down one way you have lived as if you were self-caused, and one step you will take tonight to live as a chosen creation.
?
If the entire world shares your contingency, then everything around you—every tree, every star, every breath you take—is being sustained by the same Chooser. When you look at your struggles and your desires, do you treat them as neutral facts of nature, or as moments that require you to turn to the One who specified them? What is one area of your life where you are acting as if you are governed by nature rather than by divine choice, and what will you change today?

The Sperm-Drop Cannot Create You
Sanusi argues that the sperm-drop cannot be the cause of your existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the sperm-drop from which you originated cannot be the cause of your existence, due to its lack of choice and fixed nature.

Core Idea
The sperm-drop lacks both choice and a fixed nature, so it cannot be your creator.

Sanusi begins a powerful argument from your own origin. He asks you to consider the sperm-drop from which you came. Could that drop have brought you into existence? He shows that it cannot, because it has no ability to choose your specific attributes, nor does it have a fixed nature that would determine them. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage presents two reasons why the sperm-drop cannot be the creator: (1) it lacks choice, (2) it lacks a fixed natural disposition. Find each one in the text.
The second argument is further divided into two consequences: if it had a fixed nature, you would be spherical or grow forever.
3
Understand the implications
Reflect on why Sanusi chooses these arguments. They show that the drop is not a conscious agent and not a determined cause, so it cannot be responsible for your existence.
This sets up the larger argument that all contingent things need an external specifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاطع على أنَّ النطفة التي نشأت عنها قطعاً يستحيلُ أنْ تكونَ هي الموجدة لذاتِكَ ؛ لعدم إمكان الاختيار لها حتى تُخصِّص ذاتَكَ ببعض ما جازَ عليها ، وأيضاً لا طبعَ لها [في؟] وجودِ ذاتِكَ ، وإلا لكنتَ على شكل الكرة ؛ لاستواء أجزاء النطفة ، ولا في نموّها ، وإلا لكنتَ تنمو أبداً . ومن هنا أيضاً تعلمُ : أنَّ تلك النطفة وسائر العالم لم يكن ثم كانَ ؛ إذْ كلُّهُ مثلُكَ ؛ جِرْمٌ يعمرُ فراغاً ، يمكنُ وجوده وعدمُهُ ، واتصافه بما هو عليهِ مِنَ المقادير والصفات المخصوصة وبغيرها ، فيحتاج كما احتجت إلى مُخصِّصِ يُخصِّصُهُ بما هو عليه ؛ لوجوب استواء المثلين في كل ما يجب ويجوزُ ويستحيل ، وقد وجبَ لذاتِكَ سبقُ العدم، فكذلك يجب لسائر العالم المماثل لكَ ؛ إذ لو جاز أنْ يكونَ بعضُ العالم قديماً ، والقِدَمُ لا يكون إلا واجباً للقديمِ ؛ لِمَا يأتي . . لَلَزِمَ أنْ يختص أحدُ المِثْلين عن مِثْلِهِ .. بصفة واجبةٍ ، وهو محالٌ ؛ لِمَا يلزمُ مِن اجتماعِ متنافيينِ وهو أن يكونَ مِثْلاً غيرَ مِثْلِ ! افتقار الحادثِ إلى القديم ؛ فخرج لكَ بالنظرِ في ذاتِكَ ، وانعقاد التماثل بينك وبينَ سائر الممكنات .. البرهان القاطع على حدوثِ العالم كله ؛ علوه وسفلِهِ ، عرشِهِ وكرسيّهِ ، أصلِهِ وفرعِهِ ، وأَنَّ جميعَهُ عاجز عن إيجاد نفسِهِ وعن إيجادِ غيره كعجزِكَ ، وأَنَّ الجميع مفتقر إلى فاعل مختار كافتقارِكَ ، وإِنْ مِنْ شيء إلا يسبح بحمده
The decisive proof that the sperm-drop from which you originated absolutely cannot be the one that brought you into existence; because it is impossible for it to have choice so as to specify you with some of what is possible for it, and also it has no natural disposition [in?] the existence of your self, otherwise you would be spherical due to the uniformity of the parts of the drop, nor in its growth, otherwise you would grow forever. And from this you also learn that that sperm-drop and the rest of the world were not then came to be; since all of it is like you: a body occupying space, its existence and non-existence possible, and its being characterized by the quantities and specific attributes and others, so it needs, as you needed, a Specifier who specifies it as it is; because of the necessity of equality of two similars in everything that is necessary, contingent, and impossible. And it has been necessary for your self that non-existence preceded, so likewise it is necessary for the rest of the world similar to you; for if it were possible for some of the world to be eternal, and eternity is only necessary for the eternal, as will come, then one of the two similars would be distinguished from its similar by a necessary attribute, which is impossible because of the resulting composition of two contradictories, that it be a similar that is not similar. The dependence of the contingent upon the eternal. So there emerges for you, by reflection upon your self and the establishment of similarity between you and all possible things, the decisive proof of the temporality of the entire world: its height and depth, its Throne and Kursi, its origin and branch, and that all of it is incapable of bringing itself into existence and of bringing anything else into existence, like your incapacity, and that all of it is in need of a free agent, like your need, and 'there is not a thing but glorifies His praise'.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents a proof of the world's contingency and its need for a Creator. The author begins by considering the human's origin from a sperm-drop. He argues that the drop cannot be the creator of the human because it lacks choice and natural determinism: if it had a fixed nature, the human would be spherical (since the drop's parts are uniform) or would grow indefinitely. Therefore, the drop is not the cause. Then he extends the argument: the drop and the entire world are similar to the human in being bodies that occupy space and could be otherwise. Each requires a Specifier (مخصص) to determine its existence and particular attributes. Since the human was preceded by non-existence, so must the world be. If any part of the world were eternal, it would be necessary—but then two similar things (the eternal and the contingent) would differ in a necessary attribute, which is contradictory. The passage concludes that the whole world is temporally originated and dependent on a free agent, citing the Quranic verse 'there is not a thing but glorifies His praise'.

Misconception: The sperm-drop has inherent power to cause existence.
Correction: Sanusi shows it lacks both choice and a fixed nature, so it cannot be the cause.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why can't the sperm-drop be the creator of the human?
A) It is too small
B) It lacks choice and a fixed natural disposition
C) It is impure
D) It only provides matter

Key Takeaway
The sperm-drop cannot be your creator because it has no choice and no fixed nature; therefore, the cause of your existence must be external.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You came from a drop that had no choice and no fixed nature. The One who chose your exact form and limited your growth is the only One worthy of your gratitude. Have you ever thanked Him for the specific traits He selected for you? Take a moment today to consciously thank Allah for at least three unique aspects of your creation.
?
If the sperm-drop cannot cause existence, then your very life is a direct gift from the Creator. What does that mean for how you live each day? Commit to one action today that acknowledges His exclusive role in sustaining you.
?
Your growth is limited—you will not grow forever. This points to a purposeful design. Does your life reflect that you are heading toward a meeting with the One who designed you? What is one thing you need to change to live with that awareness starting now?

From Your Contingency to the World's Contingency
Sanusi extends the argument from human contingency to the whole world, proving its temporality and need for a Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi extends the argument from your own contingency to prove that the entire world is contingent and dependent on a free Creator.

Core Idea
All things are like you: contingent, needy, and in need of a Specifier.

Having shown that the sperm-drop cannot explain your existence, Sanusi now broadens the argument. He points out that the same reasoning applies to the sperm-drop itself and to everything in the world. Because all things share the same characteristics—they are bodies, they could be otherwise, they have specific attributes—they all require a Specifier. This lesson traces that logical extension and its powerful conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Sanusi moves from the particular (you) to the universal (all things).
The phrase 'ومن هنا أيضاً تعلم' marks the transition.
2
Identify the key analogy
Sanusi says the sperm-drop and the entire world are like you: they are bodies occupying space, possible in existence, characterized by specific quantities and attributes. Find the analogy in the text.
The phrase 'كلُّهُ مثلُكَ' is central.
3
Understand the impossibility of partial eternity
Sanusi argues that if any part of the world were eternal, it would contradict the similarity principle. He uses a reductio ad absurdum. Work through that argument.
The impossibility is that two similars would differ in a necessary attribute.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
The conclusion is that the entire world is temporally originated and in need of a free agent. The lesson ends with a Quranic verse reinforcing praise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some parts of the world might be eternal (e.g., the heavens, the intellect).

What principle does Sanusi use to argue that if one part of the world were eternal, it would be impossible?
A) The principle of causality

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that your own existence was preceded by non-existence and that you are contingent in every moment, how does that change the way you rely on yourself versus your Creator? What would it mean to live with the constant awareness that you are a 'specified' being, dependent every breath?
?
The passage ends with a verse: 'there is not a thing but glorifies His praise.' If everything in the world is praising Allah, but you are not, what does that say about your state? Are you in harmony with the very nature of creation?
?
Sanusi says you need a Specifier for your specific attributes. Which of your attributes—character, abilities, circumstances—do you most often attribute to your own effort rather than to Allah? Can you honestly see them as pure gifts from the Specifier, and how would that change your gratitude?

The Proof from Change of Attributes
Sanusi proves the temporality of the world from the change of its attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the change of the world's attributes to prove that the world is temporal, and why this argument relies on the impossibility of change in the eternal.

Core Idea
Change proves temporality because the eternal never changes.

Sanusi presents a concise yet powerful argument for the temporality of the world. He first states a key principle about the eternal, then invites you to observe the changing attributes of the world. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the original Arabic and its literal English translation to grasp the argument in its own words.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'and also' (أيضاً) and 'because' (لما).
2
Identify the principle and the observation
The passage has two parts: first a principle about the eternal, then an observation about the change of the world's attributes. Notice how the second part builds on the first.
3
Understand why change implies temporality
Sanusi argues that change in attributes proves they are temporal because the eternal cannot change. This is a core axiom in kalam.
4
See why temporal attributes require a temporal subject
If attributes are temporal, then the subject (the world) must also be temporal, because a subject cannot exist without its attributes and cannot be eternal if it undergoes change.
5
Reflect on the overall proof
Consider how this argument fits into the broader kalam cosmological proof for the existence of God. The temporality of the world leads to the need for a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمُهُ . وأيضاً : لو نظرت إلى تغير صفات العالم قبولاً وحصولاً .. لدلَّكَ ذلكَ على حدوثها ؛ لِمَا يأتي من استحالة تغيرِ القديم ، ودلَّ حدوثها على حدوثِ موصوفها ؛ لاستحالة عروها عنها
What is established as eternal, its non-existence is impossible. And also: if you look at the change of the world's attributes in terms of reception and occurrence, that would indicate to you their temporality; because of what will come of the impossibility of change of the eternal, and their temporality indicates the temporality of their described subject; because of the impossibility of it being free from them.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage provides two interconnected points. First, a principle: 'What is established as eternal, its non-existence is impossible.' Then an argument: observe the change of the world's attributes—they are received and occur. This change proves that the attributes are temporal (haditha). The reason is that the eternal cannot change, as will be explained. Moreover, the temporality of the attributes implies the temporality of the subject (the world itself), because the subject cannot be free of temporal attributes; if it were eternal and attributes temporal, an eternal subject would be characterized by temporal accidents, which is impossible. Therefore, the world is temporal.

Correction: Sanusi asserts that the eternal cannot change at all; change is a sign of contingency and temporality.

Proving God's Pre-Eternity: The Argument from Circularity and Infinite Regress
Sanusi proves that God must be pre-eternal (qadim) by refuting circularity and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must be pre-eternal (qadim) and how the impossibility of infinite regress and circularity serves as the proof.

Core Idea
If God had a beginning, He would need a creator, leading to an impossible chain or circle.

Sanusi now tackles the divine attribute of pre-eternity (qidam). This is not just a statement of belief; he provides a logical argument that demonstrates its necessity. The argument works by eliminating the alternative: if God were not pre-eternal, absurd consequences would follow. In this passage, he shows that denying pre-eternity leads either to infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr), both of which are rationally impossible. Therefore, the only rational conclusion is that God is pre-eternal, without beginning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Sanusi asserts that the Maker must be pre-eternal (qadim), meaning not preceded by non-existence. Read the opening sentence carefully to grasp the definition.
The term 'qadim' in this context is a technical term meaning 'without beginning'.
2
Follow the conditional argument
If God were not pre-eternal (i.e., temporal), He would need a creator. This is because anything that begins to exist requires a cause. Trace this conditional in the text.
This step relies on the principle that every temporal being needs a creator.
3
Examine the two outcomes
The need for a creator leads to two possibilities: either the creator is not an effect of God (infinite regress) or it is an effect of God (circularity). Sanusi declares both impossible. Understand why each is absurd: infinite regress requires an actual infinite, circularity implies self-priority.
These are classic arguments in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفات التلوب فصل [ في إثباتِ صفة القدم له تعالى ، ونفي الزمانِ عنه ] ثم نقولُ : يجب أن يكون هذا الصانع لذاتِكَ ولسائرِ العالم قديماً؛ أي: غير مسبوق بعدم، وإلا لافتقر إلى محدثِ، وذلك يؤدّي إلى التسلسل إنْ كَانَ محدثه ليسَ أثراً له، أو إلى الدور إنْ كانَ، والتسلسل والدور محالانِ؛ لما في الأَوَّلِ مِنْ فراغ ما لا نهاية له بالعددِ، وفي الثاني مِنْ كون الشيء الواحدِ سابقاً على نفسه مسبوقاً بها.
Attributes of [al-thubut?]. Chapter: On proving the attribute of pre-eternity for Him, the Exalted, and negating time from Him. Then we say: It is necessary that this Maker, for your own self and for the rest of the world, be pre-eternal (qadim), i.e., not preceded by non-existence; otherwise He would need a creator. And that leads to infinite regress (tasalsul) if His creator is not an effect of Him, or to circularity (dawr) if it is. And infinite regress and circularity are impossible; because in the first is a void of infinite number, and in the second is that a single thing would be prior to itself and preceded by itself.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of God's attribute of pre-eternity (qidam). The section heading announces its subject. Sanusi states that the Maker (al-ṣāniʿ) must be qadim, meaning not preceded by non-existence. If He were temporal (muhdath), He would need a creator. That creator would either be an effect of God (if He is the first cause) or not. If the creator is not an effect of God, we get an infinite regress (tasalsul) of creators without first. If the creator is an effect of God, we get circularity (dawr) because then God would be both prior to and posterior to His creator. Both are impossible: infinite regress implies an actual infinite number of beings, which cannot be traversed; circularity implies a thing being prior to itself, a contradiction. Therefore, God must be pre-eternal.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
قديم (qadim)
حاجة إلى محدث (ḥāja ilā muḥdith)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
دور (dawr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God could be eternal in the sense of being uncreated but still have a beginning; for example, the universe might have a beginning but God could exist without a cause.
Correction: The argument shows that if God had a beginning (was temporal), He would need a creator, leading to an infinite regress or circularity. Since those are impossible, God cannot have a beginning.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what would happen if God were temporal (muhdath)?
A) He would be eternal.
B) He would need a creator, leading to either infinite regress or circularity.
C) He would be self-sufficient.
D) He would be subject to change.

Key Takeaway
God's pre-eternity is proven by the impossibility of infinite regress and circularity; if He had a beginning, an absurd chain or circle would follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you honestly examine your life, do you act as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that every moment of your existence depends on the Pre-Eternal Creator who has no beginning? What would it mean for you to truly live with the awareness that you are not self-originating but constantly sustained by a being who Himself depends on nothing?
?
The argument shows that if God had a beginning, an absurd chain would follow. But in your daily life, do you sometimes treat your own existence as if it were self-caused, relying on your own power without acknowledging that you are utterly contingent? How would your attitude towards prayer and reliance change if you fully internalized that you are not the first cause of anything?
?
The impossibility of circularity and infinite regress is not just a logical exercise—it forces you to face the fact that there is a single, necessary source of all reality. Do you live as if this truth makes a difference in how you handle your anxieties and ambitions? Can you name one specific area of your life where you still act as if the universe is unmoored, and what would it look like to anchor it in the Pre-Eternal?

Oneness of the Creator Is Necessary
Sanusi proves divine oneness via conditional argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for why the Creator must be one, based on the impossibility of two omnipotent beings coexisting.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot both be omnipotent; disagreement proves their inability.

Sanusi presents a concise but powerful argument for divine oneness. He states the thesis—the Creator must be one—and then provides the proof: if there were two, disagreement would lead to inability, which is impossible for God. This argument is a cornerstone of Islamic theology, establishing tawhid as a rational necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic carefully, noting key terms like الوحدانية and الصانع. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage has two parts: a statement (the Creator must be one) and a proof (if...then...). Identify the conditional and its conclusion.
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the argument's force
Consider why disagreement is inevitable between two distinct gods, and why inability follows. This shows the necessity of oneness.
This refutes any notion of multiple creators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الوحدانية
ثم نقول: يجب لهذا الصانع أن يكون واحداً; إذ لو كان معه ثانٍ لزم عجزهما أو عجز أحدهما عند الاختلاف
Chapter on Oneness. Then we say: The Creator must be one; for if there were a second with Him, the inability of both or one of them would follow upon disagreement.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a proof for the necessity of divine oneness (tawhid). Sanusi states that the Creator must be one, because if there were a second god, then when they disagree (about what to create, etc.), either both would be unable to realize their wills or one would be unable—both imply imperfection and inability, which is impossible for God. The argument is a classic kalam proof: two omnipotent beings cannot coexist without contradiction, so only one can exist. The text cuts off here, but the structure of the proof is clear.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think two gods could cooperate perfectly, avoiding conflict.
Correction: Sanusi shows disagreement is possible because distinct beings can have different wills, leading to inability if they conflict.

Checkpoint

A) They would work together perfectly.
B) They would both be unable or one unable upon disagreement.
C) One would overpower the other.
D) They would be the same essence.

Key Takeaway
The rational necessity of divine oneness is proven by the impossibility of two omnipotent beings coexisting without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This proof shows that two gods cannot both be all-powerful. In your own life, where have you tried to serve two masters—pleasing Allah and pleasing people, or seeking this world and the next without prioritizing? What contradiction has that caused, and what will you do about it today?
?
If you truly accept that the Creator is one, then your ultimate allegiance is to Him alone. Is there any area of your life where you are not submitting to His exclusive authority—a habit, a relationship, a fear, a hope? What step will you take right now to surrender that area to the One who alone is worthy?

Proof of Oneness from Impossibility of Infinite Numbers
Sanusi proves the oneness of the eternal being by the impossibility of an infinite number of co-eternals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of an infinite number of eternal beings proves that the necessary being must be one.

Core Idea
If there were multiple eternal beings, they'd be infinite; but infinite numbers are impossible.

Sanusi presents a subtle logical argument to prove that the necessary being is one. He begins by denying the possibility of an infinite number of existents entering existence. From this, he shows that assuming multiple eternal beings leads to a contradiction. This lesson walks through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice that the argument is concise; each clause is a step.
2
Identify the initial claim
Sanusi starts by stating that an infinite number of things entering existence is impossible. This is the foundation of the proof.
Why is it impossible? Because no specification among infinite numbers is possible.
3
Follow the conditional reasoning
If there were multiple eternal beings, they would be infinite in number. This leads to a contradiction because infinite numbers require a particularizer, which would make them temporal, but they are supposed to be eternal.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since the assumption of multiple eternal beings leads to a contradiction, the only coherent conclusion is that the eternal being must be one.
This does not prove that such a being exists, but given its existence, it must be one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دخول ما لا نهاية له عدداً في الوجود ، وهو محال ، وإلا لم يكن لبعض الأعدادِ ترجيح على بعض ، فتفتقر في تعيين بعضها إلى مُخصِّص ، وذلكَ يُوجِبُ حدوثها ، وقد تبيَّنَ وجوب قدمها ، هذا خلفٌ ، فتعيَّنَ إذا وجوبُ وَحدتِها
Entrance of what has no end in number into existence is impossible. Otherwise, there would be no priority of some numbers over others, so specifying some of them would require a particularizer, and that necessitates their temporality, while it has been established that they must be eternal. This is a contradiction. So it is determined that oneness is necessary.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the oneness of the necessary being (God). It begins by stating that an infinite number of existents entering into existence is impossible. The argument is: if there were multiple eternal beings, they would be infinite in number (since no reason to limit them). If they were infinite, there would be no priority or distinction among numbers, so no individual number could be singled out. But those beings do exist, so they must be specified by a particularizer (mukhassis). That particularization would make them temporal (haditha), yet they are supposed to be eternal. This is a contradiction. Therefore, the assumption of multiple eternal beings is false, and the being in question must be one.

Key Terms
وحدت
محال
مخصِّص
حدوث
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument directly proves the existence of God.
Correction: It actually proves that if there is an eternal being, it cannot be more than one. The existence of an eternal being is presupposed.

Checkpoint
What does the impossibility of an infinite number of existents lead to in this argument?
A) The necessity of a particularizer for each being
B) The necessity of oneness of the eternal being
C) The temporality of all beings
D) The existence of multiple eternal beings

Key Takeaway
The oneness of the necessary being is proven because multiple eternal beings would lead to the impossible consequence of an infinite number.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that treating anything as co-eternal with God leads to absurdity. Is there any person, possession, or ambition in your life that you treat as if it will never end—as if it were eternal? How can you begin to see it as the temporal thing it truly is?
?
This argument demands that the only being worthy of ultimate devotion is the One who is truly eternal. When you face trials, do you instinctively turn to something other than God as your ultimate refuge? What would it take for your heart to rely solely on the One who is truly without beginning?
?
Sanusi's reasoning forces a choice: either God is one or logic collapses. Have you ever been in a situation where you had to choose between trusting God alone and depending on a 'backup plan' that felt more reliable? How can you train yourself to make God your absolute priority in such moments?

The Impossibility of Two Gods: Disagreement and Agreement
Sanusi proves God's oneness through a dilemma of two gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that two gods would lead to impotence in both scenarios of disagreement and agreement.

Core Idea
Two gods would be powerless whether they agree or disagree.

Sanusi presents a classic kalam argument for God's oneness. He imagines what would happen if there were two creators: either they would disagree or agree on every action. In both cases, impotence follows, which is impossible for God. This lesson walks through the dilemma and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two scenarios
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Sanusi first states the claim (God must be one) and then gives the proof by examining two possibilities: disagreement or agreement between two gods.
The passage uses a conditional structure: 'if there were a second...'
2
Understand why disagreement leads to impotence
If two gods disagree on an action, one would dominate the other, implying weakness in the dominated. Either both are weak (if they mutually block each other) or one is weak (if the other prevails). Either way, impotence follows.
Impotence (عجز) is the inability to act.
3
Understand why even agreement leads to impotence
If two gods necessarily agree on every action, they cannot act independently. A single act cannot be divided between two agents, so they would mutually prevent each other, again leading to impotence.
The phrase 'الفعل الواحد يستحيل عليه الانقسام' is key: a single act cannot be split.
4
Grasp why impotence is impossible for God
Sanusi argues that impotence contradicts the divine attribute of power (قدرة). If impotence were eternal, God would never be powerful; if originated, it would imply a change in God's eternal attribute. Both are impossible.
This final step seals the proof: since impotence is impossible for God, the assumption of two gods is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [ في الوحدانية ] ثم نقول : يجب لهذا الصانع أن يكون واحداً ؛ إذ لو كانَ مَعَهُ ثانٍ لَلزِمَ عجزُهما أو عجز أحدهما عند الاختلافِ وقهرهما أو قهرُ أحدهما عند الاتفاقِ الواجب ، مع استحالة ما عُلِمَ إمكانُهُ لكلّ واحدٍ باعتبار الانفراد ، ونفي وجوبِ الوجودِ لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما ؛ للاستغناء بكلِّ منهما عن كلَّ منهما ، فإن لم يجب اتفاقهما بل جازَ اختلافهما .. لزمَ قبولُهما العجز ، وعادَ الأَوَّلُ . ويلزمُ أيضاً في الاتفاقِ مطلقاً العجزُ ؛ لأنَّ الفعل الواحد يستحيلُ عليه الانقسام ، فيتمانعان فيه ، فيلزم عجزهما أو عجز أحدهما كما في الاختلافِ ، والعجز على الإله محال ؛ لأنَّهُ يُضادُّ القدرة ؛ فإن كان قديماً لزم استحالةُ فيجب ألا يقدر هذا الإله على شيءٍ دائماً ، وإنْ كانَ حادثاً فضدُّه - وهو القدرة - قديمة ، فيستحيل عدمُها ، فلا يُوجَدُ العجزُ ، وأيضاً فيستحيل اتصافُ الإله بصفة عدمه [وهي] حادثة فإن قلت : فلِمَ لا يجوز أن ينقسم العالم بينهما ، فيكونَ أحدهما قادراً على أحد القسمين ، والآخَرُ على الآخر ، فلا يلزم التمانع ؟ فالجوابُ : أَنَّهُ تقرَّرَ قبلُ استحالة التناهي في مقدوراتِ الإلهِ ومراداتِهِ، فيستحيلُ هذا الفَرْضُ الذي ذُكِرَ في السؤالِ. وأيضاً : فالقسمان إن كانا معاً في الجواهر لزمَ مِنْ تعَلُّقِ
Section on Oneness. Then we say: It is necessary for this Maker to be one. For if there were a second with Him, it would necessitate the impotence of both or the impotence of one of them at the time of disagreement, and the dominance of both or the dominance of one of them at the time of necessary agreement, along with the impossibility of what is known to be possible for each one in terms of independence, and the negation of the necessity of existence for each of them due to the self-sufficiency of each from the other. For if their agreement is not necessary but rather their disagreement is possible, then they would accept impotence, and the first returns. It also necessitates in absolute agreement impotence, because a single act is impossible of division, so they would mutually prevent each other, so the impotence of both or of one of them follows as in disagreement. And impotence is impossible for God because it contradicts power. For if it were eternal, it would necessitate impossibility, so this god would not be able to do anything ever; and if it were originated, its opposite—which is power—is eternal, so its non-existence is impossible, so impotence does not occur. Also, it is impossible for God to be described with an attribute of non-existence, which is originated. If you say: Why is it not permissible for the world to be divided between them, so that one is able over one part and the other over the other, and mutual prevention would not follow? The answer: It has been established before the impossibility of finitude in the objects of God's power and will, so this supposition mentioned in the question is impossible. Also: the two parts, if they both exist in substances, it would necessitate from the attachment...
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a formal proof of God's oneness (وحدانية) using a dilemma. It begins by stating the claim: the Maker must be one. The proof: if there were two gods, then either they disagree or agree. If they disagree, one may dominate the other, implying weakness (عجز) in the dominated; if they agree necessarily, then both are dominated (since neither can act independently) or one is dominated. Also, under necessary agreement, a single act cannot be divided between two agents, so they would mutually prevent each other, leading to impotence. Since impotence is impossible for God (it contradicts the eternal attribute of power), the assumption of a second god is false. The objector proposes that the world could be divided between two gods to avoid conflict. The answer: God's power is infinite, so it cannot be limited to a part; also, if the parts are composed of atoms, each atom would require an act of power, etc. (answer incomplete in the source).

قهر
اتفاق
اختلاف
فعل واحد
تمانع
قدرة

Misconception: Two gods could cooperate and agree on all actions, thus avoiding conflict and impotence.
Correction: Even if they agree, a single act cannot be divided between two agents; they would mutually prevent each other, leading to impotence.

Checkpoint

A) They both act successfully
B) One must be impotent or dominated
C) They compromise

Key Takeaway
The necessity of God's oneness follows from the impossibility of impotence in a deity.

?
When you face a situation where you feel pulled between competing allegiances (work vs. family, desires vs. duty), how does the absolute oneness of the ultimate source of authority challenge you to reconsider whom you ultimately serve? What one step will you take today to align your actions under that single authority?
?
The proof shows that even agreement between two gods would lead to impotence. Sanusi argues that impotence is impossible for God because it contradicts eternal power. Objection and Answer: Dividing the World Between Two Gods
Sanusi responds to an objection about dividing the world between two gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that two gods could divide the world and avoid conflict, and why this is impossible.

Core Idea
Even dividing the world doesn't solve the problem because God's power is infinite and indivisible.

An objector proposes that two gods could simply split the universe between them, each ruling a separate domain, thereby avoiding conflict. Sanusi responds by invoking the infinite nature of God's power and the implications for the indivisibility of reality. This lesson examines that objection and the beginning of its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic text beginning with 'فإن قلت' and its translation. The objector suggests dividing the world into two parts, each under one god, so no mutual prevention occurs.
The objection is a natural counter to the previous proof.
2
Understand the proposed solution
The objector thinks that two gods could each be assigned a separate part of creation, so they do not interfere with each other. This would avoid both disagreement and the problem of a single act.
3
Grasp the first part of the answer
Sanusi responds that it has been established that God's objects of power and will are infinite (لا تناهي). Therefore, limiting God to a part of creation is impossible because His power cannot be contained.
The answer is cut off in the source, but this first argument is clear.
4
Note the incomplete second argument
The text begins a second argument about substances (الجواهر) but is incomplete. This shows that the author had more to say, likely building on the indivisibility of atoms.
The incomplete nature reminds us that the full text may have continued.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [ في الوحدانية ] ثم نقول : يجب لهذا الصانع أن يكون واحداً ؛ إذ لو كانَ مَعَهُ ثانٍ لَلزِمَ عجزُهما أو عجز أحدهما عند الاختلافِ وقهرهما أو قهرُ أحدهما عند الاتفاقِ الواجب ، مع استحالة ما عُلِمَ إمكانُهُ لكلّ واحدٍ باعتبار الانفراد ، ونفي وجوبِ الوجودِ لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما ؛ للاستغناء بكلِّ منهما عن كلَّ منهما ، فإن لم يجب اتفاقهما بل جازَ اختلافهما .. لزمَ قبولُهما العجز ، وعادَ الأَوَّلُ . ويلزمُ أيضاً في الاتفاقِ مطلقاً العجزُ ؛ لأنَّ الفعل الواحد يستحيلُ عليه الانقسام ، فيتمانعان فيه ، فيلزم عجزهما أو عجز أحدهما كما في الاختلافِ ، والعجز على الإله محال ؛ لأنَّهُ يُضادُّ القدرة ؛ فإن كان قديماً لزم استحالةُ فيجب ألا يقدر هذا الإله على شيءٍ دائماً ، وإنْ كانَ حادثاً فضدُّه - وهو القدرة - قديمة ، فيستحيل عدمُها ، فلا يُوجَدُ العجزُ ، وأيضاً فيستحيل اتصافُ الإله بصفة عدمه [وهي] حادثة فإن قلت : فلِمَ لا يجوز أن ينقسم العالم بينهما ، فيكونَ أحدهما قادراً على أحد القسمين ، والآخَرُ على الآخر ، فلا يلزم التمانع ؟ فالجوابُ : أَنَّهُ تقرَّرَ قبلُ استحالة التناهي في مقدوراتِ الإلهِ ومراداتِهِ، فيستحيلُ هذا الفَرْضُ الذي ذُكِرَ في السؤالِ. وأيضاً : فالقسمان إن كانا معاً في الجواهر لزمَ مِنْ تعَلُّقِ
Section on Oneness. Then we say: It is necessary for this Maker to be one. For if there were a second with Him, it would necessitate the impotence of both or the impotence of one of them at the time of disagreement, and the dominance of both or the dominance of one of them at the time of necessary agreement, along with the impossibility of what is known to be possible for each one in terms of independence, and the negation of the necessity of existence for each of them due to the self-sufficiency of each from the other. For if their agreement is not necessary but rather their disagreement is possible, then they would accept impotence, and the first returns. It also necessitates in absolute agreement impotence, because a single act is impossible of division, so they would mutually prevent each other, so the impotence of both or of one of them follows as in disagreement. And impotence is impossible for God because it contradicts power. For if it were eternal, it would necessitate impossibility, so this god would not be able to do anything ever; and if it were originated, its opposite—which is power—is eternal, so its non-existence is impossible, so impotence does not occur. Also, it is impossible for God to be described with an attribute of non-existence, which is originated. If you say: Why is it not permissible for the world to be divided between them, so that one is able over one part and the other over the other, and mutual prevention would not follow? The answer: It has been established before the impossibility of finitude in the objects of God's power and will, so this supposition mentioned in the question is impossible. Also: the two parts, if they both exist in substances, it would necessitate from the attachment...
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a formal proof of God's oneness (وحدانية) using a dilemma. It begins by stating the claim: the Maker must be one. The proof: if there were two gods, then either they disagree or agree. If they disagree, one may dominate the other, implying weakness (عجز) in the dominated; if they agree necessarily, then both are dominated (since neither can act independently) or one is dominated. Also, under necessary agreement, a single act cannot be divided between two agents, so they would mutually prevent each other, leading to impotence. Since impotence is impossible for God (it contradicts the eternal attribute of power), the assumption of a second god is false. The objector proposes that the world could be divided between two gods to avoid conflict. The answer: God's power is infinite, so it cannot be limited to a part; also, if the parts are composed of atoms, each atom would require an act of power, etc. (answer incomplete in the source).

تناهي
مقدورات
جواهر
تعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could avoid conflict by each ruling a separate part of the universe, like dividing territories.
Correction: This fails because God's power is infinite and cannot be limited to a part; also, the indivisibility of reality (atoms) makes such a division impossible.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
God's power is infinite and cannot be confined to a part of creation.

?
The objection suggests dividing the world between two gods as a solution to conflict. In your own heart, where have you divided your allegiance between Allah and something else? Are you willing to tear down that division and submit everything to His infinite power?

The Argument of Mutual Hindrance (Tamānuʿ) Regarding Divine Powers
Sanusi presents the tamānuʿ argument applied to divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the tamānuʿ argument as applied to the powers of multiple gods, and why it proves the impossibility of more than one creator.

Core Idea
Multiple creators would inevitably conflict, making their coexistence impossible.

Sanusi presents a refined version of the classical tamānuʿ argument. Instead of simply assuming conflict between two independent wills, he shows that even if the powers of multiple gods were different in scope, they would still lead to mutual hindrance due to the necessary connection between substances and their accidents. This argument reinforces the Islamic doctrine of tawḥīd.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: two scenarios.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage discusses two possibilities: powers are alike or powers are different (one creates substances, one creates accidents). Find them.
3
Understand the impossibility in each case
For the first, if powers are alike, they must attach to all objects, leading to conflict. For the second, even if different, the power to create substances requires power over accidents, so conflict still arises.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument shows that any scenario with multiple gods leads to contradiction, thus only one God exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدرة ببعضها تعلقها بالجميع ؛ للتماثل ، فيلزم التمانع ، وإنْ كانَ أحد القسمين الجواهر والآخرُ الأعراض .. فذلكَ لا يُعقَلُ ؛ إذ القدرة على إيجاد الجواهر لا تُعقل بدونِ القدرة على أعراضها ، وكذا العكس ؛ للتلازم الذي بينهما ، ثم ذلك لا يدفعُ التمانع عندما يريدُ أحدهما أنْ يُوجِدَ الجوهر ، والآخرُ لا يريدُ أنْ يُوجِدَ عرضَهُ
Power from some of them attaches to all [objects of power] due to similarity, so mutual hindrance necessarily follows. And if one of the two divisions is substances and the other accidents, that is not conceivable, because power to bring about substances is not conceivable without power to bring about their accidents, and likewise the opposite, due to the necessary connection between them. Then that does not prevent mutual hindrance when one of them wants to bring about the substance, and the other does not want to bring about its accident.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents a version of the tamānuʿ argument (argument from mutual hindrance) as applied to the powers of multiple gods. The author argues that if there were two or more gods, their powers would either be exactly alike (both able to create everything) or different (one creates substances, the other accidents). If they are alike, then each power must attach to all objects of power equally, leading to conflict: if one wants to create something and the other does not, contradiction arises. If they are different, it is still inconceivable because the power to create substances cannot be separated from the power to create their accidents, due to the necessary connection between substance and accident. Thus, even if one god wants to create a substance and the other wants to prevent its accident, there is still mutual hindrance. The argument demonstrates the impossibility of multiple creators.

Key Terms
القدرة
التمانع
الجوهر
العرض
التماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if powers are different (one creates substances, one creates accidents), there would be no conflict.
Correction: The author shows that even in that case, conflict is inevitable because power over substances requires power over accidents, so mutual hindrance still occurs.

Checkpoint
