A) Because substances and accidents are identical
B) Because power to create substances is not conceivable without power to create their accidents
C) Because the gods would agree with each other
D) Because accidents have no existence

Key Takeaway
The tamānuʿ argument shows that multiple gods would inevitably conflict, affirming the necessity of a single Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah alone has complete power over every atom, or do you sometimes act as if other forces (people, money, fate) share His control? What would change if you lived every moment with the certainty that no one can hinder His will?
?
This argument shows that even a supposed division of tasks between gods leads to impossibility. How does this challenge any hidden polytheism in your heart—like attributing your successes to your own effort while forgetting Allah?
?
The necessary connection between substance and accident means nothing in creation can be separated from its Creator's will. How does this make you rethink your worries about things you cannot control? Will you now entrust them fully to the One who has complete power?

Proof for Unity from Unity of Attributes
Rational proof for unity from attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for God's oneness based on the unity of attributes and why multiple gods lead to impossibility.

Core Idea
Multiple gods lead to either an infinite regress or an external specifier—both impossible.

Sanusi presents a concise rational proof for tawhid that builds on the earlier discussion of attributes. The argument shows that if more than one God existed, the number of gods would either be infinite or limited by an external cause—both of which are impossible. This lesson walks through the logical steps of that proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that the argument for unity from attributes assumes that Allah's attributes are one with His essence, not separate entities.
This premise was established earlier in the text.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the two possibilities presented: infinite number if equal to all contingents, or need for a specifier if limited.
3
Analyze the impossibility
An actual infinite number of gods is logically impossible. A need for a specifier implies an external cause, contradicting necessary existence.
Both consequences are impossible, so the assumption of multiple gods is false.
4
Connect to tawhid
The proof concludes that God must be one. Reflect on how this argument strengthens the doctrine of tawhid.
This is a rational proof, not just textual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويصحُ أنْ يُستدَلَّ على الوحدانية بما تقدَّم في وحدة الصفات ؛ فنقولُ : يلزمُ مِنْ تعدُّدِ الإلهِ وجودُ ما لا نهاية لهُ عدداً إن تعدد بعددِ الممكناتِ، أو الاحتياج إلى مُخصص إنْ وقف دونَ ذلكَ ، وكلاهما محالٌ .
And it is valid to argue for Unity (waḥdāniyya) by what preceded concerning the unity of attributes. So we say: From the multiplicity of god would follow the existence of what is infinite in number if they multiplied according to the number of contingent things, or the need for a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) if they stopped short of that. And both are impossible.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents another rational argument for the oneness of God, this time based on the unity of attributes (wahdat al-ṣifāt). The reasoning is that if there were multiple gods, they would either be equal in number to all possible contingents (which is infinite, leading to an infinite number of gods), or they would be limited in number but then there would need to be a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) that chooses that particular number. Both consequences are absurd: an infinite number of gods is impossible, and the need for a specifier would imply a cause outside the gods, which contradicts their divinity. Thus, multiple gods are impossible.

Key Terms

تعدد الإله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The argument builds on the premise that attributes are identical with the essence, which was established earlier in the discussion of attribute unity.

Checkpoint

B) There would be a need for a specifier
C) They would be infinite
D) They would not be able to create

Key Takeaway
The oneness of God is proven by the impossibility of multiple gods, because they would lead to either an infinite number or a need for an external specifier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument requires that a finite number of gods would need an external specifier. In your life, when you try to serve both Allah and your own agenda, who or what is the ‘specifier’ that decides where you stop? Are you willing to let Allah alone be the One who determines your priorities?
?
If multiple deities are logically impossible, then any allegiance you give to anything besides Allah is not just misguided—it is built on an absurdity. What concrete step can you take this week to align your actions with the reality that there is no god but Him?

Applying Tamānuʿ to Human Actions
Sanusi uses the tamānuʿ argument to prove God creates human acts and refute independent human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the tamānuʿ argument to show that God creates human actions and that human power is concurrent but not creative.

Core Idea
God alone creates; human power accompanies but does not cause.

Sanusi now extends the same rational proof of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) to a key theological question: where do human actions come from? If the tamānuʿ argument proves there is only one Creator, then no other being—including humans—can truly create their own acts. But we still feel a difference between shivering and raising a hand. This passage explains that apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation side by side. Notice how Sanusi uses the phrase 'by this same proof' to link back to the earlier argument.
The tamānuʿ argument has already been established in the previous lesson.
2
Identify the two claims
The passage makes two main claims: (1) God is the creator of human actions, and human powers have no effect; (2) Yet we affirm a concurrent human power because of the observable difference between voluntary and involuntary movements.
These two claims together form the Ashʿarī doctrine of kasb (acquisition).
3
Understand the evidence
Sanusi points to the 'necessary difference' between involuntary movement (like a shiver) and voluntary movement (like raising your hand). This difference shows that some power is present in voluntary acts, even if that power does not create the act.
This power is not a cause but a mere accompaniment.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: If God creates everything, what role do we have? How does this view preserve both divine sovereignty and human responsibility?
The answer lies in the concept of acquisition (kasb), which is not mentioned here explicitly but underlies the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا الدليل بعينه ـ أعني : دليل التمانع ـ نستدل على أَنَّهُ جلَّ وعلا هو الموجدُ لأفعال العبادِ ، ولا تأثيرَ لقُدَرِهم الحادثة فيها ، بل هي موجودة مقارنة لها . وإِنَّما قلنا بوجودِ قُدَرِ مقارنة ؛ لما نجده مِنَ الفرقِ الضروري بينَ حركة الاضطرار وحركة الاختيار .
And by this same proof—I mean the proof of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ)—we argue that He, may He be exalted and glorified, is the bringer-into-existence of the actions of servants, and there is no effect for their newly-arrived powers in them; rather they exist concurrent with them. And we only said that there exist concurrent powers because of what we find of the necessary difference between involuntary movement and voluntary movement.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
In this passage, Sanusi extends the tamānuʿ argument to the question of human actions. Since the tamānuʿ argument proves that there is only one Creator, it follows that all actions in existence are created by God alone, including human actions. Human beings do have a 'newly-arrived power' (qudrat ḥāditha) that is concurrent with their actions, but this power has no causal effect. The reason we affirm such a concurrent power is the evident difference we perceive between involuntary movements (like a shiver) and voluntary movements (like raising a hand). This difference indicates that there is some power present in the voluntary case, but it is not the creator of the action; it only 'acquires' (kash) the action.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this passage denies any human role or free will, making humans like puppets.
Correction: Sanusi affirms a concurrent human power that distinguishes voluntary from involuntary acts, preserving a meaningful role (acquisition) while denying independent creation.

Key Takeaway

Proving God's Existence from Your Contingency
Sanusi provides a syllogistic proof for God's existence from the contingency of the self.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses a rational syllogism based on your own existence to prove that God exists.

Core Idea
You did not create yourself — that simple fact proves there is a Creator.

Sanusi begins by addressing the blind follower who relies on imitation rather than reason. He urges them to look at the closest thing to them: themselves. By reflecting on the fact that they came into existence after not existing, they can logically infer that they must have a creator. This passage presents that proof in a clear syllogistic form.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the English translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the syllogistic structure.
2
Understand the syllogism
Identify the minor premise: 'I was not, then I was.' Identify the major premise: 'Everything contingent has a creator.' The conclusion is: 'Therefore, I have a creator.'
Sanusi uses the terms 'existent after non-existence' and 'contingent (hadith)' synonymously.
3
Reflect on the impossibility of self-creation
Consider why self-creation is impossible: it would require you to exist before yourself, a logical contradiction. This reinforces the need for an external creator.
This step connects the argument to everyday experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الالمينات
النظر في إثبات صفة الوجود له سبحانه وتعالى
وإذا عرفت هذا - أيها المقلد الناظر لنفسه بعين الرحمة - فأقرب شيء يخرجك عن التقليد بعون الله: أن تنظر إلى أقرب الأشياء إليك؛ وذلك نفسك. قال الله تعالى: {وفي أنفسكم أفلا تبصرون}. فتعلم على الضرورة أنك لم تكن ثم كنت، فتعلم أن لك موجداً أوجدك؛ لاستحالة أن توجد نفسك، وإلا لأمكن أن توجد ما هو أهون عليك من نفسك، وهو ذات غيرك؛ لمساواته لك في الإمكان. وإنما قلنا: (أهون عليك) لما في إيجادك نفسك من زيادة التهافت والجمع بين متنافيين؛ وهو تقدمك على نفسك وتأخرك عنها؛ لوجوب سبق الفاعل على فعله. فإذا كانت ذاته نفس فعله.. لزم المحذور المذكور.
الإشارة بـ (هذا) راجعة إلى مضمون ما سبق؛ وهو ضعف التقليد والخشية على صاحبه، وكيفية النظم للاستدلال بالنفس أن تقول: أنا لم أكن ثم كنت، أو أنا موجود بعد عدم، أو أنا حادث، كلها بمعنى. وكل من لم يكن ثم كان، أو كل موجود بعد عدم، أو كل حادث.. فله موجد أوجده. فينتج هذا البرهان: أنا لي موجد أوجدني.
Chapter on the Divine Matters. Consideration of Proving the Attribute of Existence for Him – Glorified and Exalted. And if you have understood this – O blind follower (muqallid) looking at yourself with an eye of mercy – then the closest thing that brings you out of blind following (taqlid) with the help of God is that you look at the closest of things to you; and that is your self. God Almighty said: {And in yourselves, do you not see?} So you know by necessity that you were not, then you were; so you know that you have a bringer-into-existence (mujid) who brought you into existence; because it is impossible that you bring yourself into existence, for otherwise it would be possible for you to bring into existence something easier for you than your self, namely the essence of another, due to its equality with you in possibility (imkan). And we only said: (easier for you) because in your causing your own existence there is an increase of incoherence (tafatt) and joining together of two mutually exclusive things (mutanafiyayn); namely your preceding yourself and your being posterior to it; because it is necessary that the agent precede its act. So if its essence were itself its act... the mentioned absurdity would follow. The reference with (this) refers to the content of what preceded; namely the weakness of blind following (taqlid) and fear for its possessor. And the manner of constructing the inference (istidlal) by the self is that you say: I was not, then I was, or I am an existent after non-existence, or I are contingent (hadith), all have the same meaning. And everything that was not then was, or every existent after non-existence, or every contingent... has a bringer-into-existence who brought it into existence. So this proof (burhan) yields: I have a bringer-into-existence who brought me into existence.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) for the existence of God from the contingency of one's own self. The author first addresses the muqallid (blind follower) and urges him to look at his own self as the closest object of reflection, citing the Quranic verse 'And in yourselves, do you not see?' The argument proceeds: one knows by necessity that one was not, then came to be. Therefore, one must have a bringer-into-existence (mujid) who brought one into existence. This is because it is impossible for one to bring oneself into existence, since that would require one to exist before oneself, a contradiction. If self-creation were possible, then one could create something easier than oneself, like another person, but that is also impossible due to equality in possibility. The author explains the absurdity of self-creation: it would require one to be both prior and posterior to oneself, a logical impossibility. Then he presents the formal syllogism: 'I was not, then I was' (or 'I am contingent'), and 'Everything that was not then was (or every contingent) has a bringer-into-existence'; therefore, 'I have a bringer-into-existence'. This is the classical contingent argument in a structured form.

Key Terms
وجود
موجد
حادث
برهان
تقليد
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof relies merely on introspection without logical structure.
Correction: Sanusi clearly presents it as a syllogism with a universal premise and a particular conclusion.

Checkpoint
What is the key reason Sanusi gives to show that one cannot bring oneself into existence?
A) It is too difficult for a human
B) It would require one to exist before oneself
C) God has already done it
D) The universe is eternal

Key Takeaway
By reflecting on your own existence, you can rationally conclude that you have a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that you were not then you were. Do you truly live as though your existence depends entirely on its Giver, or do you act as if you own your life? What would change today if you internalized that every breath is a gift given anew?
?
The proof shows that self-creation is impossible. Yet do you ever find yourself living as if you are self-sufficient, ignoring your contingency? What specific thought or action can you take today to remind yourself that you are dependent on Allah?
?
Sanusi addresses the muqallid—the blind follower. Are you content to believe in God just because your family does, or have you personally reflected on the evidence? If you were asked right now to produce a rational argument for God's existence from your own self, could you do it? If not, what step will you take to move from blind imitation to certainty?

The First Premise and the Self-Evident Second Premise
Sanusi states the two premises and argues the second is self-evident via fitra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first premise of the cosmological argument is self-evident and how some scholars argue that the second premise is also known by innate disposition without proof.

Core Idea
The existence of change is obvious; the need for a cause is instinctive.

Sanusi begins by laying out two premises that form the backbone of the kalam cosmological argument. The first premise is something every rational person knows without argument: that they themselves were once non-existent and then came to be. The second premise—that every new event needs a cause—is claimed by some to be equally innate. In this lesson, we examine these foundational claims and the striking evidence al-Razi provided from children and animals.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the minor premise
Read the Arabic text for the first premise and its translation. Notice how Sanusi describes it as 'known necessarily' (معلومة بالضرورة).
2
Read the discussion on the major premise
Read the part where Sanusi presents the view that the second premise is self-evident. Pay attention to al-Razi's examples from children and donkeys.
Al-Razi argues that even animals instinctively know that effects have causes.
3
Reflect on the strength of these claims
Ask yourself: Is it really self-evident that every event needs a cause? Why does Sanusi present these arguments?
This prepares you for the next lesson's proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا المُقدِّمةُ الأولى وهي الصغرى : فلا تفتقر إلى دليل ؛ لأنَّها معلومة بالضرورةِ ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ عاقل لا يرتابُ في أنَّ هيئته المخصوصة التي هو عليها وبها تَحقَّقَتْ حقيقته الإنسانية مثلاً .. كانَتْ معدومةً ثم كانت وأمَّا المُقدِّمةُ الثانية - وهي الكبرى - الحاكمة بافتقار كل حادث إلى مُحدث - بكسر الدال - : فمنهم مَنْ يدَّعي أنَّها ضرورية لا تفتقر إلى دليل ، حتى قال الفخرُ في ( المعالم » : ( إنَّ العلم بها مركوز في فطرة طباع الصبيان ، فإِنَّكَ إذا لطمتَ وجْهَ الصبيِّ مِنْ حيثُ لا يراك ، وقلتَ لهُ : إِنَّهُ حصلَتْ هذهِ اللطمةُ مِنْ غيرِ فاعل ألبتة .. لا يُصدقك ، بل في فطرة البهائم ؛ فإنَّ الحمار إذا أحسَّ بصوتِ الخشبة فزع ؛ لأنَّهُ تَقَرَّرَ في فطرتِهِ أنَّ حصول صوتِ الخشبة بدونِ الخشبة محال ) ومنهم مَنْ يُقرِّرُها بوسط ؛ أي: بدليل ، فيقولُ : إِنَّ الحادث إذا حدث في الوقتِ المعيَّنِ فالعقلُ لا يمنعُ صحة تقدُّمِهِ على الوقت الذي وُجِدَ فيه بأوقاتٍ ، أو تأخُرِهِ عنه بساعاتٍ ، فاختصاصه بالوجودِ في ذلك الوقت بدلاً عن العدم المُجوَّز يفتقر إلى مُخصص - بكسر الصادِ - وإلا كان أحد الأمرين المتساويين مساوياً لذاته راجحاً لذاته ، وهو محال ضرورةً ، فتعيَّنَ أنْ يكونَ الترجيح للوجودِ بدلاً عن العدمِ بمُرجح منفصل عن الحادثِ ؛ وهو الفاعل المختار جلَّ وعلا هذا إن قلنا : إِنَّ الوجود والعدم بالنسبة إلى الممكن مستويانِ وهو المختار ، أمَّا إِنْ قلنا : إنَّ العدمَ أَولى بهِ مِنَ الوجودِ ؛ لَقبُولِهِ إِيَّاهُ بلا سبب .. فأظهرُ في الاحتياج إلى الصانع ؛ لئلا يلزم ترجيح الوجودِ المرجوح بلا مُرجح
As for the first premise, which is the minor premise: it does not require a proof, because it is known necessarily; because every rational person does not doubt that his specific form which he has and by which his human reality is realized, for example, was non-existent then came into existence. As for the second premise—which is the major premise—stating that every contingent event (hadith) needs an efficient cause (muhdith) [with kasr of the dal]: some claim that it is necessary and does not need a proof, so much so that al-Fakhr said in al-Ma'alim: 'Knowledge of it is ingrained in the innate disposition (fitra) of children, for if you slap the face of a child from where he does not see you, and you say to him: This slap occurred from no agent at all, he will not believe you. Rather it is in the innate disposition of beasts; for the donkey, when it hears the sound of a stick, becomes frightened, because it is established in its nature that the occurrence of the sound of a stick without a stick is impossible.' And some establish it by an intermediary, i.e., by a proof, saying: When a contingent event occurs at a specific time, the intellect does not prevent the validity of its preceding the time in which it occurred by times, or its being delayed from it by hours. So its being particularized (ikhtisas) to existence at that time instead of the possible non-existence requires a particularizer (mukhassis) [with kasr of the sad]; otherwise one of the two equal alternatives would be equal in itself while being preponderant in itself, and that is impossible necessarily. So it is determined that the preponderance (tarjih) for existence instead of non-existence must be by a preponderator (murajjih) separate from the contingent event; that is the free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) exalted and high. This is if we say that existence and non-existence with respect to the possible are equal—which is the chosen view. As for if we say that non-existence is more appropriate for it than existence, due to its accepting it without cause, it is more obvious in the need for the Maker, lest there follow the preponderance of the preponderated existence without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the two premises of the classic kalam cosmological argument. The author begins by stating the first premise (the minor premise): every rational person knows necessarily that their own body or form was non-existent and then came to exist. This is self-evident and needs no proof. Then he turns to the second premise (the major premise): every contingent event (hadith) requires an efficient cause (muhdith). He notes that there is disagreement: some claim this premise is also self-evident (necessary), citing al-Razi's examples from children and animals. Others, however, insist it must be proven. The author then presents the proof used by those who require a demonstration: the argument from specification (ikhtisas). This argument proceeds as follows: If a contingent event occurs at a specific time, its existence at that time rather than earlier or later cannot be purely accidental; it requires a particularizer (mukhassis) that selects that time. Without such a particularizer, the event would be equally possible at any time and would have to preponderate itself, which is impossible. Therefore, there must be a preponderator (murajjih) external to the event, which is the free agent (God). The author then notes two possible assumptions about the nature of the possible: either existence and non-existence are equal, or non-existence is more appropriate. In either case, the need for a cause is established. This proof is the classic way in kalam to establish the necessity of a creator.

Key Terms
حادث
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does the first premise need no proof?
A) Because it is a rational demonstration.
B) Because it is known necessarily by every rational person.
C) Because it is revealed in scripture.
D) Because it is adopted from Greek philosophy.

Key Takeaway
The first premise of the cosmological argument is self-evident: we all know we came into existence. The second premise may also be innate, as shown by instinctive examples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience an unexpected change in your life—good or bad—do you instinctively recognize that it originates from a cause? How does this innate awareness, which you share with animals, relate to your acknowledgment of Allah as the ultimate cause?
?
The child refused to accept a slap without an agent. Reflect on a time you attributed an event to 'chance' or 'luck' instead of to Allah. What would it cost you to correct that habit starting today?
?
If belief in causation is embedded in your fitra, why do you sometimes suppress it when it comes to matters of faith? What comfort do you gain by denying the obvious need for a Creator?

The Proof for the Second Premise: Argument from Specification
Sanusi provides the argument from specification to prove the major premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that every contingent event requires a cause, known as the argument from specification (ikhtisas), and how it leads to the conclusion of a free agent creator.

Core Idea
A contingent event's specific time of occurrence cannot explain itself; it needs an external chooser.

While some scholars argue that the need for a cause is innate, others insist on a rational proof. In this passage, Sanusi presents that proof: the argument from specification. The reasoning goes that a contingent event, which could have occurred earlier or later, is found at a specific time. This specification requires a particularizer (mukhassis); otherwise the event would have to prefer itself, which is impossible. This leads to the necessity of a free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) outside the event. Additionally, Sanusi refines the proof by considering two possible assumptions about the nature of possible existents.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof from specification
Read the Arabic text and translation for the argument from specification. Follow the logical steps: event at specific time → could have been earlier/later → requires a particularizer → otherwise self-preponderance impossible → therefore a preponderator.
Notice the key terms: مخصص (particularizer), مرجح (preponderator).
2
Consider the two assumptions about the possible
Read the last part where Sanusi discusses two views: existence and non-existence are equal, or non-existence is more appropriate. How do each lead to the need for a cause?
Sanusi prefers the view that they are equal.
3
Reflect on why the argument is called 'from specification'
Think about how the specific timing of an event demands an external chooser. This is a key argument in kalam theology.
4
Review the conclusion
The proof concludes that the preponderator is 'the free agent' (الفاعل المختار). Why must it be free and not a natural cause?
The argument establishes that the cause is separate from the event and acts by choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refutation of the Claim that the Major Premise is Self-Evident
Sanusi refutes al-Razi's examples from children and animals, affirming the premise is theoretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes al-Razi's examples to prove that knowledge of the major premise is theoretical, not necessary.

Core Idea
Even close theoretical knowledge is not necessary knowledge.

This passage tackles a subtle but crucial distinction: some knowledge appears self-evident because it is very close to immediate, but it remains theoretical. Sanusi presents the correct view and then dismantles al-Razi's examples from children and animals, showing that they do not prove necessity but are easily explained by short reasoning or imagination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how Sanusi first states his own position before refuting al-Razi.
Focus on the logical flow of correction and refutation.
2
Identify Sanusi's main claim
Find where Sanusi says the major premise is theoretical (nazari) but obtained by a short consideration. Why does he say some mistakenly think it's necessary?
This is the central thesis of the passage.
3
Understand the refutation of children
Analyze how Sanusi denies that all children have this knowledge innately. He argues that even discerning children possess theoretical knowledge, so the claim fails.
Notice the phrase 'how so? For we see children...'
4
Understand the refutation of animals
Examine the animal example: Sanusi explains that a donkey's fear of a stick's sound is due to repeated association of pain, not a universal rational judgment. The speckled rope analogy illustrates this as imagination, not science.
This refutation uses the distinction between imagination (khayal) and scientific discrimination (tamyiz 'ilmi).
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters for arguments about God's existence. The major premise must be established through reasoning, even if that reasoning is short.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصحيح : أنَّ العلم بتلك المقدّمة الكبرى نظري ، إلا أنَّهُ يحصل بنظرِ قريبٍ كما قرَّرناه الآنَ ، ولأجلِ قربِهِ ظَنَّ قومٌ أَنَّ ذلكَ العلم ضروري وأمَّا مبالغةُ الفخر بأنَّهُ في فطرةِ الصبيان : فممنوع عمومه في جميعهم ، وإن أراد في فطرةِ أكثر مميّزيهم .. فمُسلَّم ، لكن لا تُسلّم أنَّهُ لا علم لمميَّزيهم إلا الضروري حتى يلزمَ ما ذَكَرَ ، كيف ونحن نرى الصبيان لا ينفكُونَ عن علوم نظريَّةِ لا سيما القريبة التي لا تعارضها شبهة ويتمخض العقلُ فيها ؟! وأما المبالغةُ بأنَّهُ مركوز أيضاً في فطرة البهائم بدليل ما ذَكَرَهُ في صوتِ الخشبةِ : فمِنْ أعجبِ ما يذكرُ أنَّ البهائمَ تُدرِكُ قضايا كليَّةً ولوازمها !! فلو قُدِّرَ حمارٌ أو حيوان غيرُهُ لم يُضرَبْ قطَّ بخشبة .. لم يَنفِرْ مِنْ صوتها ألبتة ، ولكن إذا تكرّرَ عليهِ ذلكَ التألُمُ عند سماعِها تخيَّلَ مِنْ حِسِّها الألم ؛ لمقارنتِهِ المُؤلِمَ ، وعدم التمييز والانفكاك في خيالِهِ ، كما أنَّ السليمَ يَنفِرُ مِنَ الحبل المبرقش لمقارنته الأذى عندَه لهذا الشكل ، وهذا مِنَ الخيالاتِ ، لا مِنَ التمييز العلمي ، والله
And the correct [view] is that knowledge of that major premise is theoretical (nazari), except that it is obtained by a short consideration (nazar qarib) as we just established; and because of its closeness, some people thought that knowledge is necessary. As for al-Fakhr's exaggeration that it is in the innate disposition of children: its generality over all of them is denied; if he meant the disposition of most of those who can discern (mumayyizeen), then it is granted, but it is not granted that there is no knowledge for those who can discern except the necessary, such that what he mentioned would follow. How so? For we see children are not free from theoretical sciences, especially the close ones that are not opposed by a doubt and in which the intellect develops? As for the exaggeration that it is also ingrained in the innate disposition of beasts, as evidenced by what he mentioned about the sound of a stick: this is among the most amazing things mentioned—that beasts comprehend universal propositions and their implications! For if a donkey or other animal that had never been struck with a stick were supposed, it would not flee from its sound at all. But when that pain is repeated upon it upon hearing it, it imagines pain from its sensation due to the connection to the painful thing, and due to the lack of discrimination and separation in its imagination, just as a healthy person flees from a speckled rope due to its connection to harm in his experience. This is from imaginations, not from scientific discrimination. And Allah [knows best].
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is the author's correction and refutation of the claim that the major premise is necessary. He begins by stating the correct view: knowledge of that premise is theoretical (nazari) but so close to self-evidence that some mistake it for necessary. Then he refutes al-Razi's examples one by one. Regarding children, he denies that all children have this knowledge innately; if al-Razi meant most discerning children, it is granted but does not prove it is necessary because children also have many theoretical bits of knowledge that are close to immediate. Regarding animals, he finds it astonishing that al-Razi attributes universal judgments to beasts. Animals may flee from sounds based on repeated association of pain, not from a rational recognition that sound implies a cause. Their response is imaginative, not intellectual. The author provides an analogy: a healthy person may flee from a speckled rope because they associate it with a snake's bite, even though the rope is harmless. This is not rational but imaginative. Thus, the examples fail to prove the premise is necessary, and the major premise remains theoretical, though easy to grasp.

Key Terms
نظري
ضروري
فطرة
تمييز
خيال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The major premise is self-evident because children and animals recognize it.
Correction: Sanusi shows that children's knowledge is theoretical (though close) and animals react from associative imagination, not rational necessity.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi refute al-Razi's animal example (the donkey and the stick)?
A) He denies that animals can hear sounds.
B) He argues that animals only react based on repeated association of pain, not rational necessity.
C) He says animals have no innate dispositions.
D) He claims the donkey's reaction is actually a proof of necessary knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The major premise for proving God's existence is theoretical (nazari), not necessary, but it is so close to self-evidence that only a short consideration is needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief about Allah simply because it felt immediate or natural, without ever questioning whether it was actually based on sound reasoning? What would it mean for your faith if that belief turned out to be merely theoretical and not necessary?
?
Sanusi shows that even children acquire theoretical knowledge as they grow. When you learn something new about your Creator, do you actively engage your intellect, or do you passively rely on familiar associations? Could some of your certainties really be just deeply ingrained assumptions?
?
The example of the animal fleeing from the stick's sound reveals how easily we confuse imagination with rational certainty. Where in your own spiritual life might you be confusing emotional familiarity with genuine knowledge? What would it take to honestly examine your convictions?

Four Valid Paths to Knowing the Creator
Sanusi enumerates four opinions on the source of the contingent's need for a Creator and declares all valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four theological opinions on the basis of the contingent's need for a Creator and Sanusi's affirmation that all are valid paths to knowledge.

Core Idea
All four opinions on why creation needs a Creator are valid paths to knowledge.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a classification of theological arguments for the existence of a Creator. Theologians differ on what exactly makes contingent beings dependent on a Maker. Sanusi lists four views and then declares that all are legitimate ways to reach certainty about the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a heading, then a list of opinions, then a conclusion.
The passage begins with 'منشأ احتياج الحادث إلى الصانع'.
2
Identify the four opinions
Find each of the four opinions: contingency, origination, both together, contingency conditioned by origination. Note the authorities mentioned: al‑Baydawi and most theologians.
3
Understand Sanusi's conclusion
Read Sanusi's verdict: 'the truth is that all of them are ways leading to knowledge of the Maker.' Reflect on the significance of this inclusive stance.
This shows that multiple arguments can be sound.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why Sanusi might affirm all views. How does this affect the way we approach theological proof?
It encourages tolerance and methodological pluralism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منشأ احتياج الحادث إلى الصانع ] وقد اختلف المُتكلّمون في منشأ احتياج الحادث إلى الصانع : فقيل : الإمكانُ ، وهو اختيار ناصر الدين البيضاوي وجماعة (٤) وقيل : الحدوث ، وهو عمدة أكثر المتكلمين ، وقيل : مجموعهما وقيل : الإمكانُ بشرطِ الحدوثِ . والحق : أنَّها كلَّها طرقٌ مُوصِلةٌ إلى العلم بالصانع
[The origin of the contingent's need for the Maker] And the theologians have differed over the origin of the contingent's need for the Maker: for it is said: contingency, and that is the choice of Nasir al‑Din al‑Baydawi and a group; and it is said: origination, and that is the mainstay of most theologians; and it is said: both of them; and it is said: contingency with the condition of origination. And the truth is: that all of them are ways leading to knowledge of the Maker.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of opinions among theologians regarding the basis upon which a contingent being (hadith) needs a Creator. Four views are listed: (1) contingency (imkan) alone, chosen by al‑Baydawi and others; (2) origination (huduth) alone, which is the position of the majority; (3) both together; (4) contingency conditioned by origination (imkan bi-shart al‑huduth). Sanusi then states the correct position: that all these are valid ways that lead to knowledge of the Creator. The passage thus affirms the legitimacy of multiple argumentative strategies in proving God's existence.

Sanusi teaches that multiple theological arguments for God's existence are valid, not just one.

?

In your life, do you act as if only your understanding of God is correct, or do you genuinely accept that others may have arrived at the same truth through different reasoning? This lesson challenges you to examine your heart for any trace of intellectual arrogance.

Eight Ways Reduced to Six for Proving the Creator
Sanusi enumerates eight argumentative ways and reduces them to six.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi derives eight argumentative ways for the Creator's existence from four opinions and two considerations, and then reduces them to six by eliminating the redundant 'imkan bi-shart al-huduth'.

Core Idea
Eight ways become six when we remove the redundant contingency-with-origination argument.

Sanusi presents a neat mathematical classification of the arguments for God's existence. By considering four possible bases of need (contingency, origination, both, etc.) and applying them to either essences or attributes, we get eight ways. But one formula is redundant, so only six remain. This lesson walks through that logical reduction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the four opinions
Recall the four opinions on the basis of need: pure contingency (imkan), pure origination (huduth), both together, and contingency with the condition of origination (imkan bi-shart al-huduth).
These are the four factors that will be multiplied.
2
Note the two considerations
Each opinion can be considered either in the essences (dhawat) of contingent beings or in their attributes (sifat). This gives two categories.
3
Multiply to get eight
Multiplying the four opinions by the two considerations yields eight distinct argumentative ways.
4 × 2 = 8
4
Identify the redundant formula
The way of 'imkan bi-shart al-huduth' is not truly distinct; it combines imkan and huduth. Sanusi removes it, which eliminates both its application to essences and to attributes, dropping two ways.
5
Confirm the final six
After elimination, six ways remain. Sanusi notes that Fakhr al-Din al-Razi also counted six ways in his works.
The reduction is authoritative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي إما أن تُعتبر في الذواتِ ، أو في الصفاتِ ، فتكونُ الطرقُ الموصلة ثمانيةً ; مِنْ ضَرْب أربعةٍ في اثنينِ ، وإنْ أُسقِطَ منها طريق الإمكان بشرطِ الحدوثِ ; لأنَّه يرجعُ في الصورة إلى طريق الاستدلال بمجموع الإمكان والحدوثِ . . سقط بسببهِ مِنَ الثمانية طريقان ، فتبقى ست طرق ، وكذا عدَّها الفخرُ في ( الأربعينَ » ، وعدَّها في «المعالم
And they are either considered in the essences or in the attributes, so the ways leading [to knowledge] become eight—by multiplying four by two. And if the way of contingency with the condition of origination is omitted, because it returns in form to the method of argument by the combination of contingency and origination, then due to its omission two ways drop from the eight, leaving six ways. And thus al‑Fakhr counted them in al‑Arba'in and counted them in al‑Ma'alim.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage explains how the four opinions on the basis of need (imkan, huduth, both, imkan bi-shart al‑huduth) can be considered under two respects: either in the essences (dhawat) or in the attributes (sifat). This multiplication yields eight possible argumentative ways (4 × 2 = 8). Then, Sanusi notes that if we remove the formula 'imkan bi-shart al‑huduth', because it is essentially reducible to the combination of imkan and huduth, then two ways drop (since that formula applies to both essences and attributes, but is removed in both). This leaves six ways. He confirms that Fakhr al‑Din al‑Razi counted them as six in his works al‑Arba'in and al‑Ma'alim. The passage thus provides a systematic enumeration and reduction of the valid arguments for the Creator's existence.

Key Terms
ذوات
صفات
ضرب
إسقاط
طرق ست

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all eight ways are distinct and equally valid.
Correction: The text shows that the way of 'imkan bi-shart al-huduth' is reducible to the combination of imkan and huduth, so it is not truly distinct. Removing it leaves six valid ways.

Checkpoint
Why does the number of ways reduce from eight to six?
A) Because two ways are invalid
B) Because the way of 'imkan bi-shart al-huduth' reduces to the combination way
C) Because al-Razi disagreed
D) Because the attributes category is dropped

Key Takeaway
The eight ways are reduced to six by removing the redundant 'imkan bi-shart al-huduth', as recognized by al-Razi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi streamlined the arguments for Allah's existence to six clear ways. Which of these six ways has personally strengthened your belief the most, and why? If you haven't reflected on them all, what is one way you can begin exploring them today?
?
This lesson shows that some arguments are redundant and can be removed without loss. In your own faith journey, are there weak or unnecessary 'proofs' you lean on? What would it look like to replace them with sounder foundations from this lesson?

Disagreement on the Basis of the Need for a Creator
Sanusi enumerates the different views on the basis of the need for a Creator and the resulting number of inference paths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different opinions on what makes an originated being need a Creator and how these views yield multiple valid paths to knowledge of the Creator.

Core Idea
All valid paths to God arise from possibility and origination, applied to essences or attributes.

Sanusi presents a scholarly disagreement among theologians about the precise basis for a contingent thing's need for a Creator. He lists four main opinions, then shows how considering essences and attributes multiplies the number of valid argumentative paths. This passage is a classic example of systematic classification in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the four bases
The passage lists four opinions on the basis of the need for a Creator: possibility (imkān), origination (ḥudūth), both together, and possibility conditioned by origination. Locate each one.
The fourth is a compound view.
3
Understand the multiplication
Sanusi says each basis can be considered in the essences (dhāt) or in the attributes (ṣifāt), giving 4 × 2 = 8 paths. See how this works conceptually.
This shows the systematic nature of kalam reasoning.
4
Follow the reductions
The fourth basis is dropped because it reduces to the combination of the first two, so two paths fall, leaving six. Then al-Razi drops two more, leaving four. Trace why each reduction occurs.
The reductions show a preference for simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اختلف المُتكلّمون في منشأ احتياج الحادث إلى الصانع : فقيل : الإمكانُ ، وهو اختيار ناصر الدين البيضاوي وجماعة وقيل : الحدوث ، وهو عمدة أكثر المتكلمين ، وقيل : مجموعهما وقيل : الإمكانُ بشرطِ الحدوثِ . والحق : أنَّها كلَّها طرقٌ مُوصِلةٌ إلى العلم بالصانع ، وهي إما أن تُعتبر في الذواتِ ، أو في الصفاتِ ، فتكونُ الطرقُ الموصلة ثمانيةً ؛ مِنْ ضَرْب أربعةٍ في اثنينِ ، وإنْ أُسقِطَ منها طريق الإمكان بشرطِ الحدوثِ ؛ لأنَّه يرجعُ في الصورة إلى طريق الاستدلال بمجموع الإمكان والحدوثِ . . سقط بسببهِ مِنَ الثمانية طريقان ، فتبقى ست طرق ، وكذا عدَّها الفخرُ في الأربعين ، وعدَّها في المعالم أربعة ، لأنَّه أسقط منها الطريقين الأخيرين ؛ لتركبهما مِنَ الأولين .
The theologians differed concerning the origin of the need of the originated [thing] for the Maker: and it was said: [it is] possibility (al-imkān), which is the choice of Nāṣir al-Dīn al-Bayḍāwī and a group; and it was said: origination (al-ḥudūth), which is the mainstay of most theologians; and it was said: both of them together; and it was said: possibility with the condition of origination. And the truth is: that all of them are paths leading to knowledge of the Maker, and they are either considered in the essences or in the attributes; so the leading paths become eight—by multiplying four by two—and if the path of possibility with the condition of origination is dropped, because it returns in form to the path of inference by the combination of possibility and origination, then by its falling two of the eight paths drop, so six paths remain; and thus al-Fakhr counted them in al-Arbaʿīn, and he counted them in al-Maʿālim as four, because he dropped the last two paths due to their being composed from the first two.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a classification and enumeration of the different theological opinions on what exactly gives rise to the need of an originated being (ḥādith) for a Creator. It begins by listing four views: (1) possibility (imkān), (2) origination (ḥudūth), (3) both together, and (4) possibility conditioned by origination. The author then states that all these are valid paths to knowledge of the Creator. Next, he explains that if we consider these approaches as applied either to essences or to attributes, we get eight paths (4 × 2). However, the path of possibility conditioned by origination is deemed redundant because it reduces to the combination of the first two, so dropping it removes two paths, leaving six. Al-Fakhr (al-Rāzī) further reduces this to four in his work al-Maʿālim, by dropping the last two paths because they are composed of earlier ones. The passage thus shows a systematic classification of arguments for God's existence.

Key Terms
منشأ
الإمكان
الحدوث
الطرق الموصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only one method (like ḥudūth) is valid for proving God's existence.
Correction: The text explicitly states that all four bases are valid paths, and all lead to knowledge of the Creator.

Checkpoint
How many fundamental bases for the need of a hadith for a Creator are mentioned?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The need of the contingent for a Creator can be argued from multiple valid bases, and these bases can be systematically categorized to produce multiple inference paths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen that theologians have laid out multiple systematic paths to knowledge of the Creator. If you were to die today, which of these paths would you say you have truly internalized as your own conviction—not just memorized? What is one concrete step you will take this week to deeply understand one of these paths as a living certainty?

Or have you built your faith on multiple foundations? Pause and ask yourself: which of the four bases (imkan, huduth, both, or the combined) do you actually feel resonates with your heart? Why?

The Pure Imkan Argument: Distinction and Proof
Sanusi distinguishes the pure imkan argument and presents its logical proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the pure imkan argument differs from other arguments for God's existence and how it logically proves a Necessary Being directly from the possibility of the world.

Core Idea
The pure imkan argument proves God without needing to first prove the world's creation.

Sanusi presents a distinctive argument for God's existence that does not rely on the world's temporal origination (huduth). Instead, it directly analyzes the concept of possibility: anything that can exist or not exist must receive existence from something else, ultimately a Necessary Being. This lesson explains the logic and contrasts it with other approaches.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the contrast Sanusi draws at the beginning.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice how Sanusi distinguishes pure imkan from other ways by the order of knowing the world's origination.
In pure imkan, knowledge of origination comes after knowledge of the Maker.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the steps: possibility → need → existence from another → Necessity, and understand why circularity and infinite regress are impossible.
Each step is justified by the previous one.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that this argument establishes a Necessary Being without assuming the world's creation as a premise.
This contrasts with arguments that begin with creation's temporal beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرق بين الاستدلال بطريق الإمكان المجرد وبين غيره من الطرق: أن العلم بحدوث العالم يتأخر في طريق الإمكان المجرد عن العلم بالصانع، وفي غيره يتقدم. وبيانه: أنا إذا حققنا أن العالم ممكن بذاته، ويدل على ذلك افتقاره، وأن كل ممكن بذاته من حيث هو هو قابل للوجود والعدم، فالوجود له ليس من ذاته، وكل ما ليس له الوجود من ذاته فالوجود له من غيره، ثم ذلك الغير لا بد وأن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته، وإلا افتقر إلى ما افتقر إليه العالم، ودار أو تسلسل على ما يأتي إن شاء الله بيانه في دليل قدم الصانع، والدور والتسلسل محالان، فثبت العلم بوجود مؤثر واجب لذاته.
And the difference between inference by the way of pure possibility (al-imkān al-mujarrad) and other ways is: that knowledge of the world's origination is posterior in the way of pure possibility to knowledge of the Maker, while in other ways it is prior. And its explanation: that if we verify that the world is possible in itself, and its need indicates that, and that every possible in itself, inasmuch as it is, is receptive to existence and non-existence, then existence for it is not from itself, and everything for which existence is not from itself, existence for it is from another; then that other must necessarily be Necessary of Existence in itself (wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihī), otherwise it would need what the world needed, leading to circularity or infinite regress, as will be explained, God willing, in the proof of the eternity of the Maker; and circularity and infinite regress are impossible; thus knowledge of the existence of an efficient cause that is Necessary in itself is established.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage first distinguishes the pure imkān argument from other arguments. In pure imkān, knowledge of the world's origination (ḥudūth) comes after (i.e., is derived from) knowledge of the Creator, whereas in other arguments it precedes. Then the passage presents the pure imkān proof: the world is possible in itself (mumkin bi-dhātihī), as shown by its need (iftiqār). Every possible being, considered in itself, is equally receptive to existence and non-existence; existence is not from its own essence. Therefore, its existence must come from another. That other must be Necessary of Existence in itself (wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihī); otherwise, if it too were possible, it would need another, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both of which are impossible. Thus we have established knowledge of a necessary efficient cause. The proof is structured as a clear logical deduction.

Key Terms
الإمكان المجرد
الحدوث
واجب الوجود
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The pure imkan argument needs prior proof that the world is created.
Correction: Actually, it directly reasons from the possibility of the world without needing to prove its temporal creation.

Checkpoint
In the pure imkān argument, what is the sequential relationship between knowledge of the world's origination and knowledge of the Creator?
A) Knowledge of origination precedes knowledge of the Creator
B) Knowledge of the Creator precedes knowledge of origination
C) They are simultaneous
D) No relationship exists

Key Takeaway
The pure imkan argument establishes the existence of a Necessary Being directly from the possibility of the world, without requiring proof of the world's temporal origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the pure imkan argument shows that your very existence is from another, how does that affect your sense of independence? Do you live as if you are self-sufficient, or do you constantly acknowledge your need for God? What would change if you truly felt your contingency every moment?
?
The argument says that circularity and infinite regress are impossible. Yet in our lives, we often fall into patterns of seeking meaning or satisfaction in things that are themselves contingent. What is one area of your life where you are trapped in a 'circular' pursuit that never leads to the Necessary Being? What step can you take today to break that cycle?
?
Sanusi says knowledge of the world's origination is posterior to knowledge of the Maker in this argument. This means you can know God without first proving the universe began. Have you ever felt that your faith depends on certain scientific or historical proofs? How does this argument free you to know God directly from the reality of your own need? What changes if you start from your own contingency rather than from external arguments?

Limitation of the Imkan Proof: Possibility of Eternity
Sanusi notes a limitation of the imkān proof: it only proves a necessary being, not an originated world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the imkān argument establishes a necessary being but does not necessarily prove that the world is originated in time — it leaves open the possibility that the world is eternal as a necessary concomitant of God.

Core Idea
The imkān argument proves a Maker, but not necessarily an originated world.

Sanusi has just presented the imkān argument for the existence of God. But now he pauses to note a critical limitation: the argument only shows that a necessary being must exist, not that the world is temporally originated. There remains the possibility that the world is eternal as a necessary concomitant of God's essence. This subtle point requires careful attention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice that the passage ends mid-sentence, indicating the argument continues.
2
Identify the two parts of the conclusion
First, the proof gives knowledge of the Maker. Second, there is a possibility that the Maker is a creator by essential concomitance, meaning the world could be eternal.
Essential concomitance (al-luzūm al-dhātī) means a necessary relationship that is inseparable from the essence.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why is this limitation significant? It shows that more argumentation is needed to prove that the world is originated, not just that a necessary being exists.
This sets up the need for additional proofs, such as the argument from accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد خرج لك من هذا: العلم بالصانع، لكن مع احتمال أن يكون صانعاً باللزوم الذاتي، فلا يكون العالم حادثاً، بل قديماً كما
So from this you have attained: knowledge of the Maker, but with the possibility that the Maker is [so] by essential concomitance (al-luzūm al-dhātī), so the world would not be originated, but eternal, as...
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage points out a limitation of the pure imkān argument. It states that from the previous proof we have attained knowledge of the Maker, but with the possibility that the Maker is a creator by essential concomitance (al-luzūm al-dhātī). That is, the proof only demonstrates a necessary being, but does not necessarily prove that the world is originated (ḥādith); it could be that the world is eternal as a necessary concomitant of God's essence. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating that further discussion follows on the next page. This serves as a critical note: the imkān argument alone is insufficient to prove the world's temporality.

Key Terms
اللزوم الذاتي
العالم قديماً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the imkān argument alone suffices to prove the world's origination.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the argument only proves a necessary being; it leaves open the possibility that the world is eternal as a necessary concomitant of God.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi identify as a possible interpretation of the pure imkān argument?
A) That the Creator is eternal
B) That the world is originated
C) That the world could be eternal as a necessary concomitant of God
D) That the argument is invalid

Key Takeaway
The imkān argument gives knowledge of a necessary being, but does not prove the world is originated in time — it leaves room for the world to be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the possibility that the imkān argument does not prove the world is originated in time, does that shake your confidence in the proof? Or does it drive you to seek a more complete understanding of God's relationship to the world?
?
If the world were eternal, coexisting with God as a necessary consequence of His essence, how would that challenge your sense of God's will and choice? Can you honestly say that you would still feel the same awe and gratitude toward a Creator who had no choice but to create?
?
Sanusi shows that even a sound argument has limits. In your own faith, are there areas where you have accepted a partial proof as complete, perhaps out of laziness or comfort? What would it cost you to dig deeper and confront those gaps?

The Three Modes of Causation and the Need for a Second Proof
Sanusi enumerates the three modes of causation and the confinement reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three modes of causation and why the existence of a Maker does not automatically prove the creation of the world.

Core Idea
The existence of a Maker is not enough; we must determine if He acts by necessity or by choice.

After establishing the existence of a necessary being, there remains a crucial question: is this being a necessary cause (making the world eternal) or a voluntary agent (making the world created)? Sanusi now explains that we need a second proof to decide this, and he begins by enumerating the only three possible modes of causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage context
Read the Arabic passage once, noting that Sanusi reminds us that the existence of a Maker is already proven, but the nature of His causation is still open.
The philosophers agree on existence but differ on causality.
2
Identify the three modes
Find the three modes: cause (essential necessitation), nature (conditional necessitation), and voluntary choice. Notice how each is defined.
The confinement proof: every agent either can refrain or not; if not, it requires conditions or not.
3
Reflect on the need for a second proof
Ask yourself why the mere existence of a Maker doesn't guarantee creation. This sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ صفة الفاعل الحق ]
فقد خرج لكَ مِنْ هذا : العلم بالصانع ، لكن مع احتمالِ أنْ يكونَ صانعاً باللُّزوم الذاتي ، فلا يكون العالم حادثاً ، بل قديماً كمـا تقول الفلاسفةُ ، واحتمالِ أنْ يكونَ صانعاً بالاختيار ، فيكون العالم حادثاً ، فيحتاج إلى دليل آخر لإثباتِ هذا المطلب - أعني : مطلب حدوث العالم - بعدَما فرغتَ مِنْ مطلب وجودِ الصانع الذي نظرُكَ فيهِ ونظرُ الفيلسوفيّ واحدٌ ، وإنَّما تنفرد عنه بهذا المطلب الثاني ، فإنَّه لم يهتد هو إليه ، فتقولُ :
صانع العالم : إمَّا أنْ يكونَ أوجبَهُ لذاتِهِ ، أو اقتضاه بطبعه ، أو أوجده باختياره ، وجهات التأثيرِ منحصرة في هذه الأوجه الثلاثة ، ووجه الحصرِ : أَنَّ كلَّ مُؤثر لا يخلو : إما أن يصح منه التركُ أو لا ، والأوَّلُ : الفاعلُ المختارُ ، والثاني : إمَّا أَنْ يَتوقَّفَ اقتضاؤُهُ على شرط وانتفاء مانع أو لا ، والأوّل : الطبيعةُ ، والثاني : العِلَّةُ
ثم تقولُ : لا جائزَ أنْ يكونَ المؤثر في هذه الممكناتِ مُوجِباً لها بذاتِهِ كالعِلَّةِ ، ولا مقتضياً لها بطبعه ؛ لأنَّ ما يُؤثر كذلكَ لا يجوز أَنْ يُخَصِّصَ مِثْلاً عن مِثْلِ ؛ لاستحالة الاختلاف في معلولِ العِلَّة الواحدة ، ومطبوع الطبيعة الواحدة ، وفاعلُ العَالَمِ قد خَصَّصَ مِثْلاً عن مِثْلِ ، فتعيَّنَ أَنْ يكونَ مُوجِداً بالاختيار .
فتقولُ حيثَذٍ : العالَمُ مُوقَعٌ بالاختيار ، وكلُّ مُوقع بالاختيار حادث ؛ إذ اختيار وجودِهِ يستلزم سبق عدمِهِ ، وإلا كان تحصيل الحاصل في الوجودِ ، وثبوت تمكن ممَّا لا يصح كونه في العدم (۱) فينتج : العالم حادث .
[The Attribute of the True Agent]
Thus from this you have attained: knowledge of the Maker, but with the possibility that the Maker is by essential necessitation, so the world is not created but eternal, as the philosophers say; and the possibility that the Maker is by choice, so the world is created. So you need another proof to establish this claim—namely, the claim of the world's creation—after you have finished with the claim of the existence of the Maker, in which your view and the philosopher's view are the same. You are only distinguished from him by this second claim, for he did not arrive at it. So you say:
The Maker of the world: either He necessitates it by His essence, or He necessitates it by His nature, or He brings it into existence by His choice. The modes of causation are confined to these three aspects. The reason for the confinement: every agent either can refrain or cannot. The first is the voluntary agent; the second either its necessitation depends on a condition and absence of an impediment or not. The first is nature, the second is the cause.
Then you say: It is not possible that the agent in these contingent things is a necessitator by His essence like a cause, nor a necessitator by His nature; because that which acts in such a manner cannot differentiate between similar things, since difference in the effect of a single cause and the impression of a single nature is impossible. Yet the Agent of the world has differentiated between similar things. So it is determined that He is a bringer-into-existence by choice.
Then you say thereupon: The world is brought about by choice, and everything brought about by choice is created, because the choice of its existence necessitates the prior non-existence of it, otherwise it would be the acquisition of what already exists, and the establishment of ability for what cannot be in non-existence (1). Thus it follows: the world is created.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage continues from the previous proof. After establishing the existence of a necessary being, there remains a possibility that this being is a necessary cause (as philosophers claim, making the world eternal) or a voluntary agent (making the world created). So we need a second proof to demonstrate that the world is created. This proof examines the modes of causation. Every agent either can refrain (voluntary) or cannot refrain. If it cannot refrain, it either depends on conditions (nature) or not (cause). The three modes are: cause (necessary by essence), nature (necessary by nature), and voluntary choice. The argument then shows that the world contains specific differentiations (like distinguishing similar things). A necessary cause or nature cannot produce such differentiations because their effects are uniform. Since the world has specific differences, its agent must be voluntary. Then, if the world is produced by choice, it must be created (hadith), because choice of existence implies prior non-existence; otherwise it would be making what already exists, which is absurd. Therefore the world is created.

Key Terms
فاعل مختار
علة
طبيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that proving a necessary being automatically proves the world is created.
Correction: A necessary being could be a necessary cause, which would make the world eternal, not created. A separate argument is needed to show the Maker is voluntary.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, how many modes of causation are there?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The three modes of causation are cause (essential necessitation), nature (conditional necessitation), and voluntary choice; every agent must fall into one of these.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers affirm a necessary being but deny that He acts by choice. In your own heart, do you truly believe that Allah could have refrained from creating you? If He had, would you still owe Him gratitude? How does this affect the way you live today?
?
Sanusi says that your view and the philosopher's view are the same on the existence of the Maker. What distinguishes you is the second claim. How sure are you that you actually live as if the world is created by a voluntary Agent, not a blind force? Examine your prayers, your trust, and your fear of Him.
?
The confinement argument shows that every agent either can refrain or cannot. In your own life, when you choose to obey or disobey Allah, do you recognize that your ability to choose is a gift from the voluntary Creator? Does that make you more grateful and more careful with your choices?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
6 min
The Argument for the Voluntary Agent and the Creation of the World
Sanusi eliminates cause and nature, establishes voluntary agent, and concludes the world is created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maker of the world must be voluntary and how that leads to the conclusion that the world is created.

Core Idea
Only a voluntary agent can account for the specific differentiations in the world.

Now that we know the three possible modes of causation, Sanusi eliminates the first two—cause and nature—because they cannot produce the specific differences we observe. This forces the conclusion that the Maker acts by choice, and that implies the world is created.

Guided Path
1
Understand the elimination argument
Read the passage and note why a cause or nature cannot produce specific differentiations between similar things. Their effects are uniform.
A necessary cause always produces the same effect; nature is similarly uniform when conditions are met.
2
Connect to the world's differentiation
Observe that the world contains specific differences (e.g., one thing given existence, another not). This cannot come from a uniform cause.
This is the core empirical evidence pointing to a voluntary agent.
3
Derive the creation of the world
If the agent is voluntary, then the world's existence was chosen, implying it was preceded by non-existence. That is the definition of creation (hadith).
Sanusi argues that choice necessarily implies prior non-existence; otherwise it would be 'acquisition of what already exists'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ صفة الفاعل الحق ]
فقد خرج لكَ مِنْ هذا : العلم بالصانع ، لكن مع احتمالِ أنْ يكونَ صانعاً باللُّزوم الذاتي ، فلا يكون العالم حادثاً ، بل قديماً كمـا تقول الفلاسفةُ ، واحتمالِ أنْ يكونَ صانعاً بالاختيار ، فيكون العالم حادثاً ، فيحتاج إلى دليل آخر لإثباتِ هذا المطلب - أعني : مطلب حدوث العالم - بعدَما فرغتَ مِنْ مطلب وجودِ الصانع الذي نظرُكَ فيهِ ونظرُ الفيلسوفيّ واحدٌ ، وإنَّما تنفرد عنه بهذا المطلب الثاني ، فإنَّه لم يهتد هو إليه ، فتقولُ :
صانع العالم : إمَّا أنْ يكونَ أوجبَهُ لذاتِهِ ، أو اقتضاه بطبعه ، أو أوجده باختياره ، وجهات التأثيرِ منحصرة في هذه الأوجه الثلاثة ، ووجه الحصرِ : أَنَّ كلَّ مُؤثر لا يخلو : إما أن يصح منه التركُ أو لا ، والأوَّلُ : الفاعلُ المختارُ ، والثاني : إمَّا أَنْ يَتوقَّفَ اقتضاؤُهُ على شرط وانتفاء مانع أو لا ، والأوّل : الطبيعةُ ، والثاني : العِلَّةُ
ثم تقولُ : لا جائزَ أنْ يكونَ المؤثر في هذه الممكناتِ مُوجِباً لها بذاتِهِ كالعِلَّةِ ، ولا مقتضياً لها بطبعه ؛ لأنَّ ما يُؤثر كذلكَ لا يجوز أَنْ يُخَصِّصَ مِثْلاً عن مِثْلِ ؛ لاستحالة الاختلاف في معلولِ العِلَّة الواحدة ، ومطبوع الطبيعة الواحدة ، وفاعلُ العَالَمِ قد خَصَّصَ مِثْلاً عن مِثْلِ ، فتعيَّنَ أَنْ يكونَ مُوجِداً بالاختيار .
فتقولُ حيثَذٍ : العالَمُ مُوقَعٌ بالاختيار ، وكلُّ مُوقع بالاختيار حادث ؛ إذ اختيار وجودِهِ يستلزم سبق عدمِهِ ، وإلا كان تحصيل الحاصل في الوجودِ ، وثبوت تمكن ممَّا لا يصح كونه في العدم (۱) فينتج : العالم حادث .
[The Attribute of the True Agent]
Thus from this you have attained: knowledge of the Maker, but with the possibility that the Maker is by essential necessitation, so the world is not created but eternal, as the philosophers say; and the possibility that the Maker is by choice, so the world is created. So you need another proof to establish this claim—namely, the claim of the world's creation—after you have finished with the claim of the existence of the Maker, in which your view and the philosopher's view are the same. You are only distinguished from him by this second claim, for he did not arrive at it. So you say:
The Maker of the world: either He necessitates it by His essence, or He necessitates it by His nature, or He brings it into existence by His choice. The modes of causation are confined to these three aspects. The reason for the confinement: every agent either can refrain or cannot. The first is the voluntary agent; the second either its necessitation depends on a condition and absence of an impediment or not. The first is nature, the second is the cause.

Misconception: Some think that the universe's order could be produced by a necessary cause or nature.
Correction: A necessary cause or nature cannot pick and choose; it would produce uniform results. The specific differentiations in the universe require a voluntary agent.

Checkpoint
Why can a necessary cause or nature not produce specific differentiations?
A) Because they are not powerful enough.
B) Because their effects are uniform and cannot differ.
C) Because they only act in the presence of matter.
D) Because they are not intelligent.

Key Takeaway
Because the world exhibits specific differentiations, its Maker cannot be a necessary cause or nature; He must be voluntary, and thus the world is created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim that Allah is the Voluntary Agent, but when life gives you unexpected difficulties, do you immediately attribute them to natural causes or human injustice instead of His deliberate choice? How can you train your heart to see His hand in the specific details of your life?
?
Sanusi proves that the world began after non-existence. Do you truly live as if you were created from nothing? Or do you sometimes act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change in your morning routine if you deeply internalized that your very existence was chosen by Allah?
?
The passage says that if the world were eternal, the choice of its existence would be 'acquisition of what already exists'. In what areas of your life do you assume things 'just are' instead of recognizing that they could have been otherwise and were specifically chosen by Allah? How can you increase your gratitude?

The Two Claims in the Argument for Ease of Creating Another
Commentary on Sanusi's explanation of the two claims and the proof that self-creation is contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two claims in the argument that creating another is easier than creating oneself, and the proof that self-creation involves a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Self-creation is impossible because it would require you to be both before and after yourself.

Sanusi's argument that creating another is easier than creating oneself may seem like a comparison of difficulty, but it is actually a logical proof. The necessary relation (ملازمة) between possibility and ease rests on two claims. This passage unpacks both claims and explains why self-creation is impossible, making creation of another the only feasible option.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read the passage orientation and short intro to grasp the purpose of this commentary. The argument is about logical ease, not comparative difficulty.
The term 'ملازمة' is key; it means a necessary logical relation.
2
Identify the two claims
The text explicitly states two claims. Find them: (1) whoever can create himself can create another, (2) creating another is easier than creating oneself.
3
Examine the proof for the first claim
The first claim is proved by equality in possibility: if another is equal to you in possibility, then the ability transfers.
This is a straightforward inference from the definition of possibility.
4
Examine the proof for the second claim
The second claim is proved by showing that self-creation requires combining two contradictory conditions: the agent must precede its act and also be the act itself, which is impossible.
The Arabic word 'متاهافت' means collapsing or contradictory, like moths falling into fire.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلى بيانِ الملازمة أشار بقولِهِ : لمساواتِهِ لكَ في الإمكان أي : لمساواة غيرِكَ لكَ في الإمكانِ وأمَّا بطلان التالي - وهو أنَّ إيجاد الإنسانِ ذاتَ غيره ممتنع - فلا يفتقر إلى بيانٍ ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ عاقل يدركُ مِنْ نفسِهِ العجز عن ذلك قولُهُ : وإنَّما قلنا : إِنَّهُ أهونُ عليكَ لمَّا اشتملَتِ الملازمة على دعويين : إحداهما : أَنَّ مَنْ أمكنَ أنْ يُوجِدَ نفسَهُ أمكنَ أَنْ يُوجِدَ غيرَهُ ، الثانيةُ : أَنَّ إيجادَهُ غيرَهُ أهون عليهِ مِنْ إيجادِهِ نفسَه .. احتاجَ إلى الاستدلال عليهما ، فاستدل على الأولى بقوله : لمساواتِهِ لكَ في الإمكان ، واحتج هنا على الثانية، فبيَّنَ أَنَّ وَجْهَ الأهونيَّةِ في إيجادِ سلامتهُ مِنْ محالٍ يختص بإيجادِهِ نفسَهُ ؛ وهو الجمعُ بينَ أمرينِ متنافيين مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ يجب أن يتقدَّمَ على نفسه ؛ لكونه فاعلاً لها ، والفاعلُ قبلَ فعلِهِ ضرورةً ، ويجب تأخُرُهُ لكونه عينَ فعلِهِ ، وهو قول متهافت ؛ أي: متساقط ، ومنه : تهافت الفراش في النارِ ؛ أي : تساقط
And to the explanation of the necessary relation he [the author] pointed by his statement: 'for its equality to you in possibility', meaning the equality of another to you in possibility. And as for the falsity of the consequent—which is that a human's bringing into existence the essence of another is impossible—it does not need explanation, because every rational person perceives from himself inability for that. His statement: 'And we only said that it is easier for you, because the necessary relation contained two claims: one of them: that whoever can bring his own essence into existence can bring into existence another; the second: that his bringing another into existence is easier for him than his bringing his own self into existence... He needed proof for them both, so he proved the first by his statement: 'for its equality to you in possibility', and he argued here for the second, so he explained that the aspect of being easier in bringing into existence his safety from the impossible that is specific to bringing his own self into existence: it is the combination of two mutually contradictory matters, in that he must precede himself, because he is an agent for it, and the agent is before its act necessarily, and he must be after it because he is the very act itself, and this is a contradictory statement; i.e., collapsing, and from it: the moth's falling into the fire; i.e., falling.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on Sanusi's text explaining the logical relation (ملازمة) in the argument that creating another is easier than creating oneself. The commentator first clarifies the author's statement about equality in possibility: it means that another thing is equal to you in possibility, so if you can create yourself, you can also create another. The falsity of the consequent—that creating another is impossible—is self-evident. Then the commentator quotes the author's next statement, which splits the necessary relation into two claims: (1) whoever can create himself can create another, and (2) creating another is easier than creating oneself. The author already proved the first claim by the equality in possibility. For the second claim, he argues that the 'ease' comes from avoiding a specific impossibility: if you were to create yourself, you would have to combine two contradictory conditions—you must precede yourself as agent (since the agent is before its act) and you must be subsequent to yourself as the very act (since you are the act itself). This contradiction is impossible, so creating oneself is impossible, while creating another does not involve this contradiction, hence it is easier. The word 'متاهافت' (contradictory) is glossed as 'collapsing', like moths falling into fire.

Key Terms
ملازمة
إمكان
أهون
فاعل
فعل
متنافيين
متاهافت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that creating another is relatively easier than creating oneself, as if comparing two possible acts.
Correction: The 'ease' is not about relative difficulty but about logical possibility: the argument proves that self-creation is impossible due to contradiction, while creating another is possible. 'Easier' means 'free from impossibility'.

Checkpoint
What is the second claim in the necessary relation discussed?
A) That whoever can create himself can create another.
B) That creating another is easier than creating oneself.
C) That creating oneself is impossible.
D) That the creator must be prior to his act.

Key Takeaway
The argument that creating another is easier than creating oneself is a logical proof: self-creation is impossible because it requires the agent to be both before and after its own act, while creating another avoids this contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that self-creation is impossible because it requires you to be both before and after yourself. Where in your daily life do you act as if you are the origin of your own existence, forgetting that you are entirely dependent on the Creator?
?
If you cannot even bring yourself into existence, how can you justify living as if your life is your own to do with as you please? What specific idol of self-sufficiency must you smash today?
?
The passage proves that only a being free from contradiction can create. Has the impossibility of self-creation truly humbled you, or have you merely intellectually assented without letting it transform how you relate to Allah? Repent now and ask Him to make you utterly reliant on Him alone.

Allah's Existence
