Objection and Answer on Prior Non-Existence
Objection and two-part answer on knowing one's prior non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that we only know change of forms is answered by showing that the self is a composite whole, whose non-existence is necessary before its parts.

Core Idea
Your own body's increase and composite structure prove you were non-existent.

Sanusi now defends the minor premise 'I was not, then I was' against a clever objection. The objector argues that we only know transformation of forms, not absolute non-existence, because our matter seems to stretch back infinitely. The answer draws on the nature of the self as a composite whole (كل مجموعي) and a composite quiddity (ماهية مركبة), showing that the whole was non-existent before its parts came to be.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector says: 'How do I know necessarily my prior non-existence, when I was a sperm in my father's loins, and so on? I only know transformation of forms.' Understand the force of this objection: it challenges the self-evidence of the minor premise.
Notice that the objector does not deny change, but denies that his essence itself was non-existent.
2
Identify the two answers given
The text gives a first answer based on the increase of your current self over the sperm, then a second objection and answer focused on the composite nature of the essence. Trace each part.
The second answer is incomplete in the text but introduces 'composite whole'.
3
Understand 'composite whole' (كل مجموعي)
The essence is like a whole made of parts. If any part was non-existent, the whole was non-existent. Since the parts (like the sperm) clearly came into existence, the whole (you) was non-existent before.
This concept is key to rebutting the infinite regress of matter.
4
Relate this to proving a Creator
If your self was non-existent then existed, it must have a cause. That cause is the Creator. The proof is complete with just the increase or the composite notion.

تحقيق معنى ( لم يكن ثمَّ كانَ ) فإِنْ قلت : كيف أعلمُ ضرورة سبق عدمي وقد كنتُ ماءً في صلب أبي ، وكذا أبي في صلب أبيه وهلم جراً ؟! غايةً الأمرِ : أَنِّي أعلمُ ضرورة تحوُّلي مِنْ صورة إلى صورة ، لا مِنْ عدم إلى وجود كما ذكرت . فالجواب : أنَّ ذاتَكَ الآنَ أكبرُ مِنَ النطفة التي عنها قطعاً، فتعلم على الضرورةِ أنَّ ما زادَ كانَ معدوماً ثم كانَ ، وإذا كانَ معدوماً ثم وُجِدَ فلا بدَّ لَهُ مِنْ مُوجِدٍ ، فقد تمَّ لكَ البرهانُ القاطعُ بهذا الزائدِ مِنْ ذاتِكَ على وجودِ الصانع دون حاجة إلى غيرِهِ هذا اعتراض على المقدّمةِ الصغرى القائلة : أنا لم أكن ثم كنتُ ، وتقريرُهُ أنْ يُقالَ : لا تُسلَّمُ أني لم أكن ثم كنتُ ، قولكم إنَّ ذلك معلوم بالضرورة ممنوع ، وسند المنع : أنّي أعلمُ أنَّ مادَّتي التي تَكوَّنْتُ منها كانَتْ في صلب أبي ، وكذا مادَّةُ أبي التي تكوَّنَ منها كانَتْ في صلب أبيه ، ولعلَّ الأمر كان هكذا إلى غير نهاية ، وإذا لاحَ الاحتمال سقط الاستدلال ، غاية الأمرِ : أَنِّي أعلمُ ضرورة تبدُّلِ الصورِ عليَّ ، لا سبقَ العدم لذاتي ، ودليلكم مبني على أنَّ نفس الذاتِ لم تكن ثم كانَتْ ، لا على أنَّ صورتها لم تكن ثم كانت أجابَ بما حاصلُهُ : أَنَّ الذاتَ مِنْ بابِ الكلِّ المجموعي والماهية المركَّبةِ ، ومِنْ لازمها انعدامها بانعدام جزئها ، ومِنَ المعلومِ
Investigation of the meaning of 'it was not, then it was'. For if you say: How do I know necessarily the precedence of my non-existence, when I was water in the loins of my father, and so my father in the loins of his father, and so on? The utmost is that I know necessarily my transformation from one form to another, not from non-existence to existence as you mentioned. The answer: That your self now is greater than the sperm from which it certainly came, so you know necessarily that what increased was non-existent then it was, and if it was non-existent then existed, it must have a cause, so the decisive proof is complete for you by this increase from your self for the existence of the Maker without need for other than it. This is an objection to the minor premise stating: 'I was not, then I was', and its formulation is to say: The claim that 'I was not, then I was' is not conceded. Your statement that this is known necessarily is denied. The basis of the denial: I know that my matter from which I was formed was in the loins of my father, and likewise my father's matter from which he was formed was in the loins of his father, and perhaps the matter was thus without end. When the probability appears, the inference collapses. The utmost is that I know necessarily the succession of forms upon me, not the priority of non-existence for my essence. And your proof is built on that the very essence was not then was, not that its form was not then was. He answered with the gist of it: That the essence is of the category of the composite whole and the composite quiddity, and it is a necessary concomitant of it that it is annihilated by the annihilation of its part, and it is known...
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer regarding the minor premise 'I was not, then I was' used in the cosmological argument. The objector argues that he does not know his own prior non-existence necessarily, because he was once a sperm in his father's loins, and so on back, so he only knows transformation of forms, not absolute non-existence. The first answer is that even if you were a sperm, your current self is larger than that sperm, and what increased was clearly non-existent before, so that increase proves a creator. Then a second objection is raised: the objector specifically denies the minor premise, claiming that his matter seems to stretch back infinitely, so he only knows change of forms, not that his essence was non-existent. The answer (cut off) says that the essence is a composite whole (كل مجموعي) and a composite quiddity (ماهية مركبة), and it is necessarily annihilated by the annihilation of its part. This means that if the parts come into existence successively, the composite whole as such was not before its parts, so the essence itself originated. The answer is incomplete in the text.

انعدام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because our matter seems to have always existed in some form (sperm line), we cannot claim that 'I was not, then I was' – we only know change of forms.
Correction: The self is a composite whole. Even if its matter traces back, the composite self as a unified entity was non-existent before its parts came together. The increase over the sperm and the composite nature both prove prior non-existence.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the objector's denial that 'I was not, then I was' is known necessarily?
A) He knows he was always in the form of a sperm.
B) He knows his matter was in his father's loins and so back, allowing infinite regress.
C) He knows only the succession of forms, not the non-existence of his essence.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The objection that we only know form–change fails because the self is a composite whose parts originate, so the whole was non-existent before, proving the minor premise and thus the existence of a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your existence is a self-evident fact, or do you truly confront the reality that you were nothing and everything you are came from outside you? What specific part of your body or your life today reminds you that you were once non-existent?
?
If every increase in your body—every cell, every limb—points to a Creator who brought it from non-existence, then what does that demand from your sense of ownership and pride? How can you actively attribute your achievements and growth to Him, not just in words but in your daily choices?
?
The objection suggests you might believe you merely changed forms, but the answer reveals you are a composite whose parts all originate. How does this truth expose any hidden belief you hold that you are self-sufficient or that your existence is necessary? What specific step will you take today to humble yourself before the One who made you from nothing?

Answer to the Objection on the Minor Premise
Sanusi refutes an objection against the premise 'I was not then I was' by analyzing the composite self.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the minor premise 'I did not exist then I existed' by showing that the self is a composite whole, so the non-existence of a part entails the non-existence of the whole.

Core Idea
The self is a composite whole; if a part began, the whole began, proving we need a Creator.

This lesson presents a key objection to a classic proof for God's existence and Sanusi's powerful rebuttal. The objection claims we only know forms change, not the essence. Sanusi responds by analyzing the self as a composite, showing that the increase over the sperm was non-existent, therefore 'I' was non-existent. This establishes the minor premise by necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection that denies the minor premise is known by necessity. Note the argument about matter in loins of fathers and infinite regress.
The objection claims only forms change.
2
Identify Sanusi's key concept
Sanusi introduces the concept of the self as a 'collective whole' (al-kull al-majmūʿī) and composite quiddity. Understand that the whole depends on its parts.
This is crucial for his answer.
3
Follow the logical consequence
Since the larger part (the increase over the sperm) was non-existent, the whole self was non-existent. Therefore, 'I was not then I was' is true by necessity.
4
Understand the conclusion
The proof for the Creator is completed: the increase in oneself necessitates a bringer-into-existence distinct from oneself.
This avoids the contradiction of self-creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نشأت فالجواب : أنَّ ذاتَكَ الآنَ أكبرَ مِنَ النطفة التي عنها قطعاً، فتعلم على الضرورةِ أنَّ ما زادَ كانَ معدوماً ثم كانَ ، وإذا كانَ معدوماً ثم وُجِدَ فلا بدَّ لَهُ مِنْ مُوجِدٍ ، فقد تمَّ لكَ البرهانُ القاطعُ بهذا الزائدِ مِنْ ذاتِكَ على وجودِ الصانع دون حاجة إلى غيرِهِ. هذا اعتراض على المقدّمةِ الصغرى القائلة : ( أنا لم أكن ثم كنتُ ) ، وتقريرُهُ أنْ يُقالَ : لا تُسلَّمُ ( أني لم أكن ثم كنتُ ) ، قولكم (١) : ( إنَّ ذلك معلوم بالضرورة ( ممنوع ، وسند المنع : أنّي أعلمُ أنَّ مادَّتي التي تَكوَّنْتُ منها كانَتْ في صلب أبي ، وكذا مادَّةُ أبي التي تكوَّنَ منها كانَتْ في صلب أبيه ، ولعلَّ الأمر كان هكذا إلى غير نهاية ، وإذا لاحَ الاحتمال سقط الاستدلال ، غاية الأمرِ : أَنِّي أعلمُ ضرورة تبدُّلِ الصورِ عليَّ ، لا سبقَ العدم لذاتي ، ودليلكم مبني على أنَّ نفس الذاتِ لم تكن ثم كانَتْ ، لا على أنَّ صورتها لم تكن ثم كانت. أجابَ بما حاصلُهُ : أَنَّ الذاتَ مِنْ بابِ الكلِّ المجموعي والماهية المركَّبةِ ، ومِنْ لازمها انعدامها بانعدام جزئها ، ومِنَ المعلومِ ضرورةً أنَّ جزاها الأكبر الزائد على النطفة لم يكن ثم كانَ ، فصدق قولنا في الصغرى : ( أنا لم أكن ثم كنتُ ) وأنَّ العلم بذلك ضروري ؛ إذ ( أنا ) ونحوُهُ مِنَ الكناياتِ عبارةٌ عنِ الهيكل المخصوص مِنْ روحٍ وبدنٍ لا عن بعضِهِ عندَ المحققين على ما تَقَرَّرَ في محلهِ ، وإذا ثبتَ أَنَّ جزءاً مِنْ ذاتي لم يكن ثم كانَ .. فذاتي لم تكن ثم كانَتْ ، فأحتاج إلى موجد لذاتي ، ويَتعيَّنُ أنْ يكونَ غيرها ؛ لئلا يلزم التهافت المذكور .
Then the answer: that your self now is larger than the sperm from which it came decisively, so you know by necessity that what increased was non-existent then existed, and when it was non-existent then existed, it must have a bringer-into-existence (mūjid), so the conclusive proof has been completed for you through this increase of your self for the existence of the Maker (al-Ṣāniʿ) without need for anything else. This is an objection to the minor premise which says: 'I did not exist then I existed.' Its exposition is that it is said: The statement 'I did not exist then I existed' is not conceded. Your saying: 'That is known by necessity' is denied, and the basis of the denial is: I know that my matter from which I was formed was in the loins of my father, and likewise the matter of my father from which he was formed was in the loins of his father, and perhaps the matter was like this ad infinitum. When possibility appears, the deduction falls. The utmost matter is: I know by necessity the change of forms upon me, not the priority of non-existence to my essence. Your proof is based on the fact that the essence itself did not exist then existed, not that its form did not exist then existed. He answered with the gist of it: that the essence is of the category of the collective whole (al-kull al-majmūʿī) and the composite quiddity (al-māhiyya al-murakkaba). It is a necessary consequence of it that its non-existence follows from the non-existence of its part. And it is known necessarily that its larger part, the increase over the sperm, did not exist then existed. So our statement in the minor premise: 'I did not exist then I existed' is true, and the knowledge of that is necessary, since 'I' and similar pronouns are expressions for the specific structure of spirit and body, not for some of it, according to the verifiers (al-muḥaqqiqīn), as established in its place. And when it is established that a part of my self did not exist then existed, then my self did not exist then existed, so I need a bringer-into-existence (mūjid) for my self, and it is determined that it be other than it, so as not to entail the mentioned contradiction.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the minor premise of a familiar proof for God's existence, followed by Sanusi's detailed answer. The objection denies that one's own non-existence is known by necessity, arguing that matter is eternal in the lineage of fathers, and only forms change. Sanusi responds by analyzing the self as a composite whole (al-kull al-majmūʿī). Since the increase upon the original sperm is a part that was non-existent and then existed, the whole self—which includes that part—was non-existent. Thus, 'I did not exist then I existed' is true by necessity, because 'I' refers to the entire composite of body and soul. This establishes the need for a Creator distinct from the self.

الماهية المركبة
موجد
الصانع
البرهان القاطع
المقدمة الصغرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof requires knowing the entire self was absolutely nothing before.
Correction: Sanusi shows it is enough to know that a part (the increase) was non-existent, because the whole (composite self) depends on its parts.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Sanusi's answer to the objection that only forms change, not the essence?
A) The essence is simple and unchanging.
B) The self is a composite whole, so if a part was non-existent, the whole was non-existent.
C) The objection is invalid because matter cannot be traced back infinitely.
D) The proof only works for bodies, not the self.

Key Takeaway
The self is a composite whole; the non-existence of any part entails the non-existence of the whole, so the minor premise 'I did not exist then I existed' is necessarily true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that your self is a composite of parts that did not exist before. Which part of your current existence—your body, your thoughts, your identity—can you honestly claim has always been there? How does knowing that every part of you came from non-existence change your view of who you are?
?
Sanusi says we know necessarily that our larger part did not exist. Think about your own body: every cell, every organ, every memory—was there a time it was not? If you are convinced that your entire self was once nothing, what does that demand from you towards the One who brought you into existence? What keeps you from acting on that demand right now?
?
The objector in this lesson tried to avoid the conclusion by appealing to an infinite chain of ancestors. In your life, where do you find yourself making excuses to avoid the clear implication that you are completely dependent on a Creator? What is one excuse you will abandon today?

The Objection of a Part Creating the Increase
Sanusi addresses and partially refutes an objection about a part creating the increase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that if a part of the self could create, then the whole self could create other things, which is impossible, thereby refuting the objection before a full proof is given.

Core Idea
If a part could create, the whole would also be creative, which is absurd.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a subtle objection: perhaps a part of the original self (like the sperm) was responsible for creating the additional parts. He acknowledges the logical possibility but quickly shows that this idea is already refuted by the same reasoning used against self-creation. The key move is that if any part had creative power, the whole self would have it too—a necessary impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal English translation. Notice how Sanusi sets up the objection before responding.
The passage ends mid-sentence with a counter-question; the answer is on the next page.
2
Identify the objection and Sanusi's strategy
Find where Sanusi states the objection: an alternative possibility that some part of the self caused the increase. Then see how he chooses to defer full refutation but provides a quick logical argument.
Sanusi's immediate argument is that if a part could invent, the self could invent other things, which is impossible.
3
Understand the logical principle
Grasp the principle: creative power cannot be limited to a part without implying the whole has it. This undermines the objection directly.
This argument is an extension of the earlier proof against self-creation.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Consider why someone might think Sanusi leaves the objection unanswered, but note that he actually provides a concise refutation here.
The checkpoint question tests this understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قصارى الأمرِ : تطرُّقُ احتمالِ أَنَّ بعض ذاتي في الأصل كالنطفة مثلاً أثَرَ في فعل البعض الزائدِ عليها ، لأنَّها مغايرة لمجموع ذاتي ، لكن سنذكر بعد هذا برهان بطلانِهِ ؛ لأنَّ الذِي قَصَدْنا أَنْ نَستَنتَجَ مِنَ البرهان السابقِ إِنَّما هو احتياج الذاتِ إلى مُوجِدٍ ، وأَمَّا تحقيق ذلكَ الموجد ما هو ، وتحقيق حدوثِ كلِّ جزءٍ مِنْ أجزاء الذاتِ بل وكلٌّ جزء مِنْ أجزاء العالم.. فسيتبيَّن بعد إنْ شاءَ اللهُ على الكمال ، على أَنَّ إسناد إيجادِ شيءٍ مِنَ الذاتِ لبعضها يندرج بطلانه فيما ذكرناهُ مِنَ البرهان على بطلان إيجاد الذاتِ نفسها ، وهو ما ألزمناه على ذلكَ التقديرِ مِنْ صحةِ إيجادِها غيرَها ؛ إذ لو كانَ لبعض الذاتِ خاصِّيَّةُ الاختراع للممكن لأمكن للذاتِ أن تخترعَ غيرها مِنْ حيثُ اشتمالها على ذلكَ البعض الذي يصح منه الاختراع، وهو باطل على الضرورة . فإن قيل : لعلَّ ذلكَ البعض إنَّما أثر بالطبع بشرطِ الاتصال والكينونة في الرحم
The upshot of the matter: The emergence of the possibility that some part of my self originally, like the sperm for example, had an effect in producing the additional part upon it, because it is different from the totality of my self. But we will mention after this a proof of its invalidity. Because what we intended to conclude from the previous proof is only the need of the self for a bringer-into-existence (mūjid). As for the investigation of what that bringer-into-existence is, and the investigation of the origination (ḥudūth) of every part of the parts of the self, indeed every part of the parts of the world, it will become clear later, God willing, completely. However, attributing the bringing-into-existence of something from the self to some of it is included in the invalidity of what we mentioned from the proof of the invalidity of the self's bringing itself into existence. And that is what we imposed on that estimation: the validity of its bringing into existence other than it. For if some part of the self had the property of inventing the possible, then it would be possible for the self to invent other than it by virtue of its inclusion of that part from which invention is valid, and that is invalid necessarily. For if it is said: Perhaps that part only acted by nature (bi-l-ṭabʿ) with the condition of connection and being in the womb...
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a further clarification and anticipation of a potential objection. Sanusi admits the logical possibility that a part of the self (e.g., the original sperm) might have produced the increase. However, he defers the full refutation to a later proof. He emphasizes that the previous proof only aimed to show that the self as a whole needs a creator, not to identify that creator or prove the origination of every part. Nonetheless, he points out that attributing creation to a part of the self is already refuted by the same reasoning used against self-creation: if a part could invent, then the whole self could invent other things, which is absurd. This argument undermines the objection before the detailed refutation is provided. The passage ends with a possible counter-question about natural action under conditions, which is presumably answered on the next page.

Correction: Sanusi actually provides a brief refutation here: if a part of the self had creative power, then the self as a whole would have it too, which is impossible. He only defers the full, detailed proof.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi argue would happen if a part of the self could invent (ikhtirāʿ) the possible?
A) The self would still need an external creator.
B) The self could then invent other things as well.

Key Takeaway
Even before giving a full proof, Sanusi shows that allowing creative power in a part of the self would absurdly grant it to the whole self.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever subconsciously attribute creative power to your own efforts, talents, or circumstances—treating them as if they could produce real change independently of Allah? How does Sanusi's argument expose that subtle shirk in daily life?
?
When you face a situation where a 'part' of your life seems responsible for an increase (wealth, health, success), do you instinctively trace that power back to Allah or to the means? What would it take for you to consistently see every part as utterly dependent on the Creator?
?
This passage refutes the possibility of any part of creation having inherent inventive power. If you truly internalize that, how would it change the way you rely on natural causes—like medicine for healing or strategy for success—in a way that neither negates nor deifies them?

Proof of the Self's Need for an External Originator
Sanusi completes the proof of need for an originator and addresses an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the self's origination requires an originator distinct from itself, and how Sanusi refutes the objection that a part of the self could cause the rest.

Core Idea
If part of your essence came to be, your whole essence needed an external originator.

This passage concludes the proof that the self cannot bring itself into existence. Sanusi shows that since part of the self (the increase over the sperm) was originated, the whole self must have an originator other than itself. He then anticipates an objection: could a part of the self have caused the increase? He refutes it briefly, pointing to a fuller refutation later.

Guided Path
1
Understand the minor premise
Sanusi asserts that knowledge that 'I was not then I was' is necessary. He explains that 'I' refers to the whole composite of spirit and body, not a part. Recognize that this is the minor premise of the argument.
The self is not just the sperm; it includes the subsequent increase.
2
Grasp the conclusion
From the minor premise, the conclusion follows: since a part of the self was originated, the whole self originated, requiring an originator that is not itself.
This avoids the logical incoherence (al-tahāfut) of self-creation.
3
Consider the objection and its refutation
Sanusi considers the possibility that a part (like sperm) could cause the additional part. He says this will be refuted later, but immediately gives a brief refutation: if a part could invent, the whole would be able to invent, which is false.
The refutation relies on the principle that if a part has a power, the whole containing that part has it.

ضرورةً أنَّ جزاها الأكبر الزائد على النطفة لم يكن ثم كانَ ، فصدق قولنا في الصغرى : ( أنا لم أكن ثم كنتُ ( وأنَّ العلم بذلك ضروري ؛ إذ ( أنا ) ونحوُهُ مِنَ الكناياتِ عبارةٌ عنِ الهيكل المخصوص مِنْ روحٍ وبدنٍ لا عن بعضِهِ عندَ المحققين على ما تَقَرَّرَ في محلهِ ، وإذا ثبتَ أَنَّ جزءاً مِنْ ذاتي لم يكن ثم كانَ .. فذاتي لم تكن ثم كانَتْ ، فأحتاج إلى موجد لذاتي ، ويَتعيَّنُ أنْ يكونَ غيرها ؛ لئلا يلزم التهافت المذكور . قصارى الأمرِ : تطرُّقُ احتمالِ أَنَّ بعض ذاتي في الأصل كالنطفة مثلاً أثَرَ في فعل البعض الزائدِ عليها ، لأنَّها مغايرة لمجموع ذاتي ، لكن سنذكر بعد هذا برهان بطلانِهِ ؛ لأنَّ الذِي قَصَدْنا أَنْ نَستَنتَجَ مِنَ البرهان السابقِ إِنَّما هو احتياج الذاتِ إلى مُوجِدٍ ، وأَمَّا تحقيق ذلكَ الموجد ما هو ، وتحقيق حدوثِ كلِّ جزءٍ مِنْ أجزاء الذاتِ بل وكلٌّ جزء مِنْ أجزاء العالم.. فسيتبيَّن بعد إنْ شاءَ اللهُ على الكمال ، على أَنَّ إسناد إيجادِ شيءٍ مِنَ الذاتِ لبعضها يندرج بطلانه فيما ذكرناهُ مِنَ البرهان على بطلان إيجاد الذاتِ نفسها ، وهو ما ألزمناه على ذلكَ التقديرِ مِنْ صحةِ إيجادِها غيرَها ؛ إذ لو كانَ لبعض الذاتِ خاصِّيَّةُ الاختراع للممكن لأمكن للذاتِ أن تخترعَ غيرها مِنْ حيثُ اشتمالها على ذلكَ البعض الذي يصح منه الاختراع، وهو باطل على الضرورة .
necessarily that its greater part, the increase over the sperm, was not then came to be, so our statement in the minor premise is true: (I was not then I was), and knowledge of that is necessary; for (I) and similar pronouns are expressions for the particular structure of spirit and body, not for part of it according to the investigators as established in its place. And if it is established that a part of my essence was not then came to be, then my essence was not then came to be, so I need an originator for my essence, and it must be determined that it is other than it; lest the mentioned incoherence follow. The upshot: the possibility that some part of my essence originally, like the sperm for example, had an effect in producing the additional part upon it, because it is different from the totality of my essence, but we will mention after this a proof of its falsehood; because what we intended to deduce from the preceding proof is only the need of the essence for an originator. As for the verification of what that originator is, and the verification of the temporal origination of every part of the parts of the essence, nay every part of the parts of the world, it will become clear later, God willing, perfectly. Moreover, attributing the bringing into existence of something from the essence to a part of it is included in the falsehood of what we mentioned from the proof of the falsehood of the essence itself bringing itself into existence, which is what we imposed on that supposition of the validity of its bringing into existence other than itself; for if some part of the essence had the property of inventing the possible, it would be possible for the essence to invent other than itself by virtue of its containing that part from which invention is valid, and that is false necessarily.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of a proof and an objection-answer. It begins by stating that we know necessarily that the greater part of our self, which is the increase over the sperm, did not exist and then came to be. This confirms the minor premise 'I was not then I was.' Knowledge of this is necessary because 'I' refers to the entire composite of spirit and body, not to a part. If a part of my essence was originated, then my entire essence was originated, thus I need an originator (mūjid) that is not myself to avoid logical incoherence (al-tahāfut). The passage then addresses a possible objection: could a part of my essence, such as the sperm, have caused the increase? The author says this possibility will be refuted later; the purpose of this proof is only to establish the need for an originator, not to verify its identity or prove the origination of every part. However, he immediately notes that attributing creation to a part of the self is already refuted by the same argument that refutes the self creating itself: if a part had the power of invention (ikhtirāʿ), then the whole self would have that power since it contains that part, but that is necessarily false.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument depends on the composition of the self into spirit and body.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the self is known as one thing; the composition is only used to explain that 'I' refers to the whole, not a part. The proof works even if the self is simple.

Checkpoint

B) Because if a part could invent, the whole could invent, which is false

Key Takeaway
The self's origination necessitates an originator distinct from itself; the possibility that a part caused the rest is refuted because it would imply the whole could invent itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The proof refutes the idea that any part of you could have caused another part. In what area of your life do you still act as if you are self-originating, creating your own success or progress without acknowledging the Creator's continuous bringing into existence?
?
If you truly believed that your very essence is contingent and that you need an external Originator every moment, how would that change your relationship with Allah—especially in moments of pride or self-sufficiency?

Refuting Natural Causation in the Origin of the Self
Sanusi refutes the objection of natural causation and concludes the agent is God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the claim that a part of the self could act by nature to produce the self, and why this leads to the conclusion that the agent must be God.

Core Idea
Nature cannot explain the variation in the self; only a free agent can.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a potential objection to the earlier proof that the self does not originate from itself or a part of itself. The objector suggests that perhaps a part of the self (like the sperm) acts by nature while connected to the womb. Sanusi refutes this by showing that natural causation would be uniform and cannot account for the observed variation and specification of the self. Thus, the agent must be a free chooser: God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening objection: 'Maybe that part acted by nature under condition of connection.' Note what the objector is claiming.
The objector is not saying the part creates the whole, but that it acts by nature under specific conditions.
2
First refutation: effect would cease
Sanusi argues that if it were by nature, its effect would stop after separation from the womb. But most of the self exists after separation. Consider why this is a problem.
If the cause is natural and conditional on connection, the effect should stop when the condition is removed.
3
Second refutation: variation contradicts nature
The self has differing parts and specifications that cannot come from a uniform nature. Nature produces uniform results; variation requires a free agent.
This is the stronger argument: nature cannot produce specific variations.
4
Conclusion: the agent is God
All possible things are equal before the free agent. So God is the originator, not the self or its parts.
This reinforces the earlier proof that the self's originator is external.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لعلَّ ذلكَ البعض إنَّما أثر بالطبع بشرطِ الاتصال والكينونة في الرحم
قلنا : فيلزمُ أنْ ينقطعَ تأثيرُهُ بعد الانفصال عن الرحم ، كيف ومعظم الذاتِ بعد الانفصالِ وُجِدَ ؟! على أنَّ اختلاف الذاتِ وتخصيص كل جزء منها بما يجوز على غيره يمنعُ قطعاً أنْ يكونَ لعلَّةٍ أو لطبيعة فيها تأثيرٌ ، فتعيَّنَ أنَّ التأثير فيها إنَّما هو بالاختيار والممكنات بالنسبة إلى الفاعل المختار سواء ، وهو الله تعالى فظهرَ أنَّ البرهان السابق يقتضي أنَّ الموجِدَ للذاتِ ليسَ نفسَها ولا جزءاً مِنْ أجزائها ، وستزيدُ ذلكَ بياناً بعدُ إِنْ شاءَ اللهُ تعالى .
For it is said: Perhaps that part only acted by nature under the condition of connection and being in the womb. We say: Then its effect would necessarily cease after separation from the womb, how [can that be] when most of the self after separation exists?! Moreover, the variation of the self and the specification of each part according to what is possible for other than it prevents absolutely that there be any effect from a cause or a nature. So it is determined that the effect upon them is only by choice, and the possible things are equal with respect to the free agent, and He is God Almighty. Thus it appears that the preceding proof necessitates that the originator of the self is neither itself nor a part of its parts, and we will increase its clarification later, God willing.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector suggests that the part of the self (e.g., the sperm) might have acted by nature (bi-l-ṭabʿ) under the condition of connection and being in the womb. The author's answer has two points: (1) If that were so, its effect would cease after separation from the womb, yet most of the self exists after separation. (2) The variation (ikhtilāf) of the self and the specification (takhṣīṣ) of each part with what is possible for others prevents any natural cause from having an effect—nature would produce uniform results. Therefore, the effect must be by choice (ikhtiyār), and all possible things are equal with respect to the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār), who is God. The conclusion is that the originator (mūjid) of the self is neither the self nor any part of it.

الاتصال
الكينونة في الرحم
الاختيار
الفاعل المختار
التخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A part of the self could act by nature to produce the rest of the self under specific conditions.
Correction: Nature produces uniform results; the self's variation and specification show it cannot be from nature.

Checkpoint

B) The self's variation and specificity refute uniform natural causation
C) The sperm cannot have any effect
D) The self is not composed

Key Takeaway
The variation and specificity of the self refute any claim of natural causation, necessitating a free agent—God—as the originator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Which explanation makes you feel more accountable for how you use them?

Sanusi says that nature cannot produce the variation we see in each self. Think about one of your own characteristics that you often dismiss as 'just the way I am.' ?
If the existence of every self is literally a choice of God—not a mechanical outcome of biology—then your very presence here is a deliberate act of divine will. What does that say about your purpose? And what response does that demand from you today, not just in belief, but in action?

The Contingency of Growth Proves Divine Choice
Sanusi demonstrates the proof of divine choice from the contingency of bodily growth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contingent nature of physical growth—its specific quantity and quality—points to the Maker's free choice, a foundational proof for divine volition in kalam.

Core Idea
Our growth being contingent proves the Giver chose it.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary develops a powerful argument for divine choice (ikhtiyār). Starting from the necessary knowledge that growth occurs from non-existence, Sanusi guides us to examine the increase itself: it occupies space and could be otherwise. This contingency forces the mind to acknowledge a Chooser. As you work through the lesson, pay attention to how the argument moves from observational fact to theological conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage ends mid-sentence due to a page break.
2
Identify the argument's structure
Break the argument into its logical steps: the necessary knowledge, the composite condition, the observation of contingency, and the conclusion about the Maker's choice.
3
Reflect on the proof's significance
Consider why this proof is important: it establishes that the Maker has free will and is not constrained by necessity. Think about how growth could have been different.
This connects to the broader kalam proof for divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فتعلم على الضرورةِ أنَّ ما زاد كان معدوماً ثم كانَ ) ذلك صدق ما ادَّعيناهُ مِنْ كونك تعلم ضرورةَ أَنَّكَ لم يعني : وبسبب تكن ثم كنتَ ؛ لأنَّ المركَّبَ لا وجود له إلا بجميع أجزائه • ثم إذا نظرت إلى هذا الزائدِ مِنْ ذاتِكَ وجدتَهُ جرماً يعمرُ فراغاً ، يجوز أنْ يكونَ على ما هو عليهِ مِنَ المقدارِ المخصوص والصفة المخصوصة ، وأن يكون على خلافهما ، فتعلمُ قطعاً أنَّ لصانعِكَ اختياراً في تخصيص ذاتِكَ ببعض ما جازَ عليها ، فيخرجُ لكَ مِنْ هذا البرهان القاطع على أنَّ النطفة التي نشأت عنها قطعاً يستحيلُ أنْ
His statement: (So you know necessarily that what increased was non-existent then existed) that is the truth of what we claimed, that you know necessarily that you did not mean: and because you were not then you were; because the composite has no existence except with all its parts. Then when you look at this increase from your self, you find it a body occupying a void, it is possible for it to be as it is of the specific quantity and specific quality, and to be contrary to them. So you know decisively that your Maker has choice in specifying your self with some of what is possible for it. So from this decisive proof emerges that the sperm from which you arose absolutely cannot...
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the earlier statement 'So you know necessarily that what increased was non-existent then existed.' It confirms that this knowledge is indeed necessary (ḍarūrī) because a composite (murakkab) only exists with all its parts. The author then directs attention to the increase (zāʾid) added to the self after birth, such as growth. This increase is a body occupying space (jirm yaʿmuru farāghan), and it is possible for it to be with its specific quantity and quality, or otherwise. This contingency proves that the Maker (ṣāniʿ) has choice (ikhtiyār) in specifying the self with those particular characteristics. The argument concludes that the sperm cannot cause itself, but the text breaks off mid-sentence at the page end.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Sanusi is arguing that the sperm itself caused the increase.
Correction: The argument is that the increase is contingent (could be otherwise), so it requires a choosing Maker, not that its origin (sperm) caused it.

B) Because it could have been different in quantity and quality
C) Because it is from the sperm

Key Takeaway
The contingency of our physical growth—its specific quantity and quality—directly proves that our Maker has free choice in creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body grew in a specific way—height, shape, features—that could have been different. If every detail was chosen for you by Allah, what does that mean for how you view yourself and how you treat your body? Will you use it only for your desires or as a trust from the Chooser?
?
You did not choose your own growth; it was given to you. Yet, you act as if your body is your own property. How can you begin to use your physical existence—your hands, your tongue, your very growth—as an act of worship, knowing it was Allah’s choice that made you exactly as you are?
?
This lesson proves that the specific quantity and quality of your increase are contingent and chosen. If you truly believed that even the smallest physical change is by divine choice, would you ever complain about your appearance or your health? What would change in your daily gratitude and patience?

Proof that the Agent of Existence is Not the Essence Itself
Sanusi argues that the originator of a being cannot be its essence or a part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the originator of a being cannot be its own essence or any part of it.

Core Idea
The essence cannot be self-causing because its effect would cease after separation.

Sanusi presents a concise argument refuting the idea that a being's essence could be its own originator. He uses the example of a child's development after birth to show that the essence continues to change and grow, which cannot be explained if the essence itself were the cause. This leads to the conclusion that the originator must be external and choosing.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Recognize the question: Can the essence of a being be the cause of its own existence? This is what Sanusi is disproving.
2
Follow the proof: the example of the child
Sanusi points out that after a child separates from the womb, most of its essence (body) continues to grow. If the essence caused itself, its effect should stop after separation, but it doesn't.
3
Grasp the logical impossibility
The essence has different parts with different properties. A uniform cause or nature cannot produce such variety; only a choosing agent can specify each part.
This refutes both self-causation and causation by nature.
4
Conclude the argument
The originator must be a choosing agent (Allah), since all possibilities are equal to Him. Thus, the originator is neither the essence itself nor any part.
Sanusi promises further clarification later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : فيلزمُ أنْ ينقطعَ تأثيرُهُ بعد الانفصال عن الرحم ، كيف ومعظم الذاتِ بعد الانفصالِ وُجِدَ ؟! على أنَّ اختلاف الذاتِ وتخصيص كل جزء منها بما يجوز على غيره يمنعُ قطعاً أنْ يكونَ لعلَّةٍ أو لطبيعة فيها تأثيرٌ ، فتعيَّنَ أنَّ التأثير فيها إنَّما هو بالاختيار والممكنات بالنسبة إلى الفاعل المختار سواء ، وهو الله تعالى فظهرَ أنَّ البرهان السابق يقتضي أنَّ الموجِدَ للذاتِ ليسَ نفسَها ولا جزءاً مِنْ أجزائها ، وستزيدُ ذلكَ بياناً بعدُ إِنْ شاءَ اللهُ تعالى .
We say: It necessarily follows that its effect is cut off after separation from the womb. How, while most of the essence after separation came into existence? Moreover, the difference of the essence and the specification of each part with what is possible for other parts absolutely prevents that there be a cause or a nature having influence in it. So it is determined that influence in it is only by choice, and contingent things with respect to the choosing agent are equal—and He is Allah, exalted. So it appears that the preceding proof requires that the originator of the essence is neither the essence itself nor a part of its parts, and we will clarify that further afterward, if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the originator of a being cannot be its own essence or a part of it. Sanusi begins with an observation: after the child separates from the womb, its essence continues to grow (most of the essence appears after separation). If the essence itself were the cause or nature influencing its own existence, then its effect should cease after separation, but it does not. Furthermore, the essence has different parts, each specified with properties different from others. Such specification cannot be due to a necessary cause or nature, because they would produce uniformity. It must be due to a choosing agent, for whom all possibilities are equal. Therefore, the proof shows that the originator of the essence is neither the essence itself nor any of its parts. Sanusi promises further clarification later.

Key Terms
تأثير
ذات
علة
طبيعة
اختيار
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A being's essence could be its own originator, especially if the essence is simple and without parts.
Correction: Sanusi's proof targets composite essences, but even a simple essence would require an external cause because its existence is contingent and not necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can't the essence itself be the originator of its own existence?
A) Because the effect would be continuous forever.
B) Because after separation from the womb the effect would cut off but it continues.
C) Because the essence is simple.
D) Because only choice can produce variation.

Key Takeaway
The originator of any contingent being cannot be its own essence or a part; it must be an external choosing agent, which is Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your very existence—every part of you that grew after conception—cannot be self-caused or due to blind nature. If it were, you would not be here. How does this truth confront your daily feeling of self-sufficiency? Will you let this moment of reflection shift your reliance from yourself to the One who chose to bring you into being?
?
The proof shows that your essence is not your own originator; you are entirely dependent on an external choosing Agent. In what areas of your life do you still act as if you are self-originating or in control of your own destiny? What would it look like to live today with conscious dependence on Allah?
?
Sanusi argues that specification of parts with different properties cannot be from nature—it must be from a deliberate choice. When you look at your own body, abilities, and circumstances, do you see them as random or as intentionally given? Write down one specific aspect of your existence you often take for granted and commit to thanking Allah for it today.

Proof from Bodily Growth Against Sperm as Originator
Sanusi uses bodily growth to disprove sperm as originator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's proof from bodily growth that the sperm cannot be the originator of the human being, and why both choice and nature are ruled out.

Core Idea
Your body's growth proves that sperm lacks both choice and nature to be the originator.

In this passage, Sanusi presents his second proof for the existence of a choosing Creator, this time focusing on the observable growth of the human body. By examining the new parts that appear as a child grows, he demonstrates that the original sperm cannot account for the specific attributes of the mature body. The argument eliminates both the possibility of the sperm having choice and the possibility of it having a nature that would produce the observed results.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise of increase
The passage begins by noting that you know necessarily that what increased was non-existent and then existed. This is the foundation of the argument.
This premise is self-evident from the observation of growth.
2
Consider the specific qualities of the increase
Look at the parts that grew: they have a specific size and quality that could have been different. This shows contingency and the need for a chooser.
Contingency points to a specifying agent.
3
Eliminate the sperm as originator via choice
The sperm cannot have the choice to specify these parts because it is not a conscious agent. So it cannot be the originator.
Choice requires will and knowledge, which sperm lacks.
4
Eliminate nature as the cause
If the sperm had a nature, it would produce a uniform sphere (since nature is uniform) and continuous growth (since nature would not stop). The fact that you are not spherical and do not grow forever shows no nature is involved.
Nature would act uniformly and without cessation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فتعلمُ على الضرورةِ أنَّ ما زادَ كان معدوماً ثم كانَ ) ذلك صدق ما ادَّعيناهُ مِنْ كونك تعلمُ ضرورةَ أَنَّكَ لم تكن ثم كنتَ ؛ لأنَّ المركَّبَ لا وجود له إلا بجميع أجزائه • ثم إذا نظرت إلى هذا الزائدِ مِنْ ذاتِكَ وجدتَهُ جرماً يعمرُ فراغاً ، يجوز أنْ يكونَ على ما هو عليهِ مِنَ المقدارِ المخصوص والصفة المخصوصة ، وأن يكون على خلافهما ، فتعلمُ قطعاً أنَّ لصانعِكَ اختياراً في تخصيص ذاتِكَ ببعض ما جازَ عليها ، فيخرجُ لكَ مِنْ هذا البرهان القاطع على أنَّ النطفة التي نشأت عنها قطعاً يستحيلُ أنْ تكون هي الموجدة لذاتِكَ ؛ لعدم إمكان الاختيار لها حتى تُخصص ذاتَكَ ببعض ما جازَ عليها ، وأيضاً لا طبع لها في وجودِ ذاتِكَ ، وإلا لكنتَ على شكل الكرة ؛ لاستواء أجزاء النطفة ، ولا في نموّها ، وإلا لكنتَ تنمو أبداً
His statement: 'So you know by necessity that what increased was non-existent then existed.' That is the truth of what we claimed, that you know necessarily that you were not then you were, because a composite has no existence except with all its parts. Then if you look at this increase from your essence, you find it a body filling a void. It is possible for it to be as it is in terms of specific quantity and specific attribute, and to be contrary to them. So you know categorically that your Maker has choice in specifying your essence with some of what is possible for it. So from this decisive proof it emerges that the sperm from which you arose is absolutely impossible to be the originator of your essence, because it cannot have choice to specify your essence with some of what is possible for it. Also, it has no nature in the existence of your essence, for otherwise you would be spherical in shape, due to the uniformity of the parts of the sperm. Nor in its growth, for otherwise you would grow forever.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a second proof, this time from the growth of the body. Sanusi begins by quoting a proposition: you know necessarily that what increased was non-existent then existed. This is self-evident from the fact that a child grows: new parts appear. A composite only exists when all its parts are present, so the increased parts were created. Next, he asks you to consider the increase: it has a specific size and quality, and it could have been different. From this you know that your Maker has choice in specifying these attributes. This leads to the decisive conclusion that the sperm (the initial seed) cannot be the originator of your essence, because it lacks the choice needed to specify different parts. Additionally, the sperm cannot have a nature that would produce your essence, because if it had a nature, the parts would be uniform like a sphere (since nature would not differentiate), and if it had a nature for growth, you would grow forever. Thus, the sperm is not the agent.

اختيار
تخصيص
صفة
مقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural processes like growth can explain the specification of body parts without a choosing agent.
Correction: Sanusi shows that nature would produce uniformity (a sphere) and endless growth, which is not observed, so nature cannot explain the specific attributes.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi argue that the sperm cannot be the originator of your essence?
A) Because the sperm has no will.
B) Because if it had a nature, you would be spherical and grow forever.
C) Both A and B.
D) Because the sperm is composed of parts.

Key Takeaway
The growth and specific attributes of the human body prove that the original sperm cannot be the originator, because it lacks choice and nature cannot produce such particularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every part of your body—its size, shape, and function—is a direct act of divine choice. How does this realization confront your tendency to take your body for granted or to attribute its formation to mere biology? What will you do today to acknowledge the Creator's hand in your own existence?
?
If the sperm had a nature, you would be spherical and grow forever. But you are not. Your finite, limited form is a constant sign of a Creator who chooses to stop and specify. How does this truth challenge your desire for unlimited life on your own terms? Will you surrender the illusion of self-sufficiency and thank Him for setting boundaries?
?
Sanusi proves that neither blind nature nor the sperm itself can account for your unique personhood. You are wanted by a Maker who selected your specific attributes. How does this knowledge affect the way you view your worth and purpose? Are you living as if your existence is an accident, or as a deliberate gift from a loving Creator?

Refuting the Nuffa as an Agent by Choice
Sanusi refutes the first mode: influence by choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the nuffa cannot act by choice and why this necessitates a free creator.

Core Idea
Choice requires life and will, but the nuffa is lifeless.

Sanusi begins by eliminating one possibility: that the nuffa acts by choice. He gives two arguments: first, a chooser must have life and will, which the nuffa lacks; second, if it could choose, its effect would not be limited to one particular being. This lesson unpacks these proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two arguments Sanusi presents.
2
Identify the two sub-arguments
The first argument is that a chooser needs life, power, will, and knowledge. The second is that if it could choose, its influence wouldn't be restricted.
The second argument also includes a comparison to the resulting perfect essence.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does this refutation matter? It shows that the nuffa cannot be the agent, so we must look elsewhere.
This leads to the need for a free creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا عرفت هذا فهذهِ الأوجه الثلاثةُ كلُّها مستحيلةٌ في النطفة : أما تأثيرها فيما نشأ عنها بالاختيار : فضروري البطلان ؛ إذ الحياة والقدرة والإرادة والعلمُ لازمةٌ للمُؤثر بالاختيار ، وهي جمادٌ لا تتصفُ بشيءٍ مِنْ ذلكَ قطعاً ، وأيضاً لو أثَرَتِ النطفة بالاختيارِ لَمَا اختَصَّ تأثيرها بهذه الذاتِ التي تكوَّنَتْ عنها دون غيرها ، ولكانَتْ هذهِ الذات الكاملة أحرى أنْ تُؤثر في إيجادِ الذواتِ ؛ لاشتمالها على النطفة المدعى لها القدرة على التأثيرِ ، ولما فيها أيضاً مِنَ الأوصاف المناسبة للتأثير ؛ كالعلم والقدرة والإرادة والحياة وغير ذلك ، وعجزها عن ذلك معلوم بالضرورة ، فأحرى ما هو أضعفُ منها. وأمَّا تأثيرها بالطبع ، وفي معناهُ العِلَّةُ : فباطل ؛ لاختصاص هذهِ الذاتِ بمقدار مخصوص وصفة مخصوصة ، ونسبتها - أعني : النطفة - إلى جميع المقادير والصفاتِ نسبةٌ واحدةٌ. فتعيَّنَ أنْ يكونَ الفاعل مختاراً ، لهُ إرادةٌ يُرجعُ بها بعض الجائز على بعض. وأيضاً : فكلُّ مِنَ النطفة والذاتِ جواهر متماثلة ، ومع ذلك فقدِ اختص بعضُها بِقُوَّةِ السمع ، وبعضُها بِقُوَّةِ البصرِ ، وبعضُها بِقُوَّةِ الشم ، وبعضُها بقُوَّةِ العقل ... إلى غير ذلكَ مِنَ الاختلافات التي لا تُحصى ، وكلٌّ يجوز أنْ يكونَ في مكان صاحبه ، وأنْ يكونَ على خلافِ ما هو عليه ، والطبيعةُ والعِلَّةُ لا يجوز أَنْ يُخصصا مِثْلاً عن مِثْلٍ.
If you have known this, then these three modes are all impossible in the nuffa (semen): As for its influence by choice on what arises from it, its falsity is necessary; because life, power, will, and knowledge are necessary for the agent by choice, and it (the nuffa) is inanimate, not characterized by any of that absolutely. Also, if the nuffa acted by choice, its influence would not be restricted to this particular essence that came from it to the exclusion of others, and this perfect essence would be more deserving of influencing the bringing-into-existence of essences, because it includes the nuffa that is claimed to have power to influence, and because of what it also contains of attributes suitable for influence, such as knowledge, power, will, life, and others. And its inability to do that is known necessarily, so even more so what is weaker than it. As for its influence by nature, and in its meaning by cause, it is invalid because of the specification of this essence to a particular quantity and a particular quality, while its relation—I mean the nuffa—to all quantities and qualities is one and the same. So it is determined that the agent be a free chooser, having a will by which it prefers some possibilities over others. Also: all of the nuffa and the essence are similar substances, and yet some are distinguished by the power of hearing, some by the power of sight, some by the power of smell, some by the power of reason, up to other differences that are countless. And each could have been in the place of the other and could have been other than what it is. And nature and cause cannot specify a similar from a similar.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the nuffa (semen) cannot be the agent responsible for the formation of a human being, concluding that the agent must be a free chooser (God). It begins by stating that all three modes of influence are impossible for the nuffa. First, influence by choice is impossible because a chooser must have life, power, will, and knowledge, but the nuffa is inanimate and lacks these. Also, if it acted by choice, its effect would not be restricted to one particular essence; moreover, the resulting perfect essence (the human) would be more deserving of influencing other essences, yet it cannot even create itself. Second, influence by nature or cause is impossible because the resulting essence has a specific quantity and quality, but the nuffa has an equal relation to all quantities and qualities, so nature/cause cannot specify. Therefore, the agent must be a free chooser with will to prefer some possibilities over others. Then a second argument: the nuffa and the resulting essence are similar substances, yet they receive different powers (hearing, sight, smell, reason). Since nature and cause cannot differentiate between similar things, this also proves the need for a free agent.

اختيار
فاعل مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The nuffa might be considered a living thing that can choose.
Correction: The nuffa is inanimate and cannot have will or life.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why can't the nuffa act by choice?
A) Because it lacks the necessary attributes of a chooser.
B) Because it is too small.
C) Because it is not made of atoms.
D) Because it was created by something else.

Key Takeaway
The nuffa cannot act by choice because it lacks life, will, and power, which are essential for a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the nuffa cannot act by choice, then who gives life and power to every being? In your daily life, do you act as if your choices and abilities are your own, or do you recognize that they are from Allah? What would change if you truly internalized that you are not the ultimate agent?
?
You possess life, will, and power—gifts that the nuffa lacks. How are you using these gifts? Are you using your will to obey the One who granted them, or to pursue your own desires as if you were independent?
?
The nuffa is inanimate, yet it becomes a perfect human. This should remind you that your existence is entirely from God. When you look in the mirror, do you see a self-made person, or do you see a sign of Allah's creative power? What gratitude does that demand from you today?

Refuting the Nuffa as an Agent by Nature and Establishing a Free Creator
Sanusi refutes influence by nature/cause and provides a second proof from the diversity of powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the nuffa cannot act by nature or cause, and why a free creator is necessary to explain the specification of qualities.

Core Idea
Nature cannot choose; only a free will can specify differences.

Sanusi now turns to the second mode: influence by nature or cause. He argues that nature cannot explain why a specific quantity and quality arise from the nuffa, since the nuffa is equally related to all possibilities. He then adds a second argument from the diversity of powers among similar substances. This lesson will make clear that only a free agent with will can account for the order in creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two parts: the nature/cause refutation and the second argument about similar substances.
2
Understand the nature/cause argument
The nuffa has an equal relation to all quantities and qualities, so nature/cause cannot specify one outcome.
This leads directly to the conclusion that the agent must be free.
3
Grasp the second argument
Similar substances (nuffa and human) are endowed with different powers. Nature/cause cannot differentiate between similars.
This reinforces the need for a free chooser.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does this prove a free creator? Because only a will can choose between equal possibilities.

?

When you consider your own unique combination of strengths and weaknesses, do you genuinely live as though each one was intentionally gifted to you? What would it look like to surrender that area to His will today?

beginner

Refutation of Drop's Causality and Proof of Free Agent
Sanusi refutes drop's causality by choice and nature, proving free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the drop (nufṭa) cannot cause the human by choice or nature, and how this necessitates a free agent Creator with will.

Core Idea
The drop is powerless; only a free agent with will creates.

Sanusi now applies the three categories of causality to the specific case of the human being. He argues that the drop (nufṭa) from which we develop cannot be the true cause. By eliminating influence by choice and by nature, he forces the conclusion that a free agent with will is the only possible Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi systematically refutes two possibilities before concluding.
2
Identify the two refuted modes
The passage refutes influence by choice (ikhtiyār) and then influence by nature (ṭabʿ) / cause (ʿilla). Understand why each is impossible for the drop.
The 'three aspects' refers to the three kinds of causal influence, but only two are discussed in detail here.
3
Follow the choice refutation
Notice the two arguments: first, the drop lacks necessary attributes (life, power, will, knowledge). Second, if it had choice, its effect wouldn't be limited to one entity.
4
Follow the nature/cause refutation
The resulting human has specific magnitude and quality, but the drop is indifferent to all magnitudes and qualities. Nature/cause cannot produce that specificity.
5
See the conclusion and additional proof
The only remaining possibility is a free agent with will. Then Sanusi adds an argument from similar substances: the drop and the human are similar substances yet have different powers—nature cannot explain such distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا عرفت هذا فهذهِ الأوجه الثلاثةُ كلُّها مستحيلةٌ في النطفة : أما تأثيرها فيما نشأ عنها بالاختيار : فضروري البطلان ؛ إذ الحياة والقدرة والإرادة والعلمُ لازمةٌ للمُؤثر بالاختيار ، وهي جمادٌ لا تتصفُ بشيءٍ مِنْ ذلكَ قطعاً ، وأيضاً لو أثَرَتِ النطفة بالاختيارِ لَمَا اختَصَّ تأثيرها بهذه الذاتِ التي تكوَّنَتْ عنها دون غيرها ، ولكانَتْ هذهِ الذات الكاملة أحرى أنْ تُؤثر في إيجادِ الذواتِ ؛ لاشتمالها على النطفة المدعى لها القدرة على التأثيرِ ، ولما فيها أيضاً مِنَ الأوصاف المناسبة للتأثير ؛ كالعلم والقدرة والإرادة والحياة وغير ذلك ، وعجزها عن ذلك معلوم بالضرورة ، فأحرى ما هو أضعفُ منها . وأمَّا تأثيرها بالطبع ، وفي معناهُ العِلَّةُ : فباطل ؛ لاختصاص هذهِ الذاتِ بمقدار مخصوص وصفة مخصوصة ، ونسبتها - أعني : النطفة - إلى جميع المقادير والصفاتِ نسبةٌ واحدةٌ . فتعيَّنَ أنْ يكونَ الفاعل مختاراً ، لهُ إرادةٌ يُرجعُ بها بعض الجائز على بعض . وأيضاً : فكلُّ مِنَ النطفة والذاتِ جواهر متماثلة ، ومع ذلك فقدِ اختص بعضُها بِقُوَّةِ السمع ، وبعضُها بِقُوَّةِ البصرِ ، وبعضُها بِقُوَّةِ الشم ، وبعضُها بقُوَّةِ العقل ... إلى غير ذلكَ مِنَ الاختلافات التي لا تُحصى ، وكلٌّ يجوز أنْ يكونَ في مكان صاحبه ، وأنْ يكونَ على خلافِ ما هو عليه ، والطبيعةُ والعِلَّةُ لا يجوز أَنْ يُخصصا مِثْلاً عن مِثْلٍ .
So if you know this, then these three aspects are all impossible in the drop (nufṭa): As for its influence on what arises from it by choice (ikhtiyār), it is necessarily invalid, because life, power, will, and knowledge are necessary for the one who influences by choice, and it [the drop] is inanimate, not characterized by any of that absolutely. Also, if the drop influenced by choice, its influence would not be restricted to this particular entity that came from it rather than another, and this complete entity would be more deserving of producing entities, because it contains the drop which is claimed to have the power to influence, and because it also contains attributes suitable for influence, such as knowledge, power, will, life, and others, while its inability [the complete entity's] to do so is known necessarily, so it is more fitting [that] what is weaker than it [is unable]. As for its influence by nature (ṭabʿ), and what is meant by it is cause (ʿilla), it is false, because of the restriction of this entity to a specific magnitude and specific quality, and its relation—I mean the drop—to all magnitudes and qualities is one and the same. So it is determined that the agent (fāʿil) must be free (mukhtār), having an will (irāda) by which it prefers some possibilities over others. Also: every one of the drop and the entity is a similar substance (jawhar), yet some are specifically endowed with the power of hearing, some with the power of sight, some with the power of smell, some with the power of intellect... up to other countless differences, and each could take the place of the other and could be otherwise, and nature and cause cannot specify one similar over another.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two possible modes of influence by the drop (nufṭa) and a proof that the true Creator is a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār). The author first states that the three ways an entity can act—by choice (ikhtiyār), by nature (ṭabʿ), or as a necessary cause (ʿilla)—are all impossible for the drop. He then refutes each: influence by choice is impossible because the drop is inanimate and lacks the necessary attributes of life, power, will, and knowledge. Moreover, if it influenced by choice, its effect would not be restricted to a single entity; rather, the resulting complete human would be more deserving of creative power since it possesses those attributes, yet it is obviously powerless—so the weaker drop is even less capable. Influence by nature or as a cause is false because the resulting entity has a specific magnitude and quality, yet the drop itself has no inherent relation to any particular magnitude or quality over another. Therefore, the agent must be a free chooser with an will. He then provides an additional argument: the drop and the resulting entity are both similar substances, yet they are assigned different powers (hearing, sight, etc.) arbitrarily—nature and necessary cause cannot assign such distinctions between similars, so a free agent is required.

جواهر متماثلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the drop itself has inherent creative power.
Correction: The drop cannot act by choice (lacks life, power, will, knowledge) nor by nature (indifferent to specifics), so it cannot be a cause.

Checkpoint
Why is influence by nature (ṭabʿ) impossible for the drop according to the passage?
A) Because the drop is inanimate.
B) Because the resulting human has a specific magnitude and quality, but the drop is related equally to all magnitudes and qualities.
C) Because the drop lacks life and power.
D) Because the drop is a similar substance.

Key Takeaway
The drop cannot cause the human by choice or nature, proving that a free agent with will is the true Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own body—its ability to see, hear, think—have you ever casually attributed its existence and abilities to 'nature' or 'biology' without pausing to acknowledge the Creator? How does this lesson's refutation of the drop's causality make you re-evaluate that assumption right now?
?
If the drop cannot produce even the smallest aspect of your form by itself, what does that mean for your daily reliance on your own strength, intelligence, or resources? Are there areas of your life where you secretly trust your own 'power' as if it were independent of Allah?
?
The proof shows that a free agent with will must be the one who assigned different powers to similar substances—like giving you hearing and another sense. Knowing that the All-Willing chose your specific abilities, do you see your unique traits as a deliberate gift from Allah, and how should that change the way you use them today?

The Proof from Specification for a Free Creator
Sanusi presents a formal syllogistic proof for the Creator's free will via specification of the human body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses a first-figure syllogism to prove that the Creator who specified your body must be a free agent.

Core Idea
The specification of your body among possibilities demands a free chooser.

Sanusi now presents a formal logical proof for the free will of the Creator. He structures his argument as a syllogism in the first figure, using the observable fact that your body has been given specific characteristics—size, shape, limbs—when many alternatives were possible. This argument, known as the proof from specification (burhān al-takhṣīṣ), shows that the Creator who made these choices must be a free agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once to get the flow of the argument. Notice that Sanusi mentions two claims but only completes the first.
The passage cuts off after introducing the second claim.
2
Identify the syllogism
Find the minor and major premises. The major premise is omitted but implied. Write it out in your own words.
The syllogism is in the first figure (shakl awwal).
3
Understand the minor premise
Sanusi explains that your body as a whole and in its parts is specified among possibilities. List some examples he gives (length, width, eye vs ear).
4
Understand why nature cannot specify
The major premise: nature and necessary cause act by essential suitability and cannot arbitrarily specify one similar over another. Why is that?
5
Draw the conclusion
The specifier must be free. Reflect on how this argument establishes the Creator's will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولُهُ الترتيب : : ( فتعلمُ قطعاً أنَّ لصانعِكَ اختياراً ) ادَّعى دعويَيْنِ على الأولى : أنَّ صانع ذاتِكَ فاعل مختارُ : واحتج عليها ببرهان مِنَ الشكلِ الأَوَّلِ ، حذفَ فِيهِ الكبرى للعلم بها ، وتقريرُهُ أنْ تقول : ذاتُكَ اختصَّتْ بجائز بدلاً عن جائز باعتبارِ مجموعها وباعتبار أجزائها وكلُّ ما كانَ كذلك ففاعلُهُ مختار لفعله ؛ فينتج : ذاتُكَ فاعلها مختار . لدليل الصغرى ظاهر ؛ فإنَّ مجموع الذاتِ قدِ اختص ببعض المقادير ؛ مِنْ كونه ذا طول مخصوص وعرض مخصوص ، والطولُ أكثرُ مِنَ العرض مثلاً معَ جواز أنْ يكونَ على خلاف ذلك ، والأشكال الهندسيَّةُ كلُّها في حقِّهِ جائزة لا رجحانَ لبعضها على بعض باعتبارِ ذاتِهِ ، وكذا أيضاً قدِ اختصَّ ببعض الأعراض مِنَ الألوان والأصوات ونحوهما دون بعض . وأما باعتبار أجزائها : فقدِ اختَصَّ بعضُها مع استوائها بأنْ كانَ عيناً ، وبعضُها بأنْ كانَ أُذُناً ، وبعضُها بأنْ كانَ يداً . . . إلى غير ذلكَ مِنَ الاختلافاتِ ، وكلٌّ في محلّ مخصوص ، ولهُ عَرَضُ مخصوص ومِقدار مخصوص ، مع جواز غير ذلك في الجميع . وأما دليلُ الكبرى : فلأنَّ تأثير الطبيعةِ والعِلَّةٍ لمَّا كانَ بالمناسبة الذاتية فيستحيلُ أنْ يناسب الضدَّين وأنْ يخصّ مِثْلاً عن مِثْلِ ، فَتَعَيَّنَ أَنْ يكونَ المُخصِّص لذاتِكَ مختاراً . الثانيةُ مِنَ الدعويين - وهي المقصودة ، والأولى وسيلة لها - : أنَّ
His saying: 'The arrangement: (So you know for certain that your Maker has choice (ikhtiyār).' He claimed two things: The first: that the Maker of your essence (dhāt) is a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār). He argued for it with a proof from the first figure (shakl awwal), in which he omitted the major premise because it is known. Its formulation is that you say: Your essence is specified with a possible thing instead of a possible thing, with respect to its totality and with respect to its parts, and everything that is thus, its agent is free in its action; so it follows: the agent of your essence is free. The proof of the minor premise is apparent; for the totality of the essence has been specified with some magnitudes, like being of a particular length and a particular width, and the length is greater than the width for example, while it is possible that it be otherwise, and all geometrical shapes are possible with respect to it, with no preference of some over others in terms of its essence. Likewise, it has been specified with some accidents (aʿrāḍ) like colors, sounds, and the like to the exclusion of others. As for with respect to its parts: some of them, despite their similarity, have been specified to be an eye, some to be an ear, some to be a hand, and so on, up to other differences, and each is in a specific place, has a specific accident and a specific magnitude, while it is possible to be otherwise in all. As for the proof of the major premise: because the influence of nature (ṭabʿ) and cause (ʿilla) is by essential suitability (munāsaba dhātiyya), it is impossible to suit two opposites and to specify one similar over another, so it is determined that the specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) of your essence is free. The second of the two claims—which is the intended one, and the first is a means to it—is that
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a formal proof for the existence of a free Creator, presented as a syllogism in the first figure (shakl awwal). The author introduces two claims (daʿwāyān): first, that the Creator of your essence is a free agent; second (the intended conclusion), something not fully stated but likely that He is one or has will—the passage cuts off. The proof for the first claim is given: your essence (your body) as a whole and in its parts is specified with particular qualities (size, shape, limbs) instead of other possibilities, so it must have a free specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ). The minor premise is that your essence exhibits such specification: the whole body has specific dimensions (length, width, shape), and the parts (eye, ear, hand) are arranged in specific places with specific accidents. The major premise (omitted but implied) is that anything that is specified among possibilities requires a free agent, because nature and necessary cause cannot arbitrarily specify one similar over another. Thus, the specifier must be free. This is a classic kalam argument (burhān al-takhṣīṣ) for the existence of a willing Creator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The syllogism is circular because it assumes what it tries to prove.
Correction: The syllogism is not circular: the minor premise is based on observable specification, and the major premise is an independent rational principle that anything specified among equals requires a free chooser.

Checkpoint
What is the logical form used in this proof for the existence of a free agent?
A) The second figure syllogism.
B) The first figure syllogism (shakl awwal).
C) A proof by contradiction.
D) An analogical argument.

Key Takeaway
The proof from specification (burhān al-takhṣīṣ) shows that the detailed features of your body, which could have been otherwise, require a free and choosing Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that every detail of your body—your height, your eye color, the shape of your hands—was specifically chosen by a free Creator. Have you ever thanked Him for exactly those details? If not, what stops you from doing so today?
?
If your body could have been otherwise, but it is exactly as it is by the will of a free Agent, then your very existence is a personal gift. How does that change how you will treat your body from this moment on?
?
The source says nature cannot specify, only a free chooser can. So the fact that you are reading this now is not random. What does that imply about your responsibility to seek and serve your Creator? What will you do about it today?

The Argument from Design in Human Body Parts
Sanusi argues from body part design to necessary Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the perfect proportion and diversity in the growth of body parts prove the existence of a wise Creator.

Core Idea
Perfect design in our bodies points directly to a perfect Designer.

Sanusi presents a vivid teleological argument from the human body. He draws attention to the remarkable variety and proportion in the growth of ears, nose, fingers, teeth, and more. This passage uses that observation to challenge materialistic explanations and affirm the existence of Allah, concluding with a stern refutation of naturalists and a prayer for guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the descriptive details about body parts and the shift to a rhetorical question.
The passage uses very specific examples – pay attention to the diversity mentioned.
2
Identify the argument structure
The argument moves from observation of design to a rhetorical question about its cause, then to the necessary conclusion that only a unique, all-powerful Creator could produce such craftsmanship.
The rhetorical question is the key turning point.
3
Understand the refutation of naturalists
Note how Sanusi dismisses the naturalists' views as absurd, calling them madness and associating them with loss of reason and faith.
This refutation reinforces the necessity of theistic explanation.
4
Reflect on the concluding prayer
The prayer asks for sound knowledge, a good end, and protection from innovations. Consider how this seals the argument in a tone of devotion.
The prayer shows the practical aim of theological knowledge.
