Non-Existence as an Object of Power and the Survival of Accidents
Sanusi elaborates on al-Qadi's view on non-existence and the debate over accident survival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between continuous and intermittent non-existence in kalam and how it impacts the debate over whether accidents can survive and whether non-existence can be an object of divine power.

Core Idea
Non-existence is not all the same; its continuity determines if it needs a cause.

In this passage, Sanusi navigates a nuanced debate about divine power and the ontology of non-existence. Al-Qadi al-Baqillani argued that non-existence can be an object of power, but a critical distinction between continuous and intermittent non-existence led to his hesitation on the survival of accidents. This distinction and its implications are central to understanding early Ash'ari positions on the nature of accidents and endurance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the passage fully before diving into commentary. Note the key terms: إعدام, عدم سابق, عدم لاحق, بقاء.
The passage is dense with technical vocabulary.
2
Understand al-Qadi's main claim
Al-Qadi holds that non-existence can be an object of power. He also insists that prior non-existence can be attributed to the effect. Ask: Why does he argue the conception of non-existence is uniform?
3
Grasp the distinction between continuous and intermittent non-existence
The text distinguishes between prior non-existence (sabiq) which is continuous and needs no preponderant, and subsequent non-existence (lahiq) which is intermittent and requires a preponderant. This distinction is key.
Continuous non-existence is the default; intermittent requires a cause.
4
See the implications for accidents and the debate
Because of this distinction, al-Qadi hesitated on whether accidents can survive. Then learn al-Razi's decisive affirmation and the early Ash'ari argument that survival would entail meaning in meaning, which the author refutes.
The early Ash'ari position is based on a specific theory of endurance (baqa').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومذهب القاضي : أنَّ الإعدام يصحُ أنْ يكونَ مُتعلَّقاً للقدرة، والزِمَ صحَّةَ إضافة العدم السابق إلى المؤثر، فإنَّ معقول العدمِ لا يختلفُ، وفُرِّقَ بأنَّ السابق مستمر، والمستمر يستغني عن المرجح، واللاحق طارى، ومقتضاه ترجيح طرف الممكنِ وترجيح الممكن لا يستغني عن المؤثر، فلأجل هذا تردَّدَ في بقاء الأعراض. وجزم الفخرُ في «المعالم» بصحة بقائها، وقدماء الأشعريَّةِ لمَّا اعتقدوا أنَّ الباقي باقٍ ببقاء، وأنَّ الجواهرَ إِنَّما صح بقاؤُها لقيامِ البقاء بها.. قالوا : لو بقيت الأعراضُ لزمَ قيام المعنى بالمعنى، وهو محال. وقد تقدَّمَ أنَّ التحقيق في البقاء خلافُهُ
And the doctrine of al-Qadi [al-Baqillani] is that non-existence (i'dam) can validly be an object of power (mutallaq li'l-qudra), and he compelled [others] to accept the validity of attributing prior non-existence to the effect, for the conception of non-existence is not different. And a distinction was made: the prior [non-existence] is continuous, and the continuous is independent of a preponderant, while the subsequent [non-existence] is intermittent, and its requirement is the preponderance of one side of the possible, and the preponderance of the possible is not independent of an effect. For this reason he hesitated regarding the survival of accidents. And al-Fakhr [al-Razi] decisively affirmed in 'al-Ma'alim' the validity of their survival. And the early Ash'aris, since they believed that the enduring endures by an endurance (baqa'), and that substances only validly endure because of the subsistence of endurance in them... said: If accidents survived, then the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning would be necessary, which is impossible. And it has preceded that the verified position regarding endurance is contrary to that.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage addresses the debate over whether non-existence (i'dam) can be an object of divine power. Al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) held that non-existence can validly be a term of power (qudra), and he compelled the acceptance that prior non-existence (i.e., the non-existence before creation) can be attributed to an effect (mu'aththir). The argument is that the conception (ma'qul) of non-existence is uniform. A distinction was then made: continuous non-existence (al-sabiq al-mustamirr) is independent of a preponderant (murajjih) because it is the default state, whereas subsequent non-existence (al-lahiq al-tari') is intermittent and requires a preponderant, because it involves a preference of one side of the possible over another, which needs an effective cause. Because of this nuance, al-Baqillani hesitated (taraddada) on the issue of the survival of accidents. In contrast, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi decisively affirmed the possibility of the survival of accidents in his 'Ma'alim Usul al-Din'. The early Ash'aris, holding that an enduring thing endures by a distinct attribute called baqa', and that substances endure due to the subsistence of baqa' in them, argued that if accidents were to survive, that would entail the subsistence of a meaning (i.e., the accident) in another meaning (i.e., the endurance), which is impossible. This, however, is refuted by the author's earlier demonstration that the verified stance on endurance is contrary to that (i.e., that endurance is not a distinct attribute subsisting in the enduring thing).

عدم سابق

طارئ

ترجيح
بقاء
معنى بالمعنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence cannot be an object of divine power because God only creates existence.
Correction: Al-Qadi argues non-existence can be an object of power, but the continuous vs. intermittent distinction resolves the paradox: continuous non-existence needs no cause, while intermittent non-existence does.

Checkpoint
Why did al-Qadi hesitate on the survival of accidents according to the text?
A) Because he believed accidents do not survive.
B) Because of the distinction between continuous and intermittent non-existence.
C) Because he agreed with al-Razi.
D) Because he thought survival requires a distinct attribute.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between continuous and intermittent non-existence is crucial in kalam for understanding whether non-existence can be an object of divine power and whether accidents can survive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as though your existence is a continuous gift from God, or do you fall into the illusion of self-sufficiency, forgetting that at every moment you are sustained by His will? How would your daily gratitude change if you truly internalized that your prior non-existence was the default and your current existence is a constant act of divine preference?
?
The early Ash'aris rejected the survival of accidents because of a theological assumption they held. Are there beliefs or practices you cling to simply because they are traditional, even if the verified truth contradicts them? Are you willing to let go of comfortable errors for the sake of sound understanding?
?
If non-existence can be an object of God's power, then nothing exists apart from His will. When you sin or neglect His commands, are you acknowledging that even your actions exist only by His permission? How does this realization press you to take responsibility for your choices and seek His forgiveness sincerely?

Why Motion and Rest Cannot Be Eternal
Sanusi explains why motion and rest individually cannot be eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that motion and rest cannot be eternal, based on their inherent logical properties.

Core Idea
Eternal things cannot be preceded or perish—motion fails both tests.

Sanusi begins his concise proof by tackling the possibility of motion or rest being eternal. He carefully explains why each fails the test of eternity, setting the stage for the formal argument against an eternal body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on motion
Read the first part of the passage carefully. Notice how Sanusi gives two reasons why motion cannot be eternal: its impossibility of persistence and its need for prior rest.
2
Understand the logic for rest
Next, focus on the argument for rest. Why would eternal rest make motion impossible? Sanusi says that if rest were eternal, its non-existence would be impossible, so the body could never move—but we observe motion.
3
Connect to the conclusion
See how this prepares for the full proof. If neither motion nor rest can be eternal, then any body that exists must have both begin in time.
This lesson covers only the first part of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخصر شيء في ذلكَ أنْ تقول : الحركة لا تكون أزليَّة ؛ لعدم إمكان بقائها ، ولملزوميتها سبقَ السكون في الحيز المنتقل عنه ، والأزلي لا يكون مسبوقاً بغيرِه ، والأزلي أيضاً يلزمُ بقاؤُهُ ، وأمَّا السكونُ أيضاً فلا يكون أزليّاً ، وإلا لاستحال عدمه ، فيستحيلُ أنْ يتحرَّكَ الجزم دائماً ، والعقل والمشاهدة يُكذِّبَانِهِ ، فتقول على هذا في نظم الدليل على حدوثِ الأجرام : لو وُجِدَ جِرْمٌ فِي الأَزْلِ لم يخلُ أَنْ يكونَ فيهِ مُتحرِّكاً أو ساكناً، لكنَّ التالي باطل بقسميه ، فالمقدَّمُ مثلُهُ. وبالجملة : فالحركة والسكون لا يكونان إلا حادثين ضرورةً ، فما لازمَهُما ـ وهو الجزم - يجب حدوثه ، ويتعالى مَنْ وجَبَ له القدمُ والبقاء أنْ يكونَ حادثاً
And the most concise thing in that is that you say: Motion cannot be eternal, because of the impossibility of its persistence, and because it necessitates precedence of rest in the place from which it moves, and the eternal cannot be preceded by something else, and the eternal also necessitates its persistence. As for rest, it also cannot be eternal, otherwise its non-existence would be impossible, so it would be impossible for the body to move constantly, and reason and observation falsify that. So you say on this in the organization of the proof of the temporality of bodies: If a body existed in eternity, it would not be free from being either moving or at rest in it, but the consequent is false in both its divisions, so the antecedent is likewise false. In summary: motion and rest are necessarily only temporally originated, so that which they entail—that is, the body—must be temporally originated, and exalted is He for whom eternity and perpetuity are necessary from being temporally originated.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a concise proof (أخصر شيء) for the temporality of bodies (حدوث الأجرام) by considering motion and rest. The author begins by stating that motion cannot be eternal for two reasons: it cannot persist (since it perishes moment by moment) and it necessitates that the body was previously at rest in the place it leaves (motion is departure from a place, so there must be prior rest there). The eternal cannot be preceded by something else, so motion is not eternal. Similarly, rest cannot be eternal, because if it were, its non-existence would be impossible, meaning the body would never move—contradicting observation. The proof is then formalized as a conditional syllogism: if a body were eternal, it would have to be either eternally moving or eternally at rest, but both are impossible (motion requires prior rest and rest would prevent motion), so the premise that the body is eternal is false. Therefore, bodies are temporally originated. The passage concludes by exalting God, who alone has true eternity and perpetuity, from being temporally originated.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
أزلي
حادث
جزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think motion could be eternal if it is continuous, but Sanusi shows motion perishes each moment.
Correction: Motion does not persist over time; it is a new accident at each instant. An eternal thing must be constant, which motion is not.

Checkpoint
What is one reason Sanusi gives for motion not being eternal?
A) Motion requires a mover
B) Motion cannot persist and requires prior rest
C) Motion is an accident
D) Motion is divisible

Key Takeaway
Motion and rest are each shown to be temporally originated because they lack the necessary attributes of eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own body is constantly in motion or rest. If both are temporary, what does that tell you about the permanence of your actions and their ultimate significance before Allah? How must you live knowing that every movement and stillness is a sign that you are not eternal and are entirely dependent on the Eternal?
?
Reflect on a time when you were completely still, perhaps in deep sleep. If that rest were eternal, you would never awaken. But you did. What does that moment of waking teach you about Allah's power to bring about change and your own utter contingency?
?
The passage argues that rest cannot be eternal because you would never be able to leave it. Is there any spiritual 'rest' or comfort in your life that you treat as if it will last forever? What would it take to honestly confront the fact that everything you cling to—status, relationships, health—is temporary and can be taken away in an instant?

The Formal Proof of Temporal Bodies
Sanusi presents the formal conditional proof and concludes that bodies are temporally originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi constructs a conditional syllogism to prove that all bodies are temporally originated.

Core Idea
If a body were eternal, it must be moving or at rest eternally—both impossible, so body is not eternal.

Having established that motion and rest cannot be eternal, Sanusi now weaves them into a formal logical proof. Using a conditional argument (if...then), he shows that the premise of an eternal body leads to a contradiction, forcing the conclusion that bodies must have a beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the formal proof passage
Read the second part of the passage. Notice the structure: 'If a body existed in eternity, it would have to be either moving or at rest. But both are false, so the antecedent is false.'
2
Understand the logical form
This is a conditional syllogism (modus tollens). The antecedent is 'a body exists eternally', the consequent is 'it is either moving or at rest eternally'. Since the consequent is impossible, the antecedent must be false.
3
Connect motion/rest to body
Sanusi concludes that since motion and rest are inevitably temporally originated, whatever entails them (the body) must also be temporally originated. This is a general principle: if an attribute is contingent, its subject is contingent.
4
Reflect on the exaltation of God
The passage ends by exalting Allah, who alone has true eternity, from being temporally originated. This reinforces the theological purpose of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخصر شيء في ذلكَ أنْ تقول : الحركة لا تكون أزليَّة ؛ لعدم إمكان بقائها ، ولملزوميتها سبقَ السكون في الحيز المنتقل عنه ، والأزلي لا يكون مسبوقاً بغيرِه ، والأزلي أيضاً يلزمُ بقاؤُهُ ، وأمَّا السكونُ أيضاً فلا يكون أزليّاً ، وإلا لاستحال عدمه ، فيستحيلُ أنْ يتحرَّكَ الجزم دائماً ، والعقل والمشاهدة يُكذِّبَانِهِ ، فتقول على هذا في نظم الدليل على حدوثِ الأجرام : لو وُجِدَ جِرْمٌ فِي الأَزْلِ لم يخلُ أَنْ يكونَ فيهِ مُتحرِّكاً أو ساكناً، لكنَّ التالي باطل بقسميه ، فالمقدَّمُ مثلُهُ. وبالجملة : فالحركة والسكون لا يكونان إلا حادثين ضرورةً ، فما لازمَهُما ـ وهو الجزم - يجب حدوثه ، ويتعالى مَنْ وجَبَ له القدمُ والبقاء أنْ يكونَ حادثاً
And the most concise thing in that is that you say: Motion cannot be eternal, because of the impossibility of its persistence, and because it necessitates precedence of rest in the place from which it moves, and the eternal cannot be preceded by something else, and the eternal also necessitates its persistence. As for rest, it also cannot be eternal, otherwise its non-existence would be impossible, so it would be impossible for the body to move constantly, and reason and observation falsify that. So you say on this in the organization of the proof of the temporality of bodies: If a body existed in eternity, it would not be free from being either moving or at rest in it, but the consequent is false in both its divisions, so the antecedent is likewise false. In summary: motion and rest are necessarily only temporally originated, so that which they entail—that is, the body—must be temporally originated, and exalted is He for whom eternity and perpetuity are necessary from being temporally originated.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a concise proof (أخصر شيء) for the temporality of bodies (حدوث الأجرام) by considering motion and rest. The author begins by stating that motion cannot be eternal for two reasons: it cannot persist (since it perishes moment by moment) and it necessitates that the body was previously at rest in the place it leaves (motion is departure from a place, so there must be prior rest there). The eternal cannot be preceded by something else, so motion is not eternal. Similarly, rest cannot be eternal, because if it were, its non-existence would be impossible, meaning the body would never move—contradicting observation. The proof is then formalized as a conditional syllogism: if a body were eternal, it would have to be either eternally moving or eternally at rest, but both are impossible (motion requires prior rest and rest would prevent motion), so the premise that the body is eternal is false. Therefore, bodies are temporally originated. The passage concludes by exalting God, who alone has true eternity and perpetuity, from being temporally originated.

In Sanusi's formal proof, what is the logical relationship between the antecedent and consequent?
A) Antecedent implies consequent
B) Consequent implies antecedent
C) They are contradictory
D) They are independent

Key Takeaway
The formal proof shows that because eternal motion and rest are impossible, no body can be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof concludes that every body is temporally originated and cannot be eternal. Reflect: In what ways do you live as if your body, your possessions, or this physical world will last forever? What would change if you truly internalized that only Allah is eternal?
?
If motion and rest are necessarily temporary, then every moment of movement or stillness is a sign that the universe depends on its Creator. Which moments of your day do you most often forget this dependence—when you are busy (moving) or when you are idle (resting)? How can you turn those very moments into reminders of Allah's sole eternity?
?
The proof humbles the intellect by showing that even the simplest logical reasoning demands we recognize the Creator's uniqueness. Where in your life do you rely on your own reasoning or worldly systems as if they are eternal truths? Will you submit your intellect to the reality of Allah's eternal existence today?

Refutation of Multiple Eternal Beings
Sanusi proves the impossibility of multiple eternal beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of multiple eternal beings is rationally impossible according to Sanusi.

Core Idea
One eternal is necessary; more than one leads to logical absurdity.

Sanusi begins by establishing that only Allah is eternal. He argues that the existence of any other eternal being would lead to contradictions: either attributes would need a specificer or each part would have attributes, resulting in multiple gods. This lesson examines his logical proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two main arguments
The passage presents two arguments: (a) the need for a specificer if attributes subsist in only some parts, and (b) the impossibility of attributes in every part leading to multiple gods.
3
Understand the rejection of composite attribute
Sanusi also rejects the claim that a single attribute can subsist in a composite whole, as it would require division of the attribute.
This third point is a bridge to the Trinity critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لاستحالة وجودِ قديم غيرِ اللهِ ، ولئلا يلزم الافتقار إلى المخصص في ترجيح بعض الأجزاء بقيام الصفاتِ بها دونَ بعض ، لكن قيام الصفاتِ بكل جزء محال؛ لأنَّهُ يُوجِبُ تعدُّدَ الآلهة ، وسيأتي برهان وجوب الوحدانية له جلَّ وعلا ، وأمَّا ادّعاءُ أَنَّ الصفة الواحدة تقومُ بالمجموع .. فلا يخفى بطلانه ، وأنَّهُ يلزمُ عليهِ انقسامُ ما لا يصح انقسامه

1 بطلانُ القول بالأقانيم الثلاثةِ ]

وإذا عرفت هذا عرفت استحالة التجزئة التي أثبتها النصارى لإلههم ، تعالى الله عزَّ وجلَّ عن ذلكَ علوّاً كبيراً ؛ فإنَّهم اعتقدوا أنَّ معبودهم جوهرُ ؛ أي: أصلُ الأقانيم ؛ وذلكَ أَنَّ لهُ عندَهم ثلاثةَ أقانيمَ : أُقنوم الوجودِ : ويُعبرونَ عنه بالأب ، وأقنوم العلمِ : ويُعبِّرُونَ عنه بالابن والكلمةِ ، وأقنوم الحياةِ : ويُعبّرون عنه بروح القدسِ

ثم قالوا : إنَّ مجموع الثلاثةَ إله واحد ! فجمعوا بين نقيضين : وحدة وكثرة ، وجعلوا الذات تتركَّبُ مِنْ مُجرَّدِ أحوالٍ لا وجود لها و اعتبارات لا تُوجَدُ إلا في الأذهان ، وذلك غير معقول أو وجوه لعاقل .

والأقنوم : كلمة يونانية ، والمراد بها في تلك اللغة : أصلُ الشيء ، ويعني بها النصارى : الأصل الذي كانَتْ عنه حقيقة إلههم ، وقد طُولبوا في دليل الحصر في الثلاثةِ ، فقالوا : لأنَّ الخلق والإبداع لا يتأتى إلا بها ، فقيل لهم : والإرادة والقدرة لا يتأتى الخلْقُ إلا بهما ، فأحكموا بأنَّ الأقانيم خمسة !(۱)
Because of the impossibility of the existence of an eternal other than Allah, and to avoid the necessity of needing a specificier in preferring some parts over others in the subsistence of attributes in them. But the subsistence of attributes in every part is impossible because it necessitates multiplicity of gods, and the proof of the necessity of oneness for Him, Majestic and Exalted, will come. As for the claim that a single attribute subsists in the whole... its falsehood is not hidden, and that it necessitates the division of what cannot be divided. [Section: The falsity of the doctrine of the three hypostases] And if you know this, you know the impossibility of the division which the Christians have affirmed for their god – exalted is Allah, Mighty and Majestic, above that with a great exaltation. For they believed that their object of worship is a substance (jawhar), i.e., the origin of the hypostases; and that according to them He has three hypostases: the hypostasis of existence – they express it as the Father; the hypostasis of knowledge – they express it as the Son and the Word; and the hypostasis of life – they express it as the Holy Spirit. Then they said: the sum of the three is one God! So they combined two contradictories: unity and multiplicity, and they made the essence composed of mere states that have no existence and considerations that exist only in minds – and that is not intelligible to any rational person. [The term] 'hypostasis' (uqnūm) is a Greek word, and its meaning in that language is the origin of a thing, and by it the Christians mean the origin from which the reality of their god came. They were asked for proof of limitation to three, and they said: because creating and originating are not achieved except by them. It was said to them: but will and power – creation is not achieved except by them. So they asserted [or were compelled] that the hypostases are five!
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a combined refutation: first, of the possibility of multiple eternal beings, and second, of the Christian doctrine of the Trinity (three hypostases). The author begins by stating that it is impossible for there to be an eternal being other than God, because if there were multiple eternal parts, each would need to have attributes, but attributes cannot subsist in every part without leading to multiple gods. He then addresses the Christian claim of a single attribute subsisting in a composite whole, calling it obviously false. He moves to the specific Christian doctrine: Christians consider their god a substance (jawhar) with three hypostases (aqānīm): existence (Father), knowledge (Son/Word), and life (Holy Spirit). They assert that these three together are one God, but this combines the contradictories of unity and multiplicity. The author explains that they have composed the divine essence of mere mental considerations with no external reality. He then notes that the term 'hypostasis' is Greek for 'origin', and that Christians were challenged to justify the limitation to three; they claimed creation requires only these three, but were countered by noting that will and power are also necessary, forcing them to either expand to five hypostases or admit inconsistency. The argument shows the logical impossibility and incoherence of the Trinity.

Key Terms
قديم
مخصص
جزء
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think multiple eternal beings are possible if they are independent.
Correction: Sanusi shows that multiple eternals lead to logical contradictions regardless of independence.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is the claim that a single attribute subsists in a composite of multiple eternals false?
A) Because attributes are not real.
B) Because it would require the attribute to be divided, which is impossible.
C) Because there is no such thing as a composite.
D) Because attributes are multiple.

Key Takeaway
Multiple eternal beings are impossible because they would require either an external specificer or result in multiple gods, both of which are absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even subconsciously, treated something in your life as an ultimate authority alongside Allah — such as your own desires, societal norms, or a person? How does Sanusi's argument expose the logical impossibility of such dual allegiance? This week, what step will you take to dismantle that false ultimate?
?
When you face a difficult decision, do you sometimes act as if multiple 'eternal' principles (like personal freedom versus obedience to God) are in conflict? How does the proof that multiple eternal beings are impossible help you resolve that tension in favor of tawhid? Commit to one decision this week where you will prioritize Allah's command without compromise.
?
If you truly believe that only Allah is eternal and self-sufficient, why do you still fear the opinions of others as if their judgment lasts forever? Write down one fear of people that you will actively combat by recalling that no one shares Allah's eternity.

Critique of the Christian Trinity
Sanusi refutes the Christian Trinity as logically incoherent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's rational critique of the Christian doctrine of the Trinity.

Core Idea
The Trinity combines unity and multiplicity, which is a logical contradiction.

Having established the impossibility of multiple eternals, Sanusi directly addresses the Christian doctrine of three hypostases in one God. He explains the Christian claim, then exposes its internal contradictions and the challenge of justifying exactly three hypostases.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Trinity
Read the Arabic passage that describes the Christian doctrine of three hypostases. Focus on how Sanusi presents their belief.
Note the terms used: جوهر, أقنوم, etc.
2
Identify the logical contradiction
Sanusi points out that claiming three hypostases yet one God combines unity and multiplicity. Underline this key point.
3
Analyze the challenge to limitation
Examine the exchange where Christians justify three hypostases and the counterargument that would expand them to five.
This shows the arbitrariness of the Trinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لاستحالة وجودِ قديم غيرِ اللهِ ، ولئلا يلزم الافتقار إلى المخصص في ترجيح بعض الأجزاء بقيام الصفاتِ بها دونَ بعض ، لكن قيام الصفاتِ بكل جزء محال؛ لأنَّهُ يُوجِبُ تعدُّدَ الآلهة ، وسيأتي برهان وجوب الوحدانية له جلَّ وعلا ، وأمَّا ادّعاءُ أَنَّ الصفة الواحدة تقومُ بالمجموع .. فلا يخفى بطلانه ، وأنَّهُ يلزمُ عليهِ انقسامُ ما لا يصح انقسامه

1 بطلانُ القول بالأقانيم الثلاثةِ ]

وإذا عرفت هذا عرفت استحالة التجزئة التي أثبتها النصارى لإلههم ، تعالى الله عزَّ وجلَّ عن ذلكَ علوّاً كبيراً ؛ فإنَّهم اعتقدوا أنَّ معبودهم جوهرُ ؛ أي: أصلُ الأقانيم ؛ وذلكَ أَنَّ لهُ عندَهم ثلاثةَ أقانيمَ : أُقنوم الوجودِ : ويُعبرونَ عنه بالأب ، وأقنوم العلمِ : ويُعبِّرُونَ عنه بالابن والكلمةِ ، وأقنوم الحياةِ : ويُعبّرون عنه بروح القدسِ

ثم قالوا : إنَّ مجموع الثلاثةَ إله واحد ! فجمعوا بين نقيضين : وحدة وكثرة ، وجعلوا الذات تتركَّبُ مِنْ مُجرَّدِ أحوالٍ لا وجود لها و اعتبارات لا تُوجَدُ إلا في الأذهان ، وذلك غير معقول أو وجوه لعاقل .

والأقنوم : كلمة يونانية ، والمراد بها في تلك اللغة : أصلُ الشيء ، ويعني بها النصارى : الأصل الذي كانَتْ عنه حقيقة إلههم ، وقد طُولبوا في دليل الحصر في الثلاثةِ ، فقالوا : لأنَّ الخلق والإبداع لا يتأتى إلا بها ، فقيل لهم : والإرادة والقدرة لا يتأتى الخلْقُ إلا بهما ، فأحكموا بأنَّ الأقانيم خمسة !(۱)
Because of the impossibility of the existence of an eternal other than Allah, and to avoid the necessity of needing a specificier in preferring some parts over others in the subsistence of attributes in them. But the subsistence of attributes in every part is impossible because it necessitates multiplicity of gods, and the proof of the necessity of oneness for Him, Majestic and Exalted, will come. As for the claim that a single attribute subsists in the whole... its falsehood is not hidden, and that it necessitates the division of what cannot be divided. [Section: The falsity of the doctrine of the three hypostases] And if you know this, you know the impossibility of the division which the Christians have affirmed for their god – exalted is Allah, Mighty and Majestic, above that with a great exaltation. For they believed that their object of worship is a substance (jawhar), i.e., the origin of the hypostases; and that according to them He has three hypostases: the hypostasis of existence – they express it as the Father; the hypostasis of knowledge – they express it as the Son and the Word; and the hypostasis of life – they express it as the Holy Spirit. Then they said: the sum of the three is one God! So they combined two contradictories: unity and multiplicity, and they made the essence composed of mere states that have no existence and considerations that exist only in minds – and that is not intelligible to any rational person. [The term] 'hypostasis' (uqnūm) is a Greek word, and its meaning in that language is the origin of a thing, and by it the Christians mean the origin from which the reality of their god came. They were asked for proof of limitation to three, and they said: because creating and originating are not achieved except by them. It was said to them: but will and power – creation is not achieved except by them. So they asserted [or were compelled] that the hypostases are five!
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a combined refutation: first, of the possibility of multiple eternal beings, and second, of the Christian doctrine of the Trinity (three hypostases). The author begins by stating that it is impossible for there to be an eternal being other than God, because if there were multiple eternal parts, each would need to have attributes, but attributes cannot subsist in every part without leading to multiple gods. He then addresses the Christian claim of a single attribute subsisting in a composite whole, calling it obviously false. He moves to the specific Christian doctrine: Christians consider their god a substance (jawhar) with three hypostases (aqānīm): existence (Father), knowledge (Son/Word), and life (Holy Spirit). They assert that these three together are one God, but this combines the contradictories of unity and multiplicity. The author explains that they have composed the divine essence of mere mental considerations with no external reality. He then notes that the term 'hypostasis' is Greek for 'origin', and that Christians were challenged to justify the limitation to three; they claimed creation requires only these three, but were countered by noting that will and power are also necessary, forcing them to either expand to five hypostases or admit inconsistency. The argument shows the logical impossibility and incoherence of the Trinity.

Key Terms
أقنوم
جوهر
أب
ابن
روح القدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Trinity is a rational mystery that cannot be understood but is not contradictory.
Correction: Sanusi argues that it is a direct logical contradiction between unity and multiplicity, not a mystery beyond reason.

Checkpoint
What three hypostases do Christians affirm according to Sanusi?
A) Father, Son, and Holy Spirit
B) Existence, Knowledge, and Life
C) Power, Will, and Knowledge
D) Soul, Body, and Spirit

Key Takeaway
The Christian Trinity is logically incoherent because it combines unity and multiplicity, and the limitation to three hypostases is arbitrary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that, when logically examined, combined two contradictory ideas? What did you do when you realized that?
?
This lesson shows that the Christian Trinity is not just a 'mystery' but a logical contradiction. Are there any beliefs you hold that you have been told are 'mysteries' but might actually be incoherent? Will you have the courage to examine them honestly?
?
If you were a Christian and read Sanusi's argument here, would you be able to defend your belief? Or would you have to admit that it lacks rational foundation? How does that challenge your commitment to truth?

Conclusive Proofs for God's Transcendence and Unknowability
Sanusi summarizes definitive proofs for God's transcendence and unknowability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four definitive conclusions that kalām proofs establish about God's existence, self-subsistence, absolute incomparability, and the obligatory limit of human comprehension.

Core Idea
Definitive proofs lead to awe: God is beyond all likeness and beyond full grasp.

This lesson presents a dense summary from Imam al-Sanūsī that gathers the essential certainties of Islamic theology in a single sentence. Before we dive into detailed arguments, it is crucial to see the whole picture: God exists, He is self-sufficient, nothing resembles Him, and no one can fully know Him except Himself. This passage serves as both a conclusion and a boundary for all theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage word by word
Read the Arabic text slowly. Notice the rhythm: 'wa-bi-l-jumla' introduces a summary. Each clause after 'al-barāhīn al-qaṭ'iyya' states a separate truth.
The phrase 'lā yuḥāṭa bihā' indicates divine attributes cannot be fully encompassed.
2
Identify the four conclusions
Look for the four things the proofs establish: (1) existence of the Most High Essence with perfect attributes, (2) God's self-subsistence, (3) impossibility of similarity to anything imaginable, (4) impossibility of attributes that imply similarity to contingent things. Then note the final statement about incapacity.
These are not random; they form a logical sequence from affirmation to negation to human limitation.
3
Reflect on the final claim
The passage ends with 'incapacity after this is obligatory' and 'none knows God except God.' Ask yourself: why is ignorance a duty? How does this protect against false claims?
This is not intellectual defeatism but a rational limit derived from the proofs themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة: فقد قامت البراهين القطعية على وجود الذات العلية موصوفة بصفات كاملة لا يُحاط بها، وعلى قيامه جل وعلا بنفسه، واستحالة مماثلته تعالى لكل ما يخطر بالبال، واستحالة اتصافه بكل ما يستلزم مماثلته للحوادث، والعجز بعد هذا عن الإدراك واجب ولا يعرف الله إلا الله جل وعلا.
In sum: definitive proofs have been established for the existence of the Most High Essence, described with perfect attributes that cannot be encompassed, and for His — exalted and majestic — being self-subsisting, and the impossibility of His — exalted — being similar to anything that occurs to the mind, and the impossibility of His being described with anything that entails His similarity to contingent things, and incapacity after this [to comprehend] is obligatory, and none knows God except God, exalted and majestic.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a concise summary of several foundational conclusions of kalām. It begins by stating that 'definitive proofs' (barāhīn qaṭ'iyya) have been established for several truths. First: the existence of the Most High Essence (al-dhāt al-'aliyya), described with perfect attributes that cannot be fully encompassed by human intellect. Second: that God is self-subsisting (qā'im bi-nafsihi), meaning He does not depend on any other. Third: the impossibility of God being similar to anything that occurs to the mind — this rejects any anthropomorphic imagination. Fourth: the impossibility of God being described with any attribute that would entail similarity to contingent things (ḥawādith). Finally, the passage concludes that after all this, incapacity ('ajz) to fully comprehend God is obligatory (wājib), and that none truly knows God except God Himself. This serves both as a doxological affirmation and a warning against excessive speculation, emphasizing divine transcendence and the limits of human reason.

Key Terms
البراهين القطعية
الذات العلية
قيامه بنفسه
المماثلة
الحوادث
العجز عن الإدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since we can prove God's attributes, we can fully comprehend His essence.
Correction: The passage stresses that incapacity to comprehend is obligatory (wājib) and that none knows God except God.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is obligatory after the definitive proofs have been established?
A) To know God's essence fully
B) To recognize one's incapacity to comprehend God
C) To describe God with every attribute that comes to mind
D) To deny all attributes from God

Key Takeaway
Definitive rational proofs establish God's existence and perfection, but also that He is absolutely incomparable and ultimately beyond full human comprehension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After learning that definitive proofs establish God's transcendence but that 'none knows God except God', how do you reconcile the certainty of belief with the humility of not fully comprehending? Does your worship reflect that awe or has it become routine? Commit to a specific change in your awareness of God's greatness this week.
?
The passage says that incapacity to comprehend is obligatory. Have you ever felt frustrated or impatient with the limits of your understanding of God? How can you turn that frustration into a deeper reverence rather than a reason to stop seeking knowledge? What step will you take to cultivate that humility in your daily reflection?
?
God is beyond anything that occurs to the mind. Can you identify an image or concept of God you have unknowingly held that might be too human-like? What will you do to purify your conception of God based on this clear proof?

The Two Proofs for God's Existence: al-Ihkaam and al-Ikhtiyaar
Sanusi explains the two proofs for God's existence and why al-ihkaam is superior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proofs for God's existence—al-ihkaam and al-ikhtiyaar—and why the proof from order (al-ihkaam) is clearer than the proof from free will (al-ikhtiyaar).

Core Idea
The order in creation is a clearer proof for God's existence than the argument from free will.

Sanusi presents two rational proofs for God's existence: one based on the precise order in the universe (al-ihkaam) and another based on the necessity of knowledge for an agent who acts by choice (al-ikhtiyaar). He asserts that the first is more evident. This passage explains the proof from choice in detail and compares the two proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two proofs
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that Sanusi directly states that inference to God is valid through two ways: al-ihkaam and al-ikhtiyaar. Underline these terms.
The passage then focuses on explaining the proof from al-ikhtiyaar.
2
Understand the reasoning behind al-ikhtiyaar
Follow Ibn al-Tilimsani's argument: God acts by choice → choice implies intention → intention requires knowledge of the intended. For God, this knowledge must be certain, not conjectural. Therefore, God is knowing.
This establishes His knowledge, which is a divine attribute.
3
Compare the clarity of the two proofs
Sanusi states that al-ihkaam is clearer. Ask yourself: why might the order of the universe be a more direct proof than the intricate reasoning about intention?
The proof from order points directly to a wise designer, while the proof from choice requires an extra step about intention.
4
Reflect on the significance for Islamic theology
Consider that both proofs are valid, but the proof from order is more accessible. This shows the importance of observing the cosmos as a sign of God's existence.
This connects to the Qur'anic call to reflect on creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ : فخرجَ مِنْ هذا أَنَّهُ يصح الاستدلال على كونه جل وعلا بوجهين : الإحكام والاختيارُ ، وأنَّ الأَوَّلَ أوضحُ مِنَ الثاني ووجه الاستدلال بالاختيار على ما قرَّرَهُ ابنُ التلمساني في ( شرح المعالم : (٣) : أَنَّهُ قد تقرَّرَ فيما مضى بالبراهين القاطعة أنَّ الله تعالى فاعل بالاختيار ، والفاعلُ بالاختيار لا بد وأن يكون قاصداً إلى ما يفعله ، والقصد إلى الشيء معَ الجهل به محال ، ولا يُتصوَّرُ القصدُ مِنَ اللهِ تعالى إلا مع العلم بالمقصودِ - وإِنْ كانَ يُتصوَّرُ مِنَ الحادثِ مَعَ العَقْدِ والظنِّ والوهم - ؛ لئلا يُتصوَّرَ مِنَ اللهِ تعالى بناءً على ذلك الاحتمال وقوع ذلك على خلافِ ما هو عليهِ ، وهو نقص يتعالى الله عنه ، فتعيَّنَ أنْ يكونَ عالماً
I say: From this it follows that it is valid to infer regarding Him, may He be exalted and glorified, in two ways: al-ihkaam (precision/order) and al-ikhtiyaar (choice/free will), and that the first is clearer than the second. The way of inference by al-ikhtiyaar, according to what Ibn al-Tilimsani established in (Sharh al-Ma'aalim): that it has been established previously by decisive proofs that Allah, exalted is He, acts by choice, and the agent by choice must intend what He does, and intending something while being ignorant of it is impossible, and intention from Allah, exalted is He, is only conceivable with knowledge of the intended – even if it is conceivable from the contingent being with resolve, conjecture, and illusion – lest it be conceived from Allah, exalted is He, based on that, the possibility of its occurrence contrary to what it is, which is a deficiency from which Allah is exalted; thus it is necessarily determined that He is knowing.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative explanation of two proofs for God's existence and the superiority of one over the other. Sanusi begins by stating his conclusion: that inference to God is valid through two ways – al-ihkaam (the order and precision in the universe) and al-ikhtiyaar (the agent's free will). He asserts that the first is clearer than the second. He then elaborates on the proof from al-ikhtiyaar by citing Ibn al-Tilimsani. The reasoning is: it has been established by decisive proofs that God acts by choice. An agent who acts by choice must intend his action. Intention cannot exist without knowledge of the intended object. From God, intention must be accompanied by certain knowledge, not mere assumption or conjecture, because attributing uncertainty to God is a deficiency. Therefore, God must be knowing. The proof from al-ihkaam is clearer because it directly indicates knowledge (as the order requires a wise designer), whereas al-ikhtiyaar requires an intermediate reasoning about intention.

Key Terms
الإحكام
الاختيار
علم
فاعل بالاختيار
قصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof from free will is invalid because it relies on human analogies.
Correction: Sanusi confirms it is valid, though less clear than the proof from order.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which of the two proofs for God's existence is clearer?
A) The proof from al-ikhtiyaar (free will)
B) The proof from al-ihkaam (order)
C) Both are equally clear
D) Neither is clear

Key Takeaway
The proof from order (al-ihkaam) is a clearer demonstration of God's existence than the proof from free will (al-ikhtiyaar), though both are valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the order in your daily life—from the regularity of sunrise to the structure of your own body—do you consciously treat it as a proof of God's existence, or do you pass by it without reflection? What specific moment today can you use as a reminder of al-ihkaam?

Think of a time when you acted with good intentions but without full knowledge, and things went wrong. How does that deficiency highlight God's perfection in the proof from al-ikhtiyaar? How can you train yourself to trust that God's knowledge is complete, even when events perplex you?
?
Sanusi says the proof from order is clearer. Which of these two proofs actually moves your heart more? Be honest: do you rely more on intellectual arguments or on experiencing God's design? What concrete step will you take this week to strengthen your conviction in the existence of God?

The Human Body: A Composition of Four Elements
Sanusi presents a brief illustration of divine wisdom in the human body's composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that the complex structure of the human body arising from four simple elements points to a wise Creator.

Core Idea
Four simple elements produce a complex human body—evidence of a wise Creator.

Sanusi begins drawing our attention to the wonders of human creation. He points out that our bodies are not random; they are composed from just four basic elements, which then differentiate into all our organs and tissues. This observation serves as a starting point for recognizing the intricate design and order in creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two parts: composition from four elements and differentiation into specific tissues.
The passage is a brief indication, not a full proof.
2
Reflect on the claim
Think about how such a simple starting point (four elements) can lead to the complexity of a human body. What does this suggest about the one who designed it?
This is a classic argument from design.
3
Consider the common misunderstanding
This is not a medical theory but a philosophical illustration. Sanusi is using it to point to divine wisdom, not to teach anatomy.
4
Connect to the bigger picture
Think about how this fits into the lesson on divine existence and causality. The order in creation points to an orderly Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage is an introductory statement for an illustration of divine wisdom in creation. These four then differentiate and form various tissues: bone, brain, nerve, veins, blood, flesh, and skin. The implication is that this complex and orderly structure from simple elements points to a wise Creator. The passage is a starting point for a longer exposition; here it is cut short by the end of the page.

Allah's Existence
beginner
6 min
The wonders of the human body as proof of divine wisdom
Sanusi enumerates the components of the human body and argues for their precise design.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intricate design of the human body—from bones to blood—demonstrates the wisdom and intentional creation of Allah.

Core Idea
Every part of your body works perfectly because it was designed with precise wisdom.

Sanusi invites us to reflect on our own bodies as evidence of divine craftsmanship. He walks through the construction of the human frame, showing how each component—bones, nerves, marrow, flesh, veins, and blood—is placed with exact purpose. This lesson follows his argument that any deviation would break the body's function, pointing to a wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the literal English translation. Notice the rhythm of Sanusi's description.
The Arabic has some unclear spots due to OCR, but the overall argument is clear.
2
Identify the basic composition
Sanusi begins by stating the body is composed of four elements (earth, water, air, fire) which differentiate into various parts. List the parts he mentions.
These parts are: bone, marrow, nerve, vein, blood, flesh, skin, nail, hair.
3
Trace the argument for each part
Follow Sanusi's explanation for bones, nerves, marrow, flesh, veins, and blood. For each, note the specific 'wisdom' (hikma) he highlights.
For example: bones are jointed to allow movement, marrow moistens bones, etc.
4
Notice the conditional reasoning
Sanusi uses 'if' statements: if bones were one piece, if nerves were stronger, if marrow were not moist, etc. These show that any change would disrupt the body's function 'by custom' (bi-ḥasab al-ʿāda).
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The overall argument is that such precise, interdependent design cannot be accidental—it must be the work of a wise Creator. Consider how this fits into the broader proof of God's existence.
This is a classic teleological argument (design argument).

Source Text

بعضُ ما أُودِعَ في الإنسانِ مِنَ العجائب 
وبيان ما ذكر على سبيل الإشارة أن نقول : جسد الإنسانِ مُركَّبٌ مِنْ أصول أربعة : الأرضُ ، والماء ، والهواء ، والنار ، ثم تفصلَتْ هذهِ الأربعةُ إلى العظم والمخ والعصب والعروق والدم واللحم والجلد والظُّفُرِ والشَّعَرِ ، وُضِعَ كلُّ واحدٍ منها لحكمة لولاها لم يكن الجسد بحسَب العادةِ ؛ فالعظام منها هي عمود الجسدِ ، فضُمَّ بعضُها إلى بعض بمفاصل وأقفالٍ مِنَ العضلاتِ والعصبِ رُبطَتْ بها ، ولم تُجعَلْ عظماً واحداً ؛ لأنَّهُ كانَ يكون مثل الحجرِ ومثل الخشبة ، لا يتحرَّكُ ولا يجلس ولا يقومُ ، ولا يركع ولا يسجد لخالقه الواحد الأحد القيوم ، وجُعِلَ العصبُ على مقدارٍ مخصوص ، لو كان أقوى ممَّا هو لم تصح عادةً حركة الجسم ولا تصرُّفه في منافعِهِ 
ثم خلق تعالى المخ في العظام في غاية الرطوبةِ ؛ ليرطِّبَ يُبْسَ العظام وشدَّتَها ، ولتقوى العظام برطوبتِهِ ، ولولا ذلك لضعُفَتْ قُوَّتُها ، وانخرمَ نظام الجسدِ لضعفها بحسَبِ مجرى العادة . ثم خلقَ اللحم وعبأَهُ على العظام ، وسدَّ بِهِ خَلَلَ الجسدِ كلَّه فصار مستوياً لحمةً واحدةً ، واعتدلَتْ هيئة الجسدِ بهِ واستوت 
ثم خلق العروق في جميع الجسدِ جداويلَ لجريان الغذاء فيها إلى أركان الجسدِ ، لكل موضعِ مِنَ الجسدِ عدد معلوم مِنَ العروقِ صغاراً وكباراً ؛ ليأخذ الصغيرُ مِنَ الغذاءِ حاجته والكبير حاجته ، ولو [؟] عليهِ أو أنقص ، أو على غير ما هي عليهِ مِنَ الترتيب.. ما صحَّ مِنَ الجسد بحسَب العادةِ شيء .
ثم أجرى الدم في العروقِ سيَّالاً خاثراً ، ولو كانَ يابساً أو أكثف
Some of the wonders placed in man. And an explanation of what was mentioned by way of indication: We say: The human body is composed of four elements: earth, water, air, and fire. Then these four differentiate into bone, marrow, nerve, vein, blood, flesh, skin, nail, and hair. Each one is placed for a wisdom, without which the body would not be as it is by custom. The bones are the pillar of the body; they are joined to each other with joints and locks from muscles and nerves tied to them. They were not made one bone because it would then be like stone and wood, not moving, not sitting, not standing, not bowing, not prostrating to its Creator, the One, the Unique, the Self-Subsisting. The nerve was made of a specific measure; if it were stronger than it is, the movement of the body and its use for benefits would not be sound by custom. Then the Exalted created the marrow in the bones extremely moist to moisten the dryness and hardness of the bones, and so that the bones become strong with its moisture. Without that, their strength would weaken and the system of the body would be disrupted due to its weakness according to the course of custom. Then He created flesh and arranged it on the bones, filling all the gaps of the body, so it became even as one mass, and the shape of the body became balanced and straight with it. Then He created veins in all the body as canals for the flow of nourishment to the limbs of the body. For every place of the body there is a known number of veins, small and large, so that the small takes its need of nourishment and the large takes its need. If they were more than they are or less, or upon other than the arrangement they are upon, nothing of the body would be sound by custom. Then He made the blood flow in the veins, fluid yet thick, and if it were dry or thicker...
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a teleological argument from design, specifically from the intricate construction of the human body. Sanusi begins by listing the four basic elements composing the body (earth, water, air, fire) and their differentiation into various parts: bone, marrow, nerve, vein, blood, flesh, skin, nail, hair. He then explains that each part is placed for a specific wisdom (hikma) without which the body would not function normally. He details the bones as the pillar, joined with joints and ligaments; if it were one bone it would be immobile, preventing actions like prostration. Nerves are of a precise strength. Marrow is moist to balance the dryness of bones. Flesh fills gaps. Veins are arranged in a network with appropriate sizes to distribute nourishment. Finally, blood is of a specific consistency. The argument emphasizes that if any part were different in amount or arrangement, the body would not work properly, thus proving deliberate design by a wise Creator. The fragmented ending suggests a continuation about blood consistency.

Misconception: The complexity of the human body could have arisen by chance or through evolution without purposeful design.
Correction: Sanusi argues that each part has a specific function such that any variation—even slight—would render the body non-functional 'by custom', which strongly suggests a wise, intentional Creator.

?

?
The passage ends abruptly but the argument is clear: if blood were drier or thicker, the body would not work. Your own life depends on a perfect consistency. Does this awareness change how you see your body? How can you honor its Creator today in a specific, concrete act of worship or service?

Wisdom in the Design of Bones and Marrow
Sanusi explains the wisdom behind the creation of bones with joints and the marrow within them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the creation of bones with joints and moist marrow demonstrates divine wisdom and purpose in the human body.

Core Idea
Every detail of the body, from joints to marrow, is designed for a specific purpose.

Sanusi draws our attention to the intricate design of the human body, particularly the bones and marrow. He argues that if bones were a single solid piece, movement and worship would be impossible. The joints and the moist marrow each serve a necessary function, pointing to a wise Creator who placed everything with precision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from general principle to specific examples.
2
Identify the two main examples
The passage focuses on bones (with joints and nerves) and marrow. What wisdom does Sanusi attribute to each?
Bones are the pillar, marrow moistens.
3
Understand the argument against a single bone
Sanusi says if bones were one piece, they'd be like stone – no movement, no worship. Why is movement essential for worship?
This shows design facilitates religious obligations.
4
Connect to the larger purpose
Reflect on how every part has a function that supports life and worship. How does this point to a wise Creator?
The argument is teleological: design implies designer.

والظُّفُرِ والشَّعَرِ ، وُضِعَ كلُّ واحدٍ منها لحكمة لولاها لم يكن الجسد بحسب العادةِ ؛ فالعظام منها هي عمود الجسدِ ، فضُمَّ بعضُها إلى بعض بمفاصل وأقفالٍ مِنَ العضلاتِ والعصبِ رُبطَتْ بها ، ولم تُجعَلْ عظماً واحداً ؛ لأنَّهُ كانَ يكون مثل الحجرِ ومثل الخشبة ، لا يتحرَّكُ ولا يجلس ولا يقومُ ، ولا يركع ولا يسجد لخالقه الواحد الأحد القيوم ، وجُعِلَ العصبُ على مقدارٍ مخصوص ، لو كان أقوى ممَّا هو لم تصح عادةً حركة الجسم ولا تصرُّفه في منافعِهِ

ثم خلق تعالى المخ في العظام في غاية الرطوبةِ ؛ ليرطِّبَ يُبْسَ العظام وشدَّتَها ، ولتقوى العظام برطوبتِهِ ، ولولا ذلك لضعُفَتْ قُوَّتُها ، وانخرمَ نظام الجسدِ لضعفها بحسَبِ مجرى العادة .
And the nails and hair, each one of them is placed for a wisdom without which the body would not be as is customary; so the bones among them are the pillar of the body, and some of them were joined to others with joints and locks of muscles and nerves bound to them, and they were not made one bone; because it would be like stone and like wood, not moving nor sitting nor standing, nor bowing nor prostrating to its Creator, the One, the Unique, the Self-Subsisting. And the nerves were created in a specific measure; if they were stronger than they are, the movement of the body and its disposal in its benefits would not be valid customarily. Then the Exalted created the marrow inside the bones in utmost moisture; to moisten the dryness and hardness of the bones, and so that the bones are strengthened by its moisture; and were it not for that, their strength would weaken, and the system of the body would be disrupted due to their weakness according to the customary course.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents a teleological argument from the design of the human body, focusing on bones and marrow. The author begins by stating a general principle: each part (starting with nails and hair) is placed for a specific wisdom, without which the body would not function normally. He then takes the bones as an example. The bones are the pillar of the body, but they are not created as a single bone. Instead, they are joined with joints and locked with muscles and nerves. If they were one solid bone, like stone or wood, the body could not move, sit, stand, or perform acts of worship like bowing and prostrating to God. This shows that the design facilitates religious obligations. The nerves are created with a precise measure: if they were stronger, movement and use of the body's benefits would be impeded. Next, the author discusses the marrow, created extremely moist to counteract the dryness and hardness of the bones, strengthening them. Without this moisture, the bones would weaken and the body's system would break down under normal conditions. Thus, every detail has a purpose pointing to the Creator's wisdom.

Wisdom in Creation of Flesh, Veins, and Blood
Sanusi enumerates three bodily systems and their design features.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the specific designs of flesh, veins, and blood each serve distinct functions, demonstrating divine wisdom and intentionality in creation.

Core Idea
Every detail of our body shows precise design pointing to the Creator.

Sanusi continues his teleological argument by examining three more bodily systems: flesh, veins, and blood. Each is created with specific properties to fulfill its role perfectly—flesh covers and protects, veins channel nourishment, and blood has the right consistency to flow and nourish. This careful design excludes the possibility of random chance and points to a wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice how Sanusi describes each system's function and the conditions that would break it.
The passage has three distinct parts: flesh, veins, blood.
2
Identify the three systems
Find where Sanusi discusses flesh, veins, and blood. For each, note the specific property that makes it work (e.g., flesh fills gaps, veins are channels, blood is fluid and thick).
3
Understand the conditions
For veins and blood, Sanusi states what would happen if their properties were different. Ask yourself: Why does this strengthen the argument for design?
The conditional statements highlight that the current state is not arbitrary.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how these three examples together build a cumulative case. The precision of each system points to intentional planning rather than chance.
This is part of the broader teleological argument for God's existence.

ثم خلقَ اللحم وعبأَهُ على العظام ، وسدَّ بِهِ خَلَلَ الجسدِ كلَّه فصار مستوياً لحمةً واحدةً ، واعتدلَتْ هيئة الجسدِ بهِ واستوت

ثم خلق العروق في جميع الجسدِ جداويلَ لجريان الغذاء فيها إلى أركان الجسدِ ، لكل موضعِ مِنَ الجسدِ عدد معلوم مِنَ العروقِ صغاراً وكباراً ؛ ليأخذ الصغيرُ مِنَ الغذاءِ حاجته والكبير حاجته ، ولو [؟] عليهِ أو أنقص ، أو على غير ما هي عليهِ مِنَ [؟] كانَتْ أكثرَ ممَّا هي الترتيب.. ما صحَّ مِنَ الجسد بحسَب العادةِ شيء .

Then He created the flesh and arranged it upon the bones, and sealed with it all the gaps of the body, so it became level as one mass of flesh, and the form of the body became balanced and even with it. Then He created the veins in all the body as channels for the flow of nourishment in them to the parts of the body; for every place of the body a known number of veins, small and large; so that the small takes from the nourishment its need and the large its need. And if [?] it or less, or upon other than what it is of [?] it were more than it is in order... nothing of the body would be sound according to custom. Then He made the blood flow in the veins fluid and thick; and if it were dry or thicker than it is, it would not flow in the veins; and if it were thinner than it is, the organs would not be nourished by it.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage continues the teleological argument, covering three more body systems: flesh, veins, and blood. First, the flesh is created to cover the bones and fill all gaps, resulting in a uniform, balanced form. This provides both protection and aesthetic harmony. Second, the veins are created as channels (like streams) to carry nourishment to all parts of the body. Each organ has a precise number of veins, both small and large, so each can receive its required amount. The author notes a conditional: if the veins were different in number or arrangement (the OCR is corrupted but the idea is clear), the body would not function properly. Third, blood is created with a specific consistency: it is fluid enough to flow in the veins yet thick enough to nourish the organs. If it were dry or thicker, it would not flow; if it were thinner, it would not provide nourishment. Thus, every detail of creation, from the structure of veins to the viscosity of blood, demonstrates divine wisdom and design.

Wisdom in Creation of Skin, Hair, Eyebrows, and Eyelashes
Sanusi details the protective and functional design of skin, hair, eyebrows, and eyelashes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific wisdoms behind the creation of skin, hair, eyebrows, and eyelashes as presented in Sanusi's argument for divine design.

Core Idea
Every detail of creation, from skin to eyelashes, serves a purpose and points to a wise Creator.

Sanusi continues his argument for divine wisdom by examining specific features of the human body. In this passage, he explains how the skin, hair, eyebrows, and eyelashes are perfectly designed for protection, comfort, and functionality. This lesson will guide you through each detail, showing how they reflect intentional design.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Let the author's descriptive style come through before analyzing.
Notice the repeated phrase 'لولا ذلك' which signals the conditional reasoning.
2
Identify the functions of skin and hair
Sanusi first describes the skin as a container for flesh, preventing it from being a red crust. Then hair is for protection and adornment, with clothing as substitute where absent.
3
Examine the design of hair roots
Note how roots are embedded in flesh and made soft, not needle-like, for comfort. This reflects intentional care in creation.
The text explicitly says 'life would not be pleasant' if roots were hard.
4
Analyze the eyebrows and eyelashes
They protect the eye from dust and falling objects. Their design allows easy raising to see and lowering to shield from harmful sights.
Also, they are not a single layer, allowing vision through them.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider how each detail—skin, hair, eyebrows, eyelashes—shows purposeful design. This points to a wise Creator who cares for human well-being.
This passage is part of a larger argument for divine existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم كسا اللحم بالجلدِ ليسترَهُ كلَّهُ كالوعاء له ، ولولا ذلك لكانَ قِشْراً أحمر ، وفي ذلك هلاكه عادةً .

ثم كساهُ الشَّعَرَ وقايةً للجلدِ وزينةً في بعض المواضع ، وما لم يكنْ فِيهِ شَعَرٌ جعلَ لهُ اللباس عوضاً منه ، وجعل أصوله مغروزةً في اللحم ؛ ليتم الانتفاعُ ببقائه ، ولَيَّنَ أصوله ولم يجعلها يابسة مثل رؤوس الإِبَرِ ؛ إذْ لو كانَتْ كذلكَ لم يَهْنَهُ عيش ، وجعلَ الحواجب والأشفار وقايةً للعين ، ولولا ذلك لأهلكها الغبارُ والسَّقَطُ ، وجعلَها على وجه يتمكَّنُ بسهولةٍ مِنْ رفعِها عن الناظرِ عند قصدِ النظرِ ، ومِنْ إرخائها على جميع العين عند إرادة إمساكِهِ النظر إلى ما تؤذي رؤيتُهُ ديناً أو دنيا ، ولم يجعلْ شَعَرَها طَبَقاً واحداً لينظرَ مِنْ خِلالِها
Then He clothed the flesh with the skin to cover it entirely, like a container for it; and were it not for that, it would be a red crust, and therein would be its destruction customarily. Then He clothed it with hair as protection for the skin and adornment in some places, and wherever there was no hair, He made clothing as a substitute for it. And He made its roots embedded in the flesh so that benefit from its remaining is complete, and He softened its roots and did not make them dry like the tips of needles; for if they were like that, life would not be pleasant for him. And He made the eyebrows and eyelashes as protection for the eye; and were it not for that, dust and falling objects would destroy it. And He made them in a manner that one can easily raise them from the eye when intending to look, and lower them over the entire eye when wanting to withhold the gaze from something harmful to see, whether in religion or worldly matters. And He did not make their hair a single layer so that one could see through them.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage details the wisdom in the creation of the skin, hair, eyebrows, and eyelashes. First, the skin is described as a covering for the flesh, like a container. Without it, the flesh would be exposed as a red crust, leading to destruction under normal circumstances. Thus, skin is essential for protection. Next, hair is created as a protective layer for the skin and also for adornment in certain areas. Where hair is absent, clothing is provided as a substitute. The roots of hair are embedded in the flesh and are soft, not like sharp needles, to ensure comfort. The author then focuses on the eyebrows and eyelashes. They protect the eyes from dust and falling objects. Their design allows easy movement: they can be raised to see or lowered to shield the eyes from harmful sights, whether religiously or worldly harmful. Importantly, the hair of the eyelashes is not a single solid layer but allows vision through them. This shows intricate design for both protection and functionality.

Correction: The passage emphasizes hair's protective function and the deliberate softness of its roots for comfort, showing intentional design.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Wisdom in Creation of Lips, Teeth, and Tongue
Sanusi explains the functions of lips, teeth, and tongue, including the delayed creation of teeth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the divine wisdom behind the specific functions and timing of creation of the lips, teeth, and tongue.

Core Idea
Every part of the body is created with precise function and timing, reflecting divine wisdom.

Sanusi continues his exposition of divine wisdom in human creation by examining the lips, teeth, and tongue. He shows how each organ's design and even its timing of appearance (like teeth) serve specific purposes, from protection to nourishment to beauty. This passage invites us to reflect on the meticulous planning in Allah's creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage ends mid-sentence, indicating continuation on the next page.
2
Identify the functions of each organ
The passage describes lips, teeth, and tongue. For each, note what purpose they serve according to Sanusi.
3
Focus on the delayed creation of teeth
Sanusi gives two reasons why teeth are not created at birth. Find them and understand the wisdom behind them.
This is the main doctrinal point of the passage.
4
Reflect on divine care
Ask yourself: how does this passage show Allah's care for both the mother and the child? The commentary will help you connect the dots.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم خلقَ شفتينِ ينطبقانِ على الفم ، يصونان الحلق والفم مِنَ الرياح والغبار ، وينفتحان بسهولة عند الحاجة إلى الانفتاح ، ولما فيهما أيضاً مِنْ كمال الزينة وغيرها

خلق بعدهما الأسنانَ ؛ ليتمكَّنَ بها مِنْ قطع مأكولِهِ وطحنِهِ ، وجعلَ اللسانَ آلةٌ يجمعُ بهِ ما تفرَّقَ مِنَ المأكولِ في أرجاء الفم ليتمكَّنَ تسهيله للابتلاع بطحْنِ الأرحاء ، وخلق فيه معنى الذوق لكلَّ مأكول ومشروب ، ولم يخلق جلَّ وعلا الأسنانَ في أَوَّلِ الخِلقةِ ؛ لئلا يضرَّ بأُمِّهِ في حالِ رضاعِهِ بالعضُ ، ولأنَّه لا يحتاجُ لها حينَئِذٍ ؛ لضَعَفِهِ عمَّا كثفَ مِنَ الأغذية التي تفتقرُ إلى الأسنان ، فلما ترعرع وصلح
Then He created two lips that close upon the mouth, protecting the throat and mouth from winds and dust, and they open easily when there is need to open, and also for the perfection of adornment and other things. He created after them the teeth, so that one can cut and grind his food by them. And He made the tongue an instrument by which he gathers what is scattered of the food in the corners of the mouth to facilitate its swallowing by the grinding of the molars. And He created in it the sense of taste for every food and drink. And He, glorified and exalted, did not create the teeth at the beginning of creation, lest the child harm his mother during his nursing by biting, and because he does not need them at that time due to his weakness for the coarse foods that require teeth. Then, when he grew up and became sound...
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage discusses the wisdom behind the creation of the lips, teeth, and tongue. The lips are designed to close over the mouth, protecting the throat and mouth from wind and dust, yet they open easily when needed. They also contribute to beauty and adornment. Next, the teeth are created after the lips for cutting and grinding food. The tongue is described as an instrument that gathers scattered food in the mouth to facilitate swallowing after the molars have ground it. Additionally, the tongue is endowed with the sense of taste for all foods and drinks. A significant point is that teeth are not created at the very beginning of life. This is to prevent the infant from harming the mother's breast while nursing, and because the infant initially only needs milk, not solid food that requires chewing. As the child grows and becomes ready for solid food, teeth appear. This demonstrates divine consideration for the mother's well-being and the child's developmental needs. The passage ends mid-sentence, noting that teeth come when the child grows up and becomes sound.

Key Terms
شفتان
أسنان
لسان
ذوق
رضاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Teeth should be present from birth since they are needed for eating solid foods.
Correction: The passage explains that teeth are not created at birth to prevent harm to the mother during breastfeeding and because the infant does not need solid food initially.

Checkpoint
Why were teeth not created at the very beginning of a person's life?
A) To make room for more teeth later
B) To prevent the infant from biting the mother while nursing
C) Because the infant has no mouth
D) Because they are not needed for speech

Key Takeaway
The delayed creation of teeth demonstrates Allah's wisdom in protecting the mother and accommodating the infant's developmental needs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Allah delayed your teeth to protect your mother from bites during nursing, does your treatment of your mother reflect the care He built into creation? What specific change can you make this week to honor her?
?
Are there aspects of your body or life that you have complained about, not realizing they are timed perfectly by a Wise Creator? Will you now trust His timing instead of grumbling?
?
The tongue gathers food and tastes—do you use your tongue more to gather blessings or to spread harm? How can you redirect its use to thank Allah and benefit others starting today?
