Both Wills Ineffective: Three Impossibilities
Sanusi enumerates three impossibilities when both gods' wills are ineffective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two gods cannot both have ineffective wills, and the three irrational consequences that follow.

Core Idea
If two gods' wills are both ineffective, they are impotent.

Sanusi continues his proof against two gods by examining the scenario where both gods' wills are ineffective. He argues that this leads to three absurdities: both gods being powerless, the subject being empty of the two contradictories, and either the act existing and not existing by both or prevention without a preventer. This lesson unpacks those three impossibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the first part of Sanusi's argument, focusing on the case where both wills are ineffective.
Notice the three impossibilities he lists.
2
Understand the premise
Sanusi assumes two gods differ in their will for the same action. Grasp why effective coexistence of both wills leads to contradiction.
This sets up the need for at least one will to be ineffective.
3
Identify the three impossibilities
List the three impossibilities Sanusi enumerates when both wills are ineffective: (1) both gods impotent, (2) subject empty of both contradictories, (3) either existence and non-existence by both or prevention without preventer.
These are all absurd.
4
Reflect on the logical flow
Trace how Sanusi deduces these impossibilities step by step. Notice the use of reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فوجه بطلان الطرفِ الأَوَّلِ ـ وهو الاختلافُ - أن نقول : لوِ في الفعل ؛ بأنْ يريد أحدهما وجودَ الجوهر ويريد الآخرُ عدمه ، أو يريد أحدهما حركتهُ والآخرُ تسكينَهُ.. لَلزِمَ عجزهما أو عجزُ أحدهما ، معَ زيادة مستحيلاتٍ سنذكرها ؛ وذلك لأنَّ نفوذ إرادتهما معاً مستحيل ؛ لما يؤدّي إليهِ مِن اجتماع النقيضين أو ما في حكمهما فيكون الجوهرُ في الزمانِ الواحدِ موجوداً معدوماً ، أو متحركاً ساكناً ، وذلكَ لا يُعقَلُ، فإذاً لا بدَّ مِنْ تعطّل النفوذ لإحدى الإرادتين أو لكليهما، فإن تعطَّلَنا معاً لزم عجز الإلهين بتعذّر الفعل مِنْ كلّ واحدٍ منهما ويلزم أيضاً عليه خلو المحل عن النقيضين ، وأيضاً فلا مانع مِنْ نفوذ إرادة كلّ واحدٍ منهما وقدرتِهِ .. إلا نفوذ إرادة الآخرِ وقدرته ، فإذا لم تنفذ الإرادتانِ لزم وجود الفعل بهما وعدم وجودِهِ بهما إِنْ ثبت المانع ، أو حصول المنعِ مِنْ غيرِ مانعِ إِنْ لم يثبتِ المانع . فهذهِ ثلاثة أوجه مِنَ الاستحالاتِ ، كلُّها تلزم على تقديرِ تعطيل الإرادتين وأَمَّا إِنْ كانَتْ إرادة أحدهما خاصَّةً هي المتعلقة : فيستحيلُ مِنْ أوجه : أحدها : أَنَّهُ يلزمُ عليهِ عدمُ عموم تعلقِ إرادة الإله وقدرته ، وقد سبق أنَّ ذلك مستحيل ، وإذا استحال لم يمكن أن يكونَ أحد الإلهين أقدرَ مِنَ الآخر ثانيها : أنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ عجزُ مَنْ لم تنفذ إرادته مع كونه إلها ! والعجز على الإله محال لما سيأتي ثالثها : أنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ عجزُ الإلهِ الذي نفذَتْ إرادتُهُ أيضاً ؛ لأنَّهُما مثلان ، فيجب لأحدهما ما وجبَ للآخرِ رابعها : الترجيحُ لأحد المثلين على مثلِهِ بصفةٍ مِنْ غيرِ مُرجح فإِنْ فُرِضَ المرجّحُ لزمَ حدوثهما ، ونقلنا الكلامَ إلى الثالث ، ولزمَ التسلسل
Then the aspect of the invalidity of the first side—which is difference—is that we say: if [they differ] in the act, such that one wills the existence of the atom and the other wills its non-existence, or one wills its motion and the other its rest, then their impotence or the impotence of one of them would be necessary, along with additional impossibilities we will mention. That is because the concurrent effectiveness of both wills is impossible due to what it would entail of the conjunction of two contradictories or what is in their ruling; thus the atom would be, in one time, existent and non-existent, or moving and still, and that is inconceivable. Therefore, the ineffectiveness of one of the two wills or of both must occur. If both are ineffective, the impotence of the two gods follows from the impossibility of action from each of them. Also necessary upon this is the vacuity of the subject from the two contradictories. Also, there is no preventer of the effectiveness of the will and power of each except the effectiveness of the will and power of the other. So if the two wills are not effective, then either the existence of the act by both and its non-existence by both is necessary if the preventer is established, or the occurrence of prevention without a preventer if the preventer is not established. These are three aspects of impossibilities, all necessary on the assumption of the ineffectiveness of the two wills. And as for if the will of one of them is specifically the one that is effective, then it is impossible from several aspects: First: that it entails the lack of universality of the attachment of the will and power of the god, and it has preceded that this is impossible. And if it is impossible, then it cannot be that one of the two gods is more powerful than the other. Second: that it entails the impotence of the one whose will is not effective despite being a god, and impotence upon a god is impossible as will come. Third: that it entails the impotence of the god whose will is effective as well, because they are equals, so what is necessary for one is necessary for the other. Fourth: the preponderance of one of the two equals over its equal by a quality without a preponderant, and if the preponderant is assumed, their temporality is necessary, and we would transfer the discussion to a third, and infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This is a proof by contradiction against the existence of two gods. Sanusi first considers what would happen if two gods differed in their will regarding a single act (e.g., one wants a substance to exist, the other wants it not to exist). He argues that if both wills were effective, contradictory states would coexist (e.g., the substance would be both existent and non-existent at the same time), which is absurd. Therefore, at least one will must be ineffective. If both are ineffective, both gods are impotent, which is impossible for a god. If only one is ineffective, then that god is impotent, and the other also becomes impotent because they are equals. Additionally, preferring one equal over another without a preponderant leads to infinite regress. Thus, difference between gods is impossible.

الإلهين
الإرادة
القدرة
عجز
نفوذ
اجتماع النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible for two gods to both have omnipotence even if they disagree.
Correction: Sanusi shows that disagreement leads to either both being impotent or one impotent, contradicting omnipotence.

Checkpoint
What is the first impossibility Sanusi mentions when both wills are ineffective?
A) Both gods become powerless.
B) One god overpowers the other.
C) The atom becomes eternal.
D) The universe ceases to exist.

Key Takeaway
Two gods with differing wills cannot both be omnipotent; if both wills fail, three absurdities follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God is one and alone in His power, does your daily life reflect that trust, or do you sometimes act as if other forces (people, systems, fate) have independent power alongside Him? What would change if you fully internalized this lesson's conclusion that only one omnipotent will can exist?
?
When you face a situation where your will conflicts with another's, do you instinctively assume that one of you must be right and the other wrong, as the world would be absurd otherwise? How does this lesson's logic about two gods inform your understanding of conflict resolution in human relationships?
?
The argument shows that two opposite wills cannot both be effective. Where in your own life are you trying to hold onto contradictory commitments—like desiring both worldly success and spiritual detachment—expecting both to succeed? What would it mean to let one go and align your will with God's?

Only One Will Effective: Four Impossibilities
Sanusi gives four reasons why only one effective will also impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the case where only one god's will is effective also leads to impossibility, through four specific arguments.

Core Idea
If one god's will alone is effective, that god is still not truly omnipotent.

After showing that both wills being ineffective is impossible, Sanusi turns to the alternative: one will is effective and the other is not. He argues that this too leads to absurdities. In this lesson, we explore the four impossibilities he lists, including the lack of universal will, the impotence of the ineffective god, the consequent impotence of the effective god due to equality, and the problem of preponderance without a preponderant.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'And as for if the will of one of them is specifically the one that is effective...' to the end.
This section covers four aspects.
2
Understand the first impossibility
If only one will is effective, that god's will is not universal (does not apply to all things), contradicting omnipotence.
Sanusi references a prior proof that God's will is universal.
3
Analyze the second and third impossibilities
The god with ineffective will is impotent; but since the two are equals, the effective god is also impotent by parity.
Equality (مثلان) is key.
4
Examine the fourth impossibility
Preferring one equal over another without a preponderant leads to infinite regress, which is impossible.
This is a classic kalam argument.

Impossibility of Agreement Between Two Gods
Sanusi argues that agreement between two gods leads to coercion and impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that necessary agreement between two gods would imply coercion, contradicting divine freedom.

Core Idea
Necessary agreement between two gods would make them coerced, not free — impossible for true divinity.

Sanusi continues the refutation of multiple gods by examining whether they could always agree. He argues that if their agreement is necessary, then each is coerced and not free, which contradicts the essence of God as the Freely Acting Creator. This passage lays out the logical consequences of such a scenario.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two types of agreement
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Sanusi divides agreement into obligatory (واجب) and possible (جائز).
This division is the foundation of his argument.
2
Understand the argument for obligatory agreement
Focus on the consequences of obligatory agreement: each god would be coerced and not free. Consider the two subcases: both unable to oppose, or one able and the other not.
Coercion follows in both subcases.
3
Reflect on the Quranic citation and further implications
Note the citation of Quran 28:68 to emphasize divine freedom. Then consider the additional points: coercion of one implies coercion of the other, the need for a preponderant, and the conversion of possible to impossible.
These reinforce the absurdity of obligatory agreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بطلانُ الطرف الثاني مِنَ التالي ـ وهو الاتفاقُ ـ فمِنْ أوجه : وذلكَ أنَّ الاتفاق : إمَّا أنْ يكونَ واجباً أو جائزاً ، ويلزم في الاتفاقِ الواجب أن يكونَ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما مقهوراً غير مختارِ إنْ كانَ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما لا يَقدِرُ على مخالفة الآخرِ ، وإنْ كانَ أَحدهما يَقدِرُ عليها دونَ الآخرِ .. لزمَ قهرُ الذي لا يَقدِرُ عليها ، ونفي كونه مختاراً ؛ لأنَّ المختار هو الذي يتأتّى منهُ الفعلُ والتركُ ، فإذا كانَ اتفاقهما معاً ، أو أحدهما واجباً .. لم يتأتَ مِنَ المجبورِ منهما ترْكُ ما اختارَهُ الآخرُ ، كيف وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَختارُ ﴾ [القصص : ٦٨] ؟ ! وأيضاً : يلزمُ مِنْ قهْرِ أحدهما قهْرُ الآخرِ ؛ لأنَّه مثله ، ويلزمُ الافتقار إلى المرجّح في تخصيص أحدِ المثلين بما لم يثبت لمثلِهِ ويلزم أيضاً في الاتفاقِ الواجب : انقلاب الممكن مستحيلاً ؛ لأنَّ كل واحدٍ منهما إذا نظرنا إليهِ منفرداً أمكن أن يُوجِدَ كلاً مِنَ الحركة
And as for the invalidity of the second side of the consequent—which is agreement—it is from several aspects: That is because agreement: either it is obligatory or possible. In obligatory agreement, it is necessary that each of them is coerced, not free, if each of them is unable to oppose the other. And if one is able to oppose without the other, then coercion of the one who is unable follows, and denial of his being free; because the free one is he from whom both action and leaving are possible. So if their agreement together or that of one is obligatory, then leaving what the other chose is not possible for the coerced one from among them. How, and 'your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' (Quran 28:68)? Also, from the coercion of one follows the coercion of the other because he is like him, and need for a preponderant is required in specifying one of the equals with what is not established for its like. Also, in obligatory agreement is required the conversion of the possible into impossible; because each of them, when we look at him individually, it is possible for him to bring into existence the whole motion...
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of two gods by examining the possibility that they always agree. Sanusi divides agreement into two types: necessary (obligatory) and possible. If their agreement is necessary, then each is coerced and not free (since they cannot oppose each other). If one can oppose but the other cannot, the one who cannot is coerced and not free. He cites Quran 28:68 to emphasize that God creates and chooses freely. Furthermore, coercion of one implies coercion of the other because they are equals, leading to the need for an external preponderant to specify one equal over the other, which would entail infinite regress or temporal origination. He also points out that necessary agreement turns what is possible (independent action) into impossible, which is absurd. The argument is incomplete in this window but lays out key inconsistencies.

Key Terms
الاتفاق
واجب
جائز
مقهور
مختار
قهر
المرجح
انقلاب الممكن مستحيلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the argument proves that God is coerced.
Correction: The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: it shows that if there were two gods with necessary agreement, they would be coerced, which is impossible for God. Thus, multiple gods cannot exist.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is a necessary consequence of obligatory agreement between two gods?
A) Both are fully free.
B) Each is coerced and not free.
C) The universe becomes eternal.
D) One god submits to the other.

Key Takeaway
Necessary agreement between two gods would require them to be coerced, not free, which contradicts the nature of God and is therefore impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are pulled between what Allah commands and your own desires, do you truly believe that only Allah has the absolute freedom to choose, or do you act as if your own choices are independent of His will? How can you deliberately submit your choices to His authority today?
?
Consider the idols of the heart—people, status, or comfort—that you sometimes treat as if they have power over your life. How does this proof that necessary agreement between two gods is impossible strengthen your conviction that Allah alone deserves your complete trust and obedience? What specific step will you take to dethrone a false 'god' in your life this week?
?
Sanusi argues that coercion contradicts divine freedom. In your own life, you often feel coerced by circumstances, expectations, or your own habits. How does recognizing that Allah alone is perfectly free and uncoerced change the way you view your struggles? Will you surrender your sense of being 'forced' to Allah and trust that His choice for you is the only truly free and good one?

Four Arguments Against the Overpowering Scenario (al-Taghalub)
Sanusi presents four arguments refuting the scenario where one god overpowers the other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical arguments Sanusi uses to refute the possibility of two gods where one overpowers the other.

Core Idea
The assumption of multiple gods leads to logical absurdities.

Sanusi now tackles a key objection to divine unity: what if two gods existed and one overpowered the other? This might seem plausible, but Sanusi shows through four arguments that this scenario leads to impossibility. Each argument builds on the last, ultimately demonstrating that monotheism is rationally necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without rushing. Let the flow of Sanusi's reasoning sink in. Then read the translation to ensure you understand the literal meaning.
Notice that the arguments are numbered: 'second', 'third', 'fourth' – the first is implied in the initial statement.
2
Identify each argument
Identify the four distinct arguments against al-taghalub. The first is implicit: one god is stronger than the other. The second argues from the weaker god's incapability, the third from the stronger god's incapability due to similarity, and the fourth from the need for a preponderator.
Each argument targets a different aspect of the over- powering scenario.
3
Understand the logical chain
See how the fourth argument uses a conditional: if there is a preponderator, both gods become originated and infinite regress follows. If no preponderator, then the preponderance is arbitrary.
This connects to earlier lessons on preponderance (tarjih) and infinite regress (tasalsul).
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Why does Sanusi consider these arguments decisive? How do they protect the Islamic concept of Tawhid?
These arguments are part of a broader proof for the oneness of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحد الإلهين أقدرَ مِنَ الآخر
ثانيها : أنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ عجزُ مَنْ لم تنفذ إرادته مع كونه إلها ! والعجز على الإله محال لما سيأتي
ثالثها : أنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ عجزُ الإلهِ الذي نفذَتْ إرادتُهُ أيضاً ؛ لأنَّهُما مثلان ، فيجب لأحدهما ما وجبَ للآخرِ
رابعها : الترجيحُ لأحد المثلين على مثلِهِ بصفةٍ مِنْ غيرِ مُرجح فإِنْ فُرِضَ المرجّحُ لزمَ حدوثهما ، ونقلنا الكلامَ إلى الثالث ، ولزمَ التسلسل .
One of the two gods is more powerful than the other. Second: that it necessitates the incapability of the one whose will is not executed despite being a god! And incapability is impossible for a god as will come. Third: that it necessitates the incapability of the god whose will was executed as well; because they are similar, so what is necessary for one is necessary for the other. Fourth: the preponderance of one of the two similars over its like by an attribute without a preponderator; if a preponderator is assumed, their origination is necessitated, and we transfer the speech to the third, and infinite regress is necessitated.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage presents four arguments against the scenario of al-taghalub (one god overcoming the other) in the context of refuting the possibility of two gods. The first argument states that if two gods exist, one must be more powerful than the other, implying the other is weaker. The second argument follows: if one god's will is not executed, he is incapable, which is impossible for a god. The third argument notes that if one god's will is executed, the other god must also be incapable due to similarity (tamathul), since what applies to one applies to the other. The fourth argument addresses preponderance: if one god is preferred over the other by an attribute without a preponderator (murajjih), that is arbitrary; if a preponderator is assumed, then both gods are originated (hadith), and the argument transfers to a third, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). All four arguments demonstrate logical impossibilities arising from the assumption of multiple gods.

Key Terms
التغالب
العجز
المثلان
الترجيح
المرجح
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the scenario of one god overpowering another is a plausible alternative to monotheism.
Correction: Sanusi shows that this scenario leads to logical absurdities such as incapability of a god and infinite regress, making it rationally impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's fourth argument against al-taghabul, what happens if we assume a preponderator (murajjih)?
A) The two gods become similar
B) The two gods become originated (hadith) and lead to infinite regress
C) The two gods become one
D) The two gods become eternal

Key Takeaway
The four arguments demonstrate that the assumption of two gods inevitably leads to logical contradictions, thus reaffirming the necessity of Tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The fourth argument shows that any attempt to explain preference among multiple gods leads to an infinite chain. Where in your life are you comfortable stopping an infinite regress (e.g., in reasons for your faith)? Are you willing to accept that God is the ultimate cause with no further explanation? Why or why not?
?
Sanusi argues that a god who is unable to execute his will is a contradiction. Reflect: Are there areas in your life where you attribute weakness or inability to Allah? How can you realign your understanding of Allah's perfect power based on this argument?
?
If you truly internalize that only one God can exist because any other possibility leads to absurdity, how would that change your daily reliance on and trust in Allah? Write down one specific situation today where you will act on this truth.

Refuting the Agreement Scenario: Coercion and Impossibility
Sanusi refutes the scenario of complete agreement (al-ittifaq) showing it entails coercion and impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the scenario of two gods always agreeing necessarily leads to coercion and impossibility, undermining the possibility of multiple gods.

Core Idea
Necessary agreement between gods makes them coerced, not free creators.

In this passage, Sanusi dismantles the possibility that two gods could always agree. He shows that if their agreement is necessary, it strips them of free will and leads to logical absurdities. Let's examine his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of agreement
Sanusi first divides agreement into necessary (wajib) and possible (ja'iz). Focus on the necessary branch as it leads to contradictions.
The possible branch is not explored in detail; the argument targets necessary agreement.
2
Follow the consequence of coercion
If agreement is necessary, each god cannot oppose the other. This implies coercion (qahr) and negation of free will (ikhtiyar). Sanusi uses the example of one able to oppose and the other not.
Free will requires both action and leaving to be possible.
3
Grasp the conversion of possible to impossible
Sanusi argues that necessary agreement makes the possible (e.g., rest) impossible if one god wills the opposite (e.g., motion). This reverses the nature of possibles.
This contradicts the principle that gods can do all possibles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بطلانُ الطرف الثاني مِنَ التالي ـ وهو الاتفاق ـ فمِنْ أوجه : وذلكَ أنَّ الاتفاق : إمَّا أنْ يكونَ واجباً أو جائزاً ، ويلزم في الاتفاقِ الواجب أن يكونَ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما مقهوراً غير مختارِ إنْ كانَ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما لا يَقدِرُ على مخالفة الآخرِ ، وإنْ كانَ أَحدهما يَقدِرُ عليها دونَ الآخرِ .. لزمَ قهرُ الذي لا يَقدِرُ عليها ، ونفي كونه مختاراً ؛ لأنَّ المختار هو الذي يتأتّى منهُ الفعلُ والتركُ ، فإذا كانَ اتفاقهما معاً ، أو أحدهما واجباً .. لم يتأتَ مِنَ المجبورِ منهما ترْكُ ما اختارَهُ الآخرُ ، كيف وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَختارُ ﴾ [القصص : ٦٨] ؟ ! وأيضاً : يلزمُ مِنْ قهْرِ أحدهما قهْرُ الآخرِ ؛ لأنَّه مثله ، ويلزمُ الافتقار إلى المرجّح في تخصيص أحدِ المثلين بما لم يثبت لمثلِهِ ويلزم أيضاً في الاتفاقِ الواجب : انقلاب الممكن مستحيلاً ؛ لأنَّ كل واحدٍ منهما إذا نظرنا إليهِ منفرداً أمكن أن يُوجِدَ كلاً مِنَ الحركة والسكون مثلاً ؛ لأنَّه إنه لا جزء إلله ، فإذا فرضنا تعلُّق إرادة أحدهما بخصوص الحركة مثلاً .. صار وقوع السكون الممكنِ مِنَ الآخرِ مستحيلاً ، وذلك قلب للحقائق وأيضاً : كونُ المانع لهُ تعلَّق إرادة الآخرِ بضده .. يلزم منه إيجاب المانع حكم المنع لما لم يقم به ، وذلك كله مستحيل
As for the invalidity of the second alternative of the consequent—which is agreement—it is from several aspects: that agreement is either necessary or possible. In necessary agreement, it follows that each of them is coerced, not free, if each of them is unable to oppose the other. If one of them is able to oppose and the other not, then coercion of the one unable follows, and negation of his being free; because the free agent is one from whom both action and leaving are possible. So if their agreement together, or one of them, is necessary, then the coerced one cannot leave what the other has chosen. How, while 'your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' (Qur'an 28:68)? Also, from the coercion of one follows the coercion of the other because he is his like, and there follows the need for a preponderator in specifying one of the similars with what is not established for its like. Also in necessary agreement: the conversion of the possible into the impossible, because if we consider each one individually, it is possible for him to bring about both motion and rest, for example, because there is no component of divinity [؟]. So if we assume the attachment of one's will to a specific motion, for example, then the occurrence of the possible rest from the other becomes impossible, and that is a reversal of realities. Also: that the preventer [man has] the attachment of the other's will to its opposite necessitates that the preventer effects the ruling of prevention for what does not subsist in it, and all that is impossible.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage refutes the scenario of al-ittifaq (complete agreement between two gods) by showing its consequences. Sanusi first divides agreement into two types: necessary (wajib) or possible (ja'iz). He then examines necessary agreement: if both gods cannot oppose each other, each is coerced (maqhur) and not free (mukhtar); if one can oppose and the other cannot, the latter is coerced, negating free will. True free will requires both action and leaving to be possible. He quotes the Qur'anic verse 'Your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' to emphasize divine freedom. Additionally, coercing one god implies coercing the other due to similarity (tamathul), requiring a preponderator (murajjih) to differentiate them. Another consequence: necessary agreement turns the possible (mumkin) into the impossible (mustahil). For example, if each god individually can create motion or rest, but one wills motion, then rest from the other becomes impossible, flipping realities. Finally, the preventer (al-mani') being attached to the other's will necessitates that the preventer effects prevention on what it does not inherently contain, all of which is impossible.

Key Terms
الاتفاق
واجب
جائز
مقهور
مختار
المانع
انقلاب الممكن مستحيلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That two gods could always agree voluntarily without any coercion.
Correction: Sanusi shows that if their agreement is necessary, it inherently strips them of free will and leads to logical absurdities, proving the scenario invalid.

Checkpoint

B) The one unable to oppose is coerced and not free

Key Takeaway
Necessary agreement between gods leads to coercion, loss of free will, and logical impossibilities, invalidating the scenario of multiple gods always agreeing.

?

Agreement Negates Necessity of Existence
Sanusi argues that perpetual agreement negates the self-necessity of existence for each god.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that complete agreement between two supposed gods destroys the necessity of existence for each, because contingent events would depend on neither individually.

Core Idea
Two gods in perfect agreement cannot both be necessary—each would be dispensable.

This passage continues Sanusi's refutation of the agreement scenario. In the previous part, he showed that agreement undermines independence; here he shows it also undermines necessity of existence. Sanusi argues that necessity of existence is tied to being the sole source of contingency. If two gods perpetually agree, contingency can turn to either, so neither is inherently necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the compact style—Sanusi packs a lot into a few lines.
2
Understand why necessity of existence is tied to dependence of contingents
Sanusi states that necessity of existence is established because contingent events depend on God. Identify this key premise.
This is a foundational axiom in kalam.
3
Analyze the hypothetical scenario
Assume two gods in perpetual agreement with no distinction. Consider how this affects the dependence relationship.
The phrase 'لا ينفرد أحدهما عن الآخر بشيء' means no attribute distinguishes them.
4
See the conclusion drawn
Sanusi concludes that necessity of existence is not realized for either. Read his reasoning: if one is absent, the other suffices.
This directly refutes the idea of multiple necessary beings.
5
Reflect on the Creed statement
Sanusi ends by quoting his own creed: 'for each one to be independent of each one of them.' Understand how this captures the argument.
The word 'الاستغناء' implies that each god could stand alone, negating mutual need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلزمُ أيضاً في الاتفاقِ : عدم وجوب الوجودِ لكل واحد منهما ؛ لأنَّ وجوبَ الوجودِ إنَّما ثبت للإلهِ مِنْ حيثُ توقف وجودِ الحوادثِ عليه ؛ لئلا يلزم التسلسلُ أو الدور عند تقدير جواز وجودِهِ فإذا قُدْرَ أنَّ ثَمَّ إلهين لا ينفرد أحدهما عن الآخر بشيء ، بل هما مُتَّفقانِ أبداً .. لزمَ عدم توقف الحوادثِ على خصوص كلُّ واحدٍ منهما ، فلا يتحقَّقُ وجوبُ الوجودِ لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما ؛ إذ على تقدير عدمِهِ تستغني الحوادث عنه بصاحبه ، والإلهُ مُتحقَّق وجوبُ وجودِهِ ، وهذا معنى قولي في العقيدة : ( للاستغناء بكلّ منهما عن كل منهما ) أي : للاستغناء بكلِّ منهما على الخصوص .
Also in the scenario of agreement: there follows the non-necessity of existence for each one of them; because the necessity of existence is only established for the god by virtue of the dependence of contingent events on him, to avoid infinite regress or circularity when his existence is deemed possible. So if it is supposed that there are two gods, each not distinct from the other in anything, but they are always in agreement, then the non-dependence of contingent events on each particular one follows, so the necessity of existence is not realized for each one of them; for if one is assumed nonexistent, the contingent events are independent of him through his companion, while the god is realized as having necessary existence. This is the meaning of my statement in the Creed: 'for each one of them to be independent of each one of them,' i.e., for each one to be independent specifically.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage presents a further argument against the agreement scenario by examining the necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud). Sanusi explains that a god's necessity of existence is established because contingent events depend on him—otherwise infinite regress or circularity would result. If two gods always agree without any distinction, then contingent events do not depend on any particular god, because if one were absent, the other would suffice. Thus, neither god is necessary in himself; each could be dispensed with. The necessity of existence is not realized for either because contingency of events could be fulfilled by the other. Sanusi references his Creed statement: 'lil-istighna' bi kullin minhuma 'an kullin minhuma' meaning independence of each specifically, highlighting that the alleged multiplicity undermines divine necessity.

الاستغناء

Correction: Sanusi shows that necessity requires exclusive dependence of contingent events on each being. If two gods agree perfectly, contingents depend on neither alone, so neither is necessary.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The necessity of existence is incompatible with multiplicity—a necessary being must be the singular source of all contingency.

Objections and Answers on Two Gods in Agreement
Sanusi responds to two objections defending the possibility of two gods in agreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the impossibility of two gods even if they agree, by showing that any scenario leads to each being merely a part of a god.

Core Idea
Two agreeing gods cannot both be fully divine—each would be merely a part.

Sanusi now considers objections that try to salvage the idea of two gods acting together in complete agreement. These objections may sound plausible, but Sanusi shows they ultimately undermine the very divinity of the supposed gods. We will follow his logical responses step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation carefully.
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is structured as two objection-response pairs.
2
Identify the first objection and Sanusi's response.
Find the phrase 'فإن قلت' (If you say) and the corresponding 'قلت' (I say). The first objection suggests necessity could be realized for one god nonspecifically. Sanusi's response uses the principle of similarity (tamathul) to show they cannot differ in necessity or possibility.
Key term: لا بعينه (not specifically).
3
Identify the second objection and Sanusi's response.
The second objection denies that an act can be independent of one god through the other; both are needed. Sanusi's response concludes that each would be only a part (juz') of a god, not a full god (ilah).
Key term: جزء إلله (part of a god).
4
Reflect on the logical force of the arguments.
Consider why Sanusi's responses are effective: the first uses similarity to force individuation; the second uses self-sufficiency to show joint necessity reduces each to a part.
A true god must be self-sufficient (ghani) and not dependent on another.
5
Summarize the conclusion in your own words.
Write a short paragraph explaining why Sanusi's refutation works even though the passage cuts off. The conclusion is clear: the objections fail to preserve true divinity for both gods.
The final sentence is incomplete, but the argument's direction is evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : يكون وجوب الوجودِ متحققاً لأحدهما لا بعينه . قلت : فيثبتُ جواز الوجودِ لأحدهما لا بعينه ، وتماثلُهما يمنعُ مِن اختلافهما في الوجوب أو الجواز .
فإن قلت : نمنع أنَّ الفعلَ يستغني بأحدهما عن الآخر ، بل لا يُوجَدُ إلا بهما ؛ فوجودُهما معاً واجبٌ .
قلتُ : فيلزم أن يكون كل واحد منهما جزء إلله ، لا إلهاً ، فيقوم
If you say: the necessity of existence is realized for one of them, not specifically. I say: then the possibility of existence is established for one of them, not specifically, and their similarity prevents their differing in necessity or possibility. If you say: I deny that the act is independent of one of them through the other; rather it only exists through both; so their existence together is necessary. I say: then it follows that each one of them is a part of a god, not a god, so it subsists...
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage contains two objection-and-answer exchanges. In the first objection, the opponent suggests that necessity of existence could be realized for one of the two gods, though not specifically identified (bi la 'ayn). Sanusi responds that this would also establish possibility of existence for one of them (again unspecified), and since the two are similar (tamathul), they cannot differ in attributes of necessity or possibility—so whichever is necessary must be necessary individually, not merely unspecified. The second objection denies that an act can be independent of one god through the other; instead, an act requires both gods together, making both necessarily existent. Sanusi replies that if both are required for any act, then each is only a part (juz') of a god, not a full god (ilah), because a true god must be self-sufficient. The argument cuts off at 'fa yaqum' (so it subsists...), but the conclusion is clear: the objection fails.

Key Terms
لا بعينه
تماثل
جزء إلله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could both be divine if they always agree and both are necessary for every act.
Correction: Sanusi shows that if both are necessary for any act, each is only a part of a god, not a full god, because a true god must be self-sufficient.

Checkpoint
What is Sanusi's response to the objection that both gods together are needed for an act, so their existence is jointly necessary?
A) That would make them equal in power
B) That would make each a part of a god, not a full god
C) That would make them originated
D) That would make them both free

Key Takeaway
Two gods in agreement cannot both be fully divine—each would be only a part of a god, not a true god.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi argues that if two gods were needed together for any act, each would be only a part of a god. Agreement Negates Divine Necessity
Main argument and first objection-response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that necessary agreement between two gods would negate their necessity and that the objection of non-specific necessity fails due to their similarity.

Core Idea
Two gods who always agree cannot both be necessary, and their similarity rules out differing in necessity.

Sanusi begins by establishing a key proof: the necessity of a deity is grounded in contingent events depending on Him. If two gods always agree, events do not depend on either specifically, so neither is necessary. He then tackles an objection that perhaps one is necessary but not specified, showing their similarity prevents such a difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi builds the argument step by step.
Pay attention to the phrase 'for the dispensation by each of them from each of them.'
2
Analyze the core argument
Identify the claim that necessity of existence is based on dependence of contingent events. See how agreement removes specific dependence, so neither is necessary.
Think about why infinite regress or circularity is avoided by this dependence.
3
Examine the first objection and response
Consider the objection that one god might be necessary non-specifically. Then see how Sanusi uses similarity (tamathul) to argue that both must have the same modal status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلزمُ أيضاً في الاتفاقِ : عدم وجوب الوجودِ لكل واحد منهما ؛ لأنَّ وجوبَ الوجودِ إنَّما ثبت للإلهِ مِنْ حيثُ توقف وجودِ الحوادثِ عليه ؛ لئلا يلزم التسلسلُ أو الدور عند تقدير جواز وجودِهِ . فإذا قُدْرَ أنَّ ثَمَّ إلهين لا ينفرد أحدهما عن الآخر بشيء ، بل هما مُتَّفقانِ أبداً .. لزمَ عدم توقف الحوادثِ على خصوص كلُّ واحدٍ منهما ، فلا يتحقَّقُ وجوبُ الوجودِ لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما ؛ إذ على تقدير عدمِهِ تستغني الحوادث عنه بصاحبه ، والإلهُ مُتحقَّق وجوبُ وجودِهِ ، وهذا معنى قولي في العقيدة : ( للاستغناء بكلّ منهما عن كل منهما ) أي : للاستغناء بكلِّ منهما على الخصوص .
فإن قلت : يكون وجوب الوجودِ متحققاً لأحدهما لا بعينه . قلت : فيثبتُ جواز الوجودِ لأحدهما لا بعينه ، وتماثلُهما يمنعُ مِن اختلافهما في الوجوب أو الجواز .
فإن قلت : نمنع أنَّ الفعلَ يستغني بأحدهما عن الآخر ، بل لا يُوجَدُ إلا بهما ؛ فوجودُهما معاً واجبٌ .
قلتُ : فيلزم أن يكون كل واحد منهما جزء إلله ، لا إلهاً ، فيقوم بكل واحدٍ منهما جزء العلمِ وجزء القدرةِ وجزء الإرادة إلى غير ذلكَ ممَّا لا يقولُ به عاقل ، وإذا كان تركيب الإله مِن جزأين مُتصلين محالاً . . فما بالك بتركيبه من جزأين منفصلين ؟!
ويلزم أيضاً مِنْ وجوب استغناء الحوادث بكل منهما عن الآخر : أن تكون الحوادث محتاجةً لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما ، غنيةً عن كل واحدٍ منهما ! وهو جمع بين متنافيين ، وهذا اللازم أقوى مِنَ الذي قبلَهُ ؛ لأنَّ السابق قد يُدَّعَى فِيهِ أَنَّهُ مِنْ بابِ التمسُّكِ بعكس الدليل (١) ، وإن كنَّا نحنُ قد قرَّرناه على وجهِ لا يَرِدُ عليهِ (٢) ، بخلاف هذا
And also necessarily follows in the case of agreement: the non-necessity of existence for each of them; because the necessity of existence is only established for the deity by virtue of the dependence of the existence of contingent events upon Him, to avoid infinite regress or circularity when assuming the possibility of His existence. So if it is assumed that there are two gods, neither being distinct from the other in anything, rather they are forever in agreement, then it necessarily follows that contingent events do not depend specifically on each of them; thus the necessity of existence is not realized for each of them, since on the assumption of its absence, contingent events would dispense with him by means of his companion, while the deity is realized as having necessary existence. This is the meaning of my statement in the Creed: 'for the dispensation by each of them from each of them'—i.e., the dispensation by each specifically.
If you say: The necessity of existence is realized for one of them but not specifically. I say: Then the possibility of existence would be established for one of them not specifically, and their similarity prevents them from differing in necessity or possibility.
If you say: I deny that the action dispenses with one of them by the other; rather it only exists through both; so their existence together is necessary. I say: Then it necessarily follows that each of them is a part of a god, not a god; for each would possess part of knowledge, part of power, part of will, and so on—things no rational person would affirm. And if the composition of the deity from two connected parts is impossible, what about His composition from two separate parts?!
And it also necessarily follows from the necessity of contingent events dispensing with each of them by the other: that contingent events are in need of each of them, yet independent of each of them! That is a combination of two contradictories. This necessary consequence is stronger than the previous one; because the previous could be claimed to be from the type of appealing to the converse of the proof, although we have established it in a way that is not subject to that objection, unlike this one.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage presents an argument showing that necessary agreement (ittifaq wajib) between two gods is impossible. The proof: The necessity of existence (wujub al-wujud) for a deity is established because contingent events (hawadith) depend on Him to avoid infinite regress or circularity. If two gods always agree, then contingent events do not depend specifically on either one, because each can substitute for the other. Therefore, neither would be necessarily existent, which contradicts the definition of deity. Sanusi clarifies this with his statement in the Creed about 'dispensation' (istighna').

He then addresses two objections:
1. Objection: Perhaps one of them is necessarily existent, but not specified which. Answer: That would mean the other is contingent (possible), but since they are identical in essence, they cannot differ in necessity and contingency; thus both must be the same, negating the twoness.
2. Objection: Perhaps neither dispenses with the other; both are necessary together. Answer: Then each would be a part of God (juz' ilah), not a god, sharing partial attributes like knowledge, power, will—which is absurd. Moreover, composition implies parts, and composition in the deity is impossible (especially from separate parts).

Finally, Sanusi notes an additional necessary consequence: if contingent events dispense with each god by the other, then those events are simultaneously in need of each god and independent of each god—a contradiction. This point is stronger because it does not rely on the converse of the proof (which might be objectionable).

Key Terms
وجوب الوجود
اتفاق
استغناء
تماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think two gods could simply agree in all actions, preserving their divinity.
Correction: Sanusi shows that necessary agreement removes specific dependence of contingent events, so neither would be necessarily existent, contradicting the definition of deity.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does necessary agreement between two gods negate their necessity?
A) Because they would both be eternal
B) Because contingent events do not depend specifically on either
C) Because they would have the same attributes
D) Because infinite regress would occur

Key Takeaway
Necessary agreement between two gods would require each to depend on the other for contingent events, negating their individual necessity and contradicting divine unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even in a fleeting thought, considered that there could be more than one ultimate source of existence? How does this argument expose the logical impossibility of such a notion, and how should it deepen your reliance on Allah alone?
?
The argument shows that necessity cannot be shared or duplicated. In what areas of your life do you still attribute a kind of independence or necessity to created things, as if they could suffice without Allah? What steps can you take to uproot that subtle shirk from your heart?
?
Sanusi uses tamathul to argue that identical beings cannot differ in modal status. Reflect: Do you sometimes treat two things as equally ultimate or equally worthy of trust? How can you ensure that only Allah holds the status of absolute necessity in your life?

Each God Becomes a Part, Not a God
Second objection-response and final stronger contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that claiming both gods are necessary together leads to each being a part of a god, and that the final point about simultaneous need and independence is a stronger contradiction.

Core Idea
If two gods together are necessary, each becomes a part, not a god, and the resulting contradiction is inescapable.

Sanusi addresses a second objection: perhaps the action requires both gods, so both are necessary. He shows this implies each is only part of a god, with partial attributes, which is absurd. He then presents an even stronger necessary consequence: contingent events would need each god yet be independent of each, a direct contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second objection and Sanusi's response, including the final contradiction.
Notice the rhetorical question about composition from separate parts.
2
Analyze the second objection and its refutation
Consider the claim that action requires both gods. Understand why Sanusi says each would be a part (juz') of a god with partial attributes.
Think about why composition, especially from separate parts, is impossible for God.
3
Examine the final necessary consequence
Identify the contradiction that contingent events would be both in need of each god and independent of each. Appreciate why Sanusi calls it stronger.
Contrast with the previous argument which might rely on the converse of the proof.

5 min
The Conditional Argument for Incapacity in Two Gods
Sanusi restates the argument from agreement/disagreement leading to incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the possibility of agreement between two gods leads to the possibility of disagreement and thus necessitates incapacity, forming the core of the tamānuʿ argument.

Core Idea
If two gods can agree, they can disagree, and disagreement forces incapacity.

Sanusi presents a restatement of the classic argument from mutual hindrance. He begins with a conditional chain: if agreement between two gods is possible, then disagreement is possible, and disagreement necessarily entails incapacity. This logical progression aims to show that even the hypothesis of two gods leads to an impossible consequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the logical structure of 'if...then...' statements.
2
Identify the conditional chain
Find how agreement leads to disagreement, and disagreement to incapacity. Identify the terms 'ملزوم' and 'لازم'.
3
Understand the formulation's purpose
The author says this formulation is 'أنسب للفظ العقيدة' – ask why it is more appropriate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعبارة أخرى : أن نقول : كلَّما جازَ اتفاقهما جازَ اختلافهما ، وكلما جازَ اختلافُهما لزمَ قبولُهما العجز ؛ لأن الاختلاف ملزوم للعجز ، فالقابل للاختلافِ قابل للعجزِ ؛ ضرورةَ أنَّ القابلَ لملزوم الشيء قابل للازمِهِ ، فينتج إذاً : كلَّما جازَ اتفاقهما لزمَ قَبولهما العجز ، وهذا التقرير أنسب للفظ العقيدة ويلزمُ أيضاً في الاتفاقِ مطلقاً العجزُ ؛ لأنَّ الفعل الواحد يستحيل عليه الانقسام ، فيتمانعان فيه ، فيلزم عجزهما أو عجز أحدهما كما في الاختلافِ ، والعجز على الإله محال ؛ لأنَّهُ يُضادُّ القدرة ؛ فإنْ كانَ قديماً لزم استحالة عدمِهِ ، فيجب ألا يقدر هذا الإله على شيءٍ دائماً ، وإنْ كانَ حادثاً فضدُّه - وهو القدرة - قديمة فيستحيل عدمها فلا يُوجَدُ العجزُ ، وأيضاً فيستحيلُ اتصافُ الإله بصفة حادثة يعني : أنَّه يلزمُ في الاتفاق مطلقاً ـ أي : سواء قُدِّرَ واجباً أو جائزاً - مِنَ التمانع الموجب للعجزِ ما لزمَ في الاختلافِ ؛ وذلك لأنَّهما قد تتوجَّهُ إرادتهما إلى ما لا يقبلُ الانقسامَ مِنْ عَرَضِ أو جوهر فردِ ، فلا يمكنُ حينَئِذٍ أَنْ تَنفُذَ فيه إلا إرادة واحدة وقدرةٌ واحدةٌ ، ويجب عدم النفوذ للإرادة الأخرى والقدرة الأخرى ، وإذا كان كذلكَ فمَنْ لم تنفُذُ فِيهِ إرادتُهُ ولا قدرتُهُ لزمَ عجزُهُ، فإنْ فُرِضَ أَنَّهُ لم تَنفُذُ فِيهِ الإرادتانِ لزمَ عجز الإلهين
And in other words: we say: whenever their agreement is possible, their disagreement is possible, and whenever their disagreement is possible, they are necessarily subject to incapacity; because disagreement necessitates incapacity, so that which is susceptible to disagreement is susceptible to incapacity; of necessity, that which is susceptible to the necessary concomitant of a thing is susceptible to its necessary consequent. So it follows therefore: whenever their agreement is possible, they are necessarily subject to incapacity. And this formulation is more appropriate to the wording of al-ʿAqīda. And also in absolute agreement, incapacity necessarily follows; because a single action is impossible to divide, so they mutually hinder each other in it, so the incapacity of both or one of them follows just as in the case of disagreement. And incapacity with respect to God is impossible; because it contradicts power (qudra). For if it were eternal, its non-existence would be impossible, so this god would never be able to do anything at all. And if it were originated, its opposite—which is power—is eternal, so its non-existence is impossible, so incapacity does not exist. Also, it is impossible for God to be described with an originated attribute. Meaning: it necessarily follows in absolute agreement—that is, whether the action is considered necessary or contingent—from the mutual hindrance necessitating incapacity what necessarily followed in disagreement. And that is because their wills may be directed towards that which does not admit of division, whether an accident or an indivisible atom; then at that time only one will and one power can be effective in it, and non-effectiveness is necessary for the other will and the other power. And if that is the case, then the one whose will and power are not effective necessarily has incapacity. And if it is supposed that both wills are not effective, then the incapacity of both gods necessarily follows.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage presents a restatement of the proof from mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) for the impossibility of two gods. The author first states a conditional chain: if agreement between two gods is possible, then disagreement is possible; disagreement necessitates incapacity; therefore, agreement also necessitates incapacity. This formulation is said to be more faithful to the wording of the text al-ʿAqīda. Then the author explains that even in absolute agreement (whether the act is necessary or contingent), incapacity follows because a single indivisible act cannot be performed by two agents; they hinder each other, resulting in the incapacity of at least one. Incapacity in God is impossible because it contradicts power: if eternal, God would never be able to act; if originated, power would be prior and incapacity could not arise. Also, an originated attribute is impossible for God. The author then elaborates that the same mutual hindrance occurs in agreement as in disagreement: when both wills target an indivisible entity (accident or atom), only one will and power can succeed; the other must fail, leading to its incapacity. If both fail, both gods are incapable.

Key Terms
اتفاق
اختلاف
عجز
لزوم
ملزوم
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two gods agree, there is no conflict, so two gods are possible.
Correction: The argument shows that the possibility of agreement itself implies the possibility of disagreement, and disagreement leads to incapacity, so two gods are impossible.

Checkpoint
What does the possibility of agreement between two gods imply according to the argument?
A) They are harmonious.
B) Disagreement is impossible.
C) Disagreement is possible.
D) They are both powerful.

Key Takeaway
The tamānuʿ argument shows that even the possibility of agreement between two gods entails the possibility of disagreement and thus incapacity, making two gods impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life have you tried to 'serve two masters,' believing you could maintain peace between them? How does this proof expose the inner conflict and spiritual incapacity that results from such divided loyalty?
?
When you truly grasp that even the possibility of a second deity leads to necessary incapacity, does your heart feel the weight of Allah's sole supremacy? Does this knowledge dissolve any hidden reliance on created things?
?
The argument hinges on the logical necessity that if something is possible, its opposite is also possible. Are there beliefs or actions in your life where you have assumed something is 'always true' without considering the implications of its opposite? How can this reasoning sharpen your commitment to tawḥīd?

6 min
The Argument from Mutual Hindrance in Indivisible Acts
Sanusi explains that even in agreement, mutual hindrance over indivisible acts necessitates incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even if two gods are in complete agreement, their simultaneous will over an indivisible action leads to mutual hindrance and thus incapacity, which is impossible for God.

Core Idea
Even perfect agreement cannot avoid conflict over an indivisible act.

Sanusi continues the argument by extending it to absolute agreement, whether the act is necessary or contingent. He argues that if two gods direct their wills toward an indivisible entity (like an atom or accident), only one will can succeed, leading to the incapacity of at least one god. This reinforces the impossibility of multiple gods.

Guided Path
1
Understand mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ)
The two gods hinder each other because the act cannot be split. Recognize that this is the core mechanism.
2
Analyze the case of an indivisible act
The text discusses actions that do not admit division, such as accidents or atoms. Only one will can be effective.
3
Consider divine attributes
Incapacity contradicts power; if eternal, God would never act; if originated, it contradicts eternal power. Also, God cannot have originated attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعبارة أخرى : أن نقول : كلَّما جازَ اتفاقهما جازَ اختلافهما ، وكلما جازَ اختلافُهما لزمَ قبولُهما العجز ؛ لأن الاختلاف ملزوم للعجز ، فالقابل للاختلافِ قابل للعجزِ ؛ ضرورةَ أنَّ القابلَ لملزوم الشيء قابل للازمِهِ ، فينتج إذاً : كلَّما جازَ اتفاقهما لزمَ قَبولهما العجز ، وهذا التقرير أنسب للفظ العقيدة ويلزمُ أيضاً في الاتفاقِ مطلقاً العجزُ ؛ لأنَّ الفعل الواحد يستحيل عليه الانقسام ، فيتمانعان فيه ، فيلزم عجزهما أو عجز أحدهما كما في الاختلافِ ، والعجز على الإله محال ؛ لأنَّهُ يُضادُّ القدرة ؛ فإنْ كانَ قديماً لزم استحالة عدمِهِ ، فيجب ألا يقدر هذا الإله على شيءٍ دائماً ، وإنْ كانَ حادثاً فضدُّه - وهو القدرة - قديمة فيستحيل عدمها فلا يُوجَدُ العجزُ ، وأيضاً فيستحيلُ اتصافُ الإله بصفة حادثة يعني : أنَّه يلزمُ في الاتفاق مطلقاً ـ أي : سواء قُدِّرَ واجباً أو جائزاً - مِنَ التمانع الموجب للعجزِ ما لزمَ في الاختلافِ ؛ وذلك لأنَّهما قد تتوجَّهُ إرادتهما إلى ما لا يقبلُ الانقسامَ مِنْ عَرَضِ أو جوهر فردِ ، فلا يمكنُ حينَئِذٍ أَنْ تَنفُذَ فيه إلا إرادة واحدة وقدرةٌ واحدةٌ ، ويجب عدم النفوذ للإرادة الأخرى والقدرة الأخرى ، وإذا كان كذلكَ فمَنْ لم تنفُذُ فِيهِ إرادتُهُ ولا قدرتُهُ لزمَ عجزُهُ، فإنْ فُرِضَ أَنَّهُ لم تَنفُذُ فِيهِ الإرادتانِ لزمَ عجز الإلهين
And in other words: we say: whenever their agreement is possible, their disagreement is possible, and whenever their disagreement is possible, they are necessarily subject to incapacity; because disagreement necessitates incapacity, so that which is susceptible to disagreement is susceptible to incapacity; of necessity, that which is susceptible to the necessary concomitant of a thing is susceptible to its necessary consequent. So it follows therefore: whenever their agreement is possible, they are necessarily subject to incapacity. And this formulation is more appropriate to the wording of al-ʿAqīda. And also in absolute agreement, incapacity necessarily follows; because a single action is impossible to divide, so they mutually hinder each other in it, so the incapacity of both or one of them follows just as in the case of disagreement. And incapacity with respect to God is impossible; because it contradicts power (qudra). For if it were eternal, its non-existence would be impossible, so this god would never be able to do anything at all. And if it were originated, its opposite—which is power—is eternal, so its non-existence is impossible, so incapacity does not exist. Also, it is impossible for God to be described with an originated attribute. Meaning: it necessarily follows in absolute agreement—that is, whether the action is considered necessary or contingent—from the mutual hindrance necessitating incapacity what necessarily followed in disagreement. And that is because their wills may be directed towards that which does not admit of division, whether an accident or an indivisible atom; then at that time only one will and one power can be effective in it, and non-effectiveness is necessary for the other will and the other power. And if that is the case, then the one whose will and power are not effective necessarily has incapacity. And if it is supposed that both wills are not effective, then the incapacity of both gods necessarily follows.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage presents a restatement of the proof from mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) for the impossibility of two gods. The author first states a conditional chain: if agreement between two gods is possible, then disagreement is possible; disagreement necessitates incapacity; therefore, agreement also necessitates incapacity. This formulation is said to be more faithful to the wording of the text al-ʿAqīda. Then the author explains that even in absolute agreement (whether the act is necessary or contingent), incapacity follows because a single indivisible act cannot be performed by two agents; they hinder each other, resulting in the incapacity of at least one. Incapacity in God is impossible because it contradicts power: if eternal, God would never be able to act; if originated, power would be prior and incapacity could not arise. Also, an originated attribute is impossible for God. The author then elaborates that the same mutual hindrance occurs in agreement as in disagreement: when both wills target an indivisible entity (accident or atom), only one will and power can succeed; the other must fail, leading to its incapacity. If both fail, both gods are incapable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two gods always agree, they can coexist harmoniously without conflict.
Correction: The argument shows that even if they agree, they cannot both simultaneously will an indivisible act without one becoming incapable.

Checkpoint
What happens when two gods will an indivisible act according to Sanusi?
A) Both wills succeed simultaneously.
B) Only one will succeeds.
C) The act is split between them.
D) They cooperate to perform the act.

Key Takeaway
The tamānuʿ argument proves that even perfect agreement between two gods leads to mutual hindrance in indivisible acts, necessitating incapacity, which is impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have ever assumed that harmony among multiple authorities is always possible, what does this argument about indivisible acts expose about that assumption? Where in your life have you trusted in multiple ultimate sources of control, and how does that lead to inner incapacity?
?
The argument shows that true power cannot be shared. When you attribute power to anything alongside Allah—even your own ability or the help of others—in what area of your life are you unconsciously affirming the existence of 'two gods'? What specific step will you take today to redirect that dependency solely to Allah?
?
Incapacity is impossible for Allah. Yet how often do you live as if Allah is incapable of handling a particular situation, and you must take control? How does the certainty that Allah's power is absolute and indivisible challenge your fear and anxiety right now?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
8 min
Why Multiple Gods Cannot Divide the World
The author rejects dividing the world among multiple gods, proving impossibility through arguments from limitlessness and interdependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why dividing the world among multiple gods is impossible, based on arguments from the limitlessness of God's power and the interdependence of substances and accidents.

Core Idea
Multiple gods cannot divide creation without conflict.

This passage addresses a common objection to the argument from mutual impediment (tamānuʿ). The objector suggests that two gods could avoid conflict by dividing the world between them. Sanusi responds with two powerful arguments showing why such a division is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objector's proposal to split the world into two domains. Notice how it seems to avoid conflict.
2
Examine the first response
Sanusi invokes the previously established principle that God's objects of power are limitless. Ask yourself why any division would impose finitude.
This is a key foundational idea.
3
Examine the second response
This argument covers two scenarios: both parts substances, or one substances and one accidents. Notice how similarity and concomitance prevent a true division.
4
Understand the overall conclusion
The objection fails to undermine the original implication. Reflect on how these arguments strengthen the case for God's oneness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : فلِمَ لا يجوز أن ينقسم العالم بينهما قسمين ، فيكون أحدهما قادراً على أحد القسمين ، والآخرُ على الآخر ، فلا يلزم التمانع ؟
فالجوابُ : أَنَّهُ تقرَّرَ قبلُ استحالة التناهي في مقدوراتِ الإله ومراداته ، فيستحيلُ هذا الفَرْضُ الذي ذُكِرَ في السؤال .
وأيضاً : فالقسمان إن كانا معاً في الجواهر لزمَ مِنْ تعلَّقِ القدرةِ ببعضها تعلَّقها بالجميع ؛ للتماثل ، فيلزم التمانع وإنْ كانَ أحد القسمين الجواهر والآخرُ الأعراض .. فذلكَ لا يُعقَلُ ؛ إذ القدرة على إيجاد الجواهر لا تُعقَلُ بدونِ القدرة على أعراضها ، وكذا العكسُ ؛ للتلازم الذي بينهما ، ثم ذلكَ لا يدفعُ التمانع عندما يريد أحدهما أنْ يُوجد الجوهر ، والآخر لا يريدُ أنْ يُوجد عرضَهُ
هذا السؤالُ إيراد على الملازمة التي ذكرنا أولاً ؛ وهي قولنا في العقيدةِ : ( إذ لو كان معَهُ ثانٍ لَلزِمَ عجزهما ... ( إلى آخره . ووجه الإيرادِ أنْ يُقالَ : لا تُسلَّمُ أَنَّهُ يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِ اللهِ ثَانِ عجزهما
If you say: Why is it not permissible for the world to be divided between them into two parts, so that one of them is powerful over one part and the other over the other part, so that mutual impediment (tamānuʿ) does not follow? The answer: It has been established previously the impossibility of finitude in the objects of power (maqdūrāt) and will (irādāt) of God, so this supposed scenario mentioned in the question is impossible. Also: If the two parts are together in the category of substances (jawāhir), then from the relation of power to some of them, its relation to all of them necessarily follows, due to similarity (tamāthul), so mutual impediment follows. And if one of the two parts is substances and the other is accidents (aʿrāḍ), that is unintelligible; for power to bring about substances cannot be conceived without power over their accidents, and likewise the reverse, due to the necessary concomitance (talāzum) between them. Moreover, that does not avert mutual impediment when one of them wants to bring about a substance while the other does not want to bring about its accident. This question is an objection to the implication (mulāzama) we mentioned earlier, namely our statement in the Creed: 'If there were with Him a second, their incapacity would follow... etc.' The point of the objection is to say: It is not conceded that from the existence of a second to God their incapacity follows.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair that addresses the possibility of multiple gods dividing the world between them. The objector asks: why not divide the world into two parts, each god controlling one, thereby avoiding conflict (tamānuʿ)? The author gives two responses. First, it has already been established that the objects of God's power and will are limitless (istihālat al-tanāhī), so any proposed division would impose finitude on God's power, which is impossible. Second, the author considers two scenarios: if both parts are substances (jawāhir), then because substances are similar (tamāthul), power over some implies power over all, leading to conflict. If one part is substances and the other is accidents (aʿrāḍ), then this is unintelligible because power over substances necessarily entails power over their accidents due to the necessary concomitance (talāzum) between them; thus no true division is possible. Even if such a division were granted, conflict would still occur when one god wants to create a substance and the other refuses to create its accident. The passage concludes by noting that this question is an objection to the earlier claim that the existence of a second god leads to their mutual incapacity. The objector tries to deny that implication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that dividing domains avoids conflict, so multiple gods could coexist peacefully.
Correction: The passage shows that division is impossible due to the limitlessness of divine power and the interdependence of substances and accidents; thus conflict is inevitable.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can the world not be divided between two gods into substances and accidents?
A) Because substances and accidents are dissimilar, so each god could control one type.
B) Because power over substances cannot be conceived without power over their accidents.
C) Because accidents do not need a creator.
D) Because substances are more important than accidents.

Key Takeaway
Division of creation among multiple gods is impossible because God's power is limitless and creation's parts are interdependent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if some area of your life is outside Allah's control, as if He is limited in power over that part of your world? How does this passage's insistence on the limitlessness of His power challenge that hidden doubt?
?
When you rely on secondary causes (like your job, health, or others), do you ever forget that their power is completely dependent on Allah? How can this argument about necessary concomitance remind you to put your trust solely in the Creator?
?
If you truly believed that no division of power is possible, would you still fear anything other than Allah? What small fear in your heart today reveals a failure to fully internalize this truth, and what step will you take to uproot it?
